Then pour a small quantity of hot water into the tube: hold your hand and turn it with pressure from your hand for a short time, so that the edge of the tube is pressed against the root of the nail, and the hot water finds its way to the depth, to the root of the nail. And let the patient wait for about an hour after the application of the hot water.
Then release it, for the claw is removed with its roots.
Thus do with these one after another until you come upon all who are in the body from among them.
Then care for them after they are removed, with what produces flesh from ointment.
On the method of cauterization in trembling.
When someone has trembling from cold in a nerve, or from quartan fever, or otherwise: then it is necessary that you cauterize him with four cauteries, or five on the vertebrae of the back, and between every vertebra with cautery, and in his chest with cautery, and on his stomach with cautery.
For tremor rests with the olive cautery and health hastens in the case of coldness of ailment.
On the method of cauterizing Albathir.
Chapter LVII.
Sometimes foul matter, that is, pustules, are expelled from the body of Albathir, which are made from corrupt coarse materials.
Therefore, first of all, when they appear, it is necessary to cauterize the entire head of the boil with a fine cautery and myrtle wood, whose end has been set alight with fire. Or with the root of long birthwort. Or with a lentil-shaped cautery.
And sometimes the adhesions are cauterized at the beginning of their expulsion in this way: and they do not increase; and the superfluity breaking them, they heal from them.
Nevertheless, it should be done after evacuation with venesection.
On the manner of cauterization for blood flow from an artery in a wound.
Many times blood flow occurs from an artery which has been cut at a wound, either externally or at the incision of a boil, or cautery of a limb, and similar things: and it becomes difficult to stop it.
When this happens to someone, then hasten with your hand to the opening of the artery, and place your index finger over it; and tightly compress it until the blood is stopped under your finger, and nothing comes out of it.
Then place in a small oil lamp cauteries and many more, and blow over them until they are well-ignited flames.
Then take from them one, or a little, or a large piece, according to the wound and place where the artery is ruptured, and lay the cautery upon the very vein, after which you remove your finger quickly: and hold the cautery until the blood is cut off.
If it is expelled with the finger from the mouth of the artery and the cauterium is extinguished, then take another cauterium more quickly, from those which are prepared in fire.
Therefore, do not cease doing this one after another until the blood is cut off.
And beware, lest you burn the nerve that is there, and evil befall the sick person further.
And know that when blood flows from an artery, then it is not possible to cut it off: especially when the artery is large, except with one of four methods. Or with a cautery, as we said. Or when it is not extracted. For when it is taken out, the extremities of it contract, and the blood is cut off. Or it may be tied with a strong ligature thread. Or medicinal substances may be placed upon it, which are of a property to stop blood, and it is bound with a poultice-like constriction appropriately.
But he who administers its cutting with a band, and stenosis with a cloth, or the placement of burning things, and similar things, does not benefit him entirely, unless rarely.
If this happens to someone, and there is no physician or medicine present, then he should begin with the position of the index finger over the opening of his vein, as we have narrated, and constrict it strongly until the blood is stopped from the upper part of the wound and above the artery. And the finger should not be moved, from above it with cold water of very great coldness always, until the blood clots and thickens, and is cut off.
And meanwhile he should consider what is needed from cautery, or from medicine.
On incision, perforation, and phlebotomy in wounds, and similar things.
We have said in the first chapter about every ailment in which it is convenient to use fire and medicine burning together, and its opportunities, its causes, and its instruments, and the forms of cauteries.
And I placed there chapters from the top of the head to the foot.
And I shall proceed in this chapter along the same way: so that he who seeks may be relieved of his sought-for thing.
And before I begin this, it is necessary that you learn, O sons, that in this chapter there is that which is from terror that is in the first chapter of cauterization.
And because of this, greater caution is needed, because in the operation which is in this chapter, often evacuation occurs with blood, with which life stands at the opening of a vein, or incision over an abscess, or opening of a fistula, or healing of a wound, or extraction of an arrow, or incision over a stone, and similar things: from all of which tremor and fear are associated, and death falls in many of them.
And I warn you, let not that thing fall upon you in which there is hesitation, for sometimes modes of men with modes of ailments fall upon you in this art. Among these are those who are angered by their illness, and death is light to them; because of the vehemence that they find from their infirmity and the length of their malice. And when pain is from fear it signifies death.
And of these there is one who gives his tibia and dictates it with him, hoping for health, and his illness is harmful.
Therefore it is not right that you consent to him who comes to you from those, whose manner is such. Let your caution be stronger than your greed and avarice. And do not presume anything from that, unless after certain knowledge, which verifies among you that which has arrived at a praiseworthy outcome.
And attend to the care of all your patients, with prognosis and indication of that which is fitting for health.
In that, indeed, there is for you aid in acquiring fame and glory, and memory and praise.
May God inspire you, O sons, with his understanding, and may he not take away from you guidance and inspiration; for it is in his hand.
And I indeed have arranged this Chapter by distinguishing it into chapters, according to what preceded in the chapter on cauterization, from the neck to the feet: that your seeking of what you want from it, and what you need, may be eased.
On the treatment of water that gathers in the heads of children.
Chapter I.
Know that this infirmity happens often to boys at birth, or when a midwife tightens the head of the boy without ease.
And whenever it also happens even from an unknown cause which is not known.
And I indeed did not see this illness in others than in children: and to everyone whom of them I saw it hastened death: therefore it seemed good to me to omit the operation on it.
And I indeed saw of these a boy whose head was filled with water, and his head increased every day until he could not sit upon himself because of the size of his head; and the moisture increased until he died.
And this humidity indeed, either collects between skin and bone, or it gathers under the bone above the fascia.
And the operation on that, if there is moisture between skin and bone, and the abscess is small, then it is necessary to find a fissure in the middle of the head, one in width: and let the length of the fissure be like two knots, until the moisture runs out.
And this is the shape of a spatula.
If therefore the moisture is more increased, and the abscess is larger, then put these two incisions, according to this form.
And if the moisture is under the bone, and its sign is that you see the sutures of the head open from all sides, and water is depressed when you press it with your hand to the interior, and it does not hide from you: then it is necessary that you find three fissures in the middle of the head, according to this way.
And after the incision, remove all the moisture. Then bind fissures with cloths and pads. Then embrocate it from above with wine and oil until the fifth day.
Then loosen the ligament, and care for the wound with a pad and ointment.
And do not let go of tightening the head with equality. And feed the sick with every dry food little humidity, until the limb is strengthened and healed.
And another way of incision is, that you consider where the magnitude of the abscess appears, and the collection of water.
Because sometimes it is on the back of the head, or on the forehead, or on the right side, or on the left side.
Therefore ascend with the incision where pus and water filling appear to you: and cut it according to what is possible for you.
And observe, so that you do not cut the artery, and there occurs a flow of blood, and the sick person dies from that flow with the evacuation of moisture.
On the incision of arteries that are behind the ears.
Chapter II.
When pus occurs to someone's eyes or chest, and it becomes old, and medication does not help: then the last cure is in that, incision of these two arteries.
Therefore, when you wish to cut them, let the weak head shave itself. Then rub the place with a rough cloth, so that the arteries may appear. Then let the weak one tighten his neck with any of his cloths. Then consider where the vein pulses. And those places are places that are behind the ears depressed: and rarely is it hidden, except in some men.
Then mark with wax on both sides: and cut both with a spatula on each side, the incision reaching to the bone; and let it be across the width of the head.
And if you wish, insert a spatula under the artery, and cut it upwards with the incision.
And let the length of the incision be like two joined fingers.
For when a vein is cut, blood flows out with a pulsating outflow, which leaps into the interiors with frequent jumps.
If the artery does not appear to the sense, then it is necessary that measures from the ear the quantity of space three fingers. Then signs with a lead cautery, and find up to the bone.
And that which ought to be emitted from the blood is VI ounces and perhaps you emit more, or less from it. The whole thing according to the quantity which appears to you from the strength of the sick person and the filling of his artery.
Then consider the wound. Then if anything remains near the bone from the excision, then cut that out so that a hot abscess does not occur; then bind the wound with ligatures from linen cloth.
Then care for it with ointment until it heals.
On the extraction of arteries that are in the temples.
Chapter III.
When it happens to a man an old headache, or acute catarrh because of acrimonious humidities, and heat in the temples, or an old, violent soda, and similar ones: and it is treated with modes of medicine's cure, and it does not help: then we have experienced in these diseases the extraction of arteries from the temples, or cauterization of them, as we have narrated.
And the manner of operation in extracting them is that the weak should shave the hairs which are on the temples, and apply to the artery appearing in the temple. For indeed it is shown to you by its pulse, and rarely is it concealed except in a few men, or with extreme cold.
If it is hidden from you, then let the sick person wrap their head with any cloth of theirs: then rub the place with a rough cloth. Or place vapor with hot water until a manifest appearance appears to you. Then take the spatula which I formed a little while ago. Then scrape away the skin with it until you reach the artery. Then throw a hook into it and extract it upward until you have drawn it out of the wound and made it come out from the tunics that are underneath it on every side.
If, however, the artery is fine, then wrap it with the tip of a hook. Then cut from that part according to the quantity with which its extremities extend, and contract, so that there is no flow of blood. For if it is not struck, and if it is not cut off, blood does not rise fully.
Then evacuate from blood six ounces, up to three.
If the artery is large: then it is necessary to tie it in two places with a double, strong thread, of silk: or from cords of aloeswood, so that it may not quickly proceed to its own putrefaction before the consolidation of the wound, and there be a flow of blood.
Then cut away the superfluity which is between the two ligaments. Do this at that hour, or after another hour.
And if you wish, cauterize it with a knife cautery up to the bone, until its extremities are cut and it will stand in the place of that same work.
It is better to do so unless if the weak one has a fever, or a hot constitution. For a cautery is one of those things which help to end moistures. Therefore it is better healing.
And it is necessary after the extraction of arteries, that the place be filled with old charcoal: and pellets be placed upon it made in a central way.
And after the solution it is treated with dry medicines which make flesh grow, and with plasters until it heals.
If while you are operating blood flows from an artery, then hasten to cut it off with a cauterium, or to fill the place with zegi; and bind your hand until the blood is stopped. If none of those things is present for you, then place your finger over the location until the blood clots. And embrocate the place with water of violent cold until the sharpness subsides, and be bound tightly as is necessary.
And because it is lighter and easier to extract the artery, it is, that you cauterize the vein with this cautery having two blades: and let the sharpness of the two blades be similar to an almond. However, they are much less sharp than two knives. Because if they are sharp, like two knives, apply cold to both; and they will not cut the flesh with speed. When therefore there is a little thickness in them, fire heat is applied to them and flesh is quickly cut away; with this work is better than all works, and lighter, and easier.
And this is the form of a cautery.
And let the space that is between two knives be the quantity of the thickness of a finger, after you have marked two places with encaustic: and put both burning ones well, until they come to the bone, and cut the vein in two places: so that it may be lengthened which is between its extremities. For they are not thoroughly consolidated with this cautery.
Chapter IV.
On the treatment of acute and constant flow of tears from the eyes.
When there are continual tears, and there is a flow of them from the veins that are above the skull externally: and it makes no difference in them anything of medical treatment with medicines, and you see the face of the sick already red, and he feels on his forehead walking, as if it were the walking of ants: and his eyes are exhausted, moist, so that they are already corroded with corrosion: and his eyelids have been heated from the sharpness of the tears: then the ancients devised in the cure of them this operation, and called this cure, with the Sword.
And it is, as you should instruct the sick person to remove hairs that are on their forehead. Then, in the forehead, make three incisions equidistant according to the length of the forehead: and let the length of the incision be as two fingers, and one incision be equidistant in length from the nose in the middle of the forehead, and the second according to the length somewhat from the movement of the shoulders, and the third on the other side: and beware of the incision of the arteries that are on both sides: and extend your hand from the continuation of the two jaws, and let the space of all incisions be the quantity of three fingers joined together: and let there be a prepared sponge for you, or many dried cloths, with which you will dry the blood.
Then introduce a spatula of two sharp ends, whose following form is. From the incision which is at the side of the temple, as far as the second incision; and scrape with it the whole skin which is between the two incisions, together with the fascia, which is over the bone. Then do this also from the middle incision to the second incision. Then remove this spatula and again introduce into the first incision another instrument which is called a culter-like sharp one on one side, blunt on the other. And this is its form.
And place its sharp side towards the attached flesh with skin, and its blunt side towards the bone: and drive it until it reaches the middle fissure: and cut with it all vessels which descend from the head to the eyes: except that the incision shall continue up to that which appears from the skin.
Then make it in the middle fissure up to another fissure.
And after an equal quantity of blood has run out, express the places from the clots of congealed blood.
Then place in every fissure of the skin from boiled intestines, and place over them cotton balls soaked in wine, oil, or vinegar. And see that a hot abscess does not occur.
And on the third day loosen the ligament, and administer much infusion with lukewarm water. Then care for it with ointment of basil oil, and other things which are used to heal wounds, until it is healed.
Chapter V.
On the treatment of acute tears and catarrhs of the eyes from within the head.
When anyone has acute, sharp, persistent catarrh, and you see their eyes weakened, small, and their appearance already debilitated; and the eyelids are ulcerated, and hairs fall out from them, and there is a sharp, painful pain in the depth of the head, followed by sneezing: then take from these occurrences that those matters and catarrh do not come unless from many veins.
And better treatment for these is this. And it is, as you instruct the sick to shave their forehead. Then cut one incision in the middle of the forehead, or another somewhat lengthwise, and begin with the incision from the left temple to the right temple: and let the incision be up to the bone: and stretch your hand from the temples which move when chewing: until when the bone is revealed: and you will dry all the blood with sponges.
Then separate between the two lips of the fissure with a cauterized gut, or with linen ligatures. Then bind from above with wool: and soak the wool with wine and oil, so that a hot abscess may not occur.
And when you have loosened it, and see that the hot abscess has already diminished: then it is necessary to cauterize the bone until new flesh begins to grow in it.
Then care for it with a drying regimen that causes flesh to grow; as if you take two parts of wheat flour and four parts of colophony, and prepare a poultice from them, and apply it to the birth of flesh in these wounds.
Chapter VI.
On the treatment of that which falls into the ear.
Anything that falls into the ear is one of four kinds: either a mineral stone, or something like a stone, such as iron and glass; or it is from a plant, such as a chickpea and beans; or it is a fluid thing, such as water, vinegar, and things like them; or some kind of animal.
When therefore a stone falls into the ear, or a kind of stone from those which do not grow in the ear, expose the ear to the sun: if then you see the stone, then distill into the ear something of violet oil, or sesame. Then administer its extraction, and make him sneeze with the condiment, and plug his nostrils at the approach of sneezing, after you have placed wadding from cloth or wool around the ear, and extended the ear upwards.
For this ailment often occurs.
If it does not come out, then administer its extraction with Gesti, that is, Pizigarolis thin ones: whose form is this.
If therefore he goes out with a gasp, it is good.
And if not, then administer its extraction with a thin, curved hook of little duplication.
If it does not exit through that, then make a tube out of bronze, and insert the end of the tube into the ear opening a great deal, and cover what is around the tube with wax softened with oil, so that there be no way for the wind except through the tube; then draw it out with your own breath attraction strongly.
For often indeed it happens as we have narrated. If this method does not work, then take from helkalimbat, or from viscus, that is, prepared gluten with which birds are caught, a small amount: and put it at the end of the radius after you have wrapped upon it a cotum placed squarely; then introduce it into the ear foramen with facility, after you have dried the ear from humidity.
If therefore it does not come out with all that we have narrated, then hasten to cut it open before a hot abscess or spasm occurs.
And the manner of incision is, that the surgeon first make a cut on the diseased one's head and draw blood from it according to the measure of its virtue. Then make the patient sit between your hands, and turn his ear upward, and find a small fissure in the root of the ear in the place depressed by its fat; and let the fissure be lunate in figure, until you reach the stone: then remove it with that which is possible to you from the instruments.
Then sew the fissure with speed from your hour, and take care of it until it heals.
If something falling into the ear is one of those grains that grow and swell, then administer its removal.
If it does not obey you at the exit, then take a thin, subtle spatula, according to this form.
And administer with it the excision of that kind from the grains, which fall into the ear, and do not do this when you think that that grain is already moistened by the vapor of the ear, until you place yourself a multitude of splints: then extract them with hooks, or with a fine thread, or with suction, as we have said, for the extraction of it may be alleviated.
In the water which enters into the ear, it is necessary for the sick person to use a sneeze with some condiment first; and already he has filled his ear with a little boiled old wool; and he lies on that ear, in which there is water. If therefore it goes out through that, it is good.
And if not: then take many stones, according to the length of the finger, thin, soft, and heat them with fire for a little while, and let the sick person insert one of them into the hole of his ear: and let him leap upon one foot from that side, and strike with another stone upon the stone which is in the ear. Therefore, do not cease doing this with stone and stone until all the water emerges.
And now water goes out so that you take from papyrus or from rush one and insert one end of it into the ear: and set the other end on fire until much of it is burned. Then take another hook and do this many times until all the water comes out, and withdraw it with a tube according to what was previously said about the stone.
In extracting an animal that has entered it, if it is small in body, like a flea and the like, care for it as I said in the division.
But if it is of a large body, which appears to the sense; then administer its extraction with forceps and hooks: and the affair of it is easier than all those held in the ear.
In the extraction of a worm that has been generated in the ear, when you have treated it with what we have said in the division, and in the treatment of distillations, and your treatment does not help: then you must look at the ear in the sun. If therefore something appears there from worms, then remove it with pliers or with subtle hooks.
If however nothing appears there, then take a cannula, whose form is this, narrow below, wide above: and introduce the delicate end into the ear according to the quantity which the sick person can tolerate; then suck with it with strong suction. Do this many times until all worms come out.
If they do not obey you at the exit, then tightly surround the pipe with cauterization, as I said on the stone.
If they do not exit as we said, then administer distillations which the ancients have already tested for the destruction of worms: and you will find this in the treatise on distillations.
And let your pouring of oil and medicines into the ear be with this instrument, whose form is depicted here. As you see, make it from silver or copper, narrow below, with a small hole, wide above.
Therefore, let what is driving in the cavity of the cannula be made of properly centered metal. And if you wish, take a rod and wrap it at its end with a piece of cloth in a decent wrapping; then throw oil or juice, or whatever you wish from these medicines into a tube; and let the tube be in the ear; then introduce the rod with the cloth from above, and squeeze it with your hand in moderate compression until the oil is driven into the concavity of the ear, and the sick person feels it inside; and that which is poured into the ear should already be warmed with a little fire; and beware that what distills into it is not very cold: for medicines do not tolerate this.
Chapter VII.
On the treatment of the accident of earwax accumulation.
Sometimes infants emerge from their mothers' wombs unperforated.
And it also happens to some men that an inflammation occurs in the ear passage from a wound, or flesh born in it.
And this poultice is sometimes made deep in the ear hole, which sight does not reach; and sometimes it happens to the outside of the hole a little, and sight reaches it.
Therefore that which happens in the depth of the ear, and which is not comprehended by sight, is in many dispositions difficult to heal.
And in that which follows the sight, you ought to place the ear of the sick person in the sun, and look at it: then if you see an apparent hairiness, open it with a fine spatula, whose form is this which follows, let its extremity be in which there is some small width, whose part be sharp: and the rest of the spatula be gentle on two sides: so that it does not injure the ear.
If therefore the earwax is born of flesh in the passage of the ear: then hold it with a fine hook, and cut it with facility of force until all the flesh is removed.
If the suppuration is deep in the ear, then take a thin, soft rod and heat it slightly with fire; then put it into the ear canal.
If therefore you feel swelling with that which the sick person finds with heaviness of hearing, attempt to open it with facility: and beware lest you wound the nerves of the ear.
Then place licorice in the ear according to the amount of width of the opening which you have enveloped in Egyptian ointment; do this for several days until you are secure about the consolidation of the wound.
Or take licorice and pour it into rose water, and pulverize over it ground zinc, and use it.
If you see the ear already suppurating, then it is necessary to extract the pus and allow it with another lint cloth, which you wrap in a cauterized bandage that has been made with rose oil, until the abscess sits hot; then take care of it until it heals.
If it happens that there is a flow of blood, then immerse a sponge or cloths in cold water and place them over the ear: and use the remaining cure which cuts off the flow of blood as previously stated in the treatise on distillations.
On the treatment of a wound in the eyelids.
Chapter VIII.
Injuries of the eyelid which occur sometimes are humid and sometimes dry.
Therefore, it is necessary that you hold them with pliers: or with a hook, and cut them with a spatula from their roots. Then place powdered ziziphus over the areas.
If blood flows from them, then cauterize them with a fine lentil-shaped cautery.
And to cauterize them is better after incision. For many times they return when something remains from their roots. And when they are cauterized with fire, those roots burn up and do not return.
On the treatment of prolapsed eyelids of the eye.
Chapter IX.
Many times something happens in the eyelids that is similar to a grain in its hardness and strength, and because of this it is called a Granuloma: and it is an aggregation of gross moisture in the upper eyelid, and the lower eyelid.
The operation therefore is, that you consider then, if there is a gland proceeding in it, which appears from the eyelid, and move to every part, and its matter is easy.
Therefore, make a cut across it. Then scrape it from every part with ease until it comes out. Then suspend it with a hook, and cut it away.
If you do not prepare the cutting for him before you penetrate the eyelid with a small incision, then it will harm him in some way.
If the fissure is large, then gather it with suture and care for it until it heals.
And if it is small, no evil will come to you from it. For the ointment restores it itself, and makes flesh of it.
And if the granulation is large and slopes towards the inner surface of the ear, then turn the eyelid back, and suspend the granulation with a hook: besides which you will need to make an incision, and press it from every part.
If the eyelid is lengthened through an incision, it does not harm in any way.
Then wash the eye after the cutting of a cataract with salt water; and take care of the place with it that it may become flesh until the sick man is healed.
On the treatment of a harelip
Chapter X.
Axirnath is a fat that is generated in the tunic of the upper eyelid: and it happens most often to children: and it weighs down their eyes, and catarrh occurs in them in the mornings, and they cannot look at the light of the sun, because tears rush to them, and therefore you see them lying on their faces always, and sleep on them, and their eyelids are moist under the eyebrows, which have already been covered by inflation: and the prominence of Axirnath is apparent to the eye, and when you press the place with your fingers, you feel Axirnath between them.
And the mode of operation is that he place his head in your bosom.
Then take a linen cloth, and prepare a ligature in it, and make a circle from that ligature according to the quantity that contains Axirnath from every part. Then place him upon it, and press with your fingers from every part, that Axirnath may be gathered in the middle of the circle.
Then find in the middle of that moisture with a spatula cutting incisions across its width, and let not the incision be larger than that made in phlebotomy in depth.
Either it is necessary to cut through all the skin until you reach Axirnath, for in many arrangements Axirnath proceeds from a fissure according to the shape of white fatty twig.
Therefore extract it with the cloth, which you have soaked between your fingers to the outside: and you turn your hand right and left until it is scattered. And beware, lest you add it into the fissure, so that you do not reach the eye with the spatula and wound it.
If you do not see Axirnath at the beginning of the fissure, add a little into the fissure with ease until Axirnath proceeds. Then remove it, as we said.
Then immerse cloths in vinegar and water, and place them over the site, and bind it with a splint.
And there are some men who grind salt and place it in the hollow of the fissure so that it may liquefy what remains of that moisture.
Then care for it until it is healed.
If a hot abscess occurs in a place, then treat it with soothing plasters until it heals.
On ways of treating a drooping eyelid, that is, when the eyelid softens the eye so that it wrinkles as it ought not.
Chapter XI.
When hairs grow on the eyelids beyond the course of the natural hairs, and age: then they harm the eye, and cause modes of illness, such as frequent tearing, and softening of the eyelids, and clouding, until it becomes the cause of the destruction of the eye.
And a cataract of the eye is cured according to four ways. Or with fire by cauterization. Or with acute medicine, according to what was said before in the chapter on cauterization. Or it is treated by Ataxmir through incision and suture. Or through tubes, as I shall say.
You must place the head of the sick person in your bosom. Then turn the eyelid of the eye with your left hand.
If therefore it turns, it is good. And if not: introduce a needle into it, in which there is a thread from the lower part of the eyelid, and make the needle penetrate with the thread from above, and let it be near the hairs themselves, that is, near the edge of the eyelid, where the hairs are born: and draw the thread upwards with the eyelid, and turn it with the radio.
Then find in the inner part of the eyelid under the added hairs with a spatula cutting from the greater lacrimal gland to the lesser lacrimal gland.
Then remove the thread and place a small piece under the eyelid from cooked cloth or linen; then sign over the eyelid with encaustic wax so that it is like the shape of a myrtle leaf.
Nevertheless, it is necessary that the figure be made according to the quantity and elevation of the eyelid; because indeed it is diversified in men.
From these [techniques] is one who needs that it be cut from the eyelid a quantity large, according to the quantity which softens from the eyelid.
Then when you have marked two lines with the spathulum and begin from the larger lacrimal gland to the smaller one, and let there be a fissure near the natural hairs, as thick as the thickness of a radius.
Then introduce a hook into one of the two corners of the skin. Then scrape the entire lesion. Then gather with suture the two lips together, with a needle and fine wool thread; and wipe away the blood, and make adhere what is superfluous from the threads over the two supercilia, with some things that are hard to come by, if you wish to do so: and if not, do not care.
Then leave the suture and thread for three or four days, then care for it afterward.
And if you wish, let the wound go without suture, and care for it in such a way that it dries and contracts. For the eyelid must be raised when sealing a wound and bringing it together; but it will be better later.
This mode of healing Ataxmir taught the ancients.
Nevertheless, there is concern over the sick person, and it is from a good operation, and there is no fear in it.
And another method is in Atxamir also: and it is, as signs on the eyelid a figure which is like the figure of a myrtle leaf, as we have narrated.
Then lift the eyelid with three separate or combined hooks, according to the following form, then cut what is superfluous with small scissors, according to this form, with equality of incision.
If it is not possible for you to hold the hook and it is not equal: then take an awl in which there is a thread, and introduce it in the middle of the figure: and introduce another thread near the greater tear duct, and the second thread near the lesser tear duct: and gather together between your fingers the threads with equality.
Then lift your hand with an equal elevation; and lift the skin above which is the signated wound, as we have narrated.
Then gather the two lips of the wound together with a suture: and take care of it until it heals.
If however an abscess occurs at your incision or your fissure: then treat that abscess with a cautery and similar things, from sedative ointments.
And sometimes it happens that the eyelashes also turn inwards.
Therefore, you ought to use that which we have said about incisions; but suture is more artificial on both sides. On the treatment of ataxia with dogs.
And it is, as you convert the eyelid, and cut the fissure which is inside, according to what I have narrated. Then by art make two canes, or two fine strips of wood: the length of which should be according to the length of the eyelid, and their width should be less than the width of a spatula: and you have already cut at the extremities of both from both parts where you hold the threads.
Then gather both together with subtlety over that which is superfluous from the eyelid of the eye, and bind two canes from both parts with a strong binding, and release it for several days. For if the skin is tightly bound, it will die and become discolored and rot until it falls off by itself.
If therefore it delays, then cut it with shears. Then care for it until it is healed.
When therefore it is consolidated, elevate the eyelid: and hairs do not prick.
And this is the shape of two tubes, which you will make according to this figure, and quantity in length and width.
Chapter XII.
On the elevation of piercing hairs in the eye with an awl.
The operation consists of taking a fine needle and inserting into it a thread made of fine silk, soft. Then bring together the extremities of it, and knots both knots very finely: and let the length of the threads be one palm.
Then place another subtle thread under the first in Alinxotati [a type of surgical clamp], and tie the ends of it if you wish.
Then let the patient lay his head in your lap, and be near the sun as the operation is declared; because it is a subtle or minute operation.
Then introduce a needle with an awl into the root of the eyelid, and make it penetrate quickly into the eyelid until you extract it from above the natural eyelash.
Then draw the needle up above the incision; and introduce the needle into the incision: if there is one, or two, or three, no more.
Then draw your hand with it until the hair comes out with its root in the eyelid with the natural hair.
If however the eyelash is pulled out and the hair does not come out with it, pull the eyelash downward, with the thread which you have composed in it, until the eyelash comes out of the upper opening, and you see it with your eye: then pull the eyelash and the thread, and extract both, and close the eye: and let him keep it closed for two or three days, until the hair adheres to its place, and flesh grows over it.
If the hair is short, then either associate with it a long hair from natural hairs, and elevate it with it; or let it go, until it lengthens after a space, and then elevate it.
Chapter XIII.
When this happens to the eye, it is called Leporino.
And this Al-Zahrawi is either natural or accidental.
Therefore, an accidental wound is made from the consolidation of a wound, or section, or cautery, and things like these.
And the mode of operation on it is, that you may find that consolidation and that you may separate its lips, and place in it what is between them a ligature made of linen, and bind it until it heals.
And it is not proper that you use in its cure things that dry and constrict, for if you do this, Alxatrati will return worse than it was. Rather use those things which soften, such as hay: and embrocate with water in which has been cooked marshmallow root, flax seed; and ointment of dyaquilon, which has been liquefied with some oil; and envelop with it licorice, and care for it with this.
And your treasury of healing is, that you endeavor with every skill, so that it may not be solidified, according to the form which was in the first place.
And this operation which we have said, is nothing but a certain rectification of Alxatarati, so that it does not return to the form of the place according to what it was altogether.
On the treatment of a tumor on the lower eyelid, which is a wrinkle or contraction upward to the right and left.
Chapter XIV.
This incision which is made below, is truly called Alxatrati. It is natural and it is accidental.
And an accidental wound is made from a cut, or fissure, or cautery, and those similar things.
And the way of operation in it is, that you take a needle, in which there is a doubled thread, and dye it in flesh, and make it penetrate from the left lachrymal to the right lachrymal, until the thread comes to both extremities of the flesh.
Then extend the flesh upward with a knife, and cut it with a wide spatula. If therefore the shape of the eyelid returns as it ought, it is good.
And if not, take a probe and put it in the fissure, and turn it with it eyelid: and find two fissures on the inner side of the eyelid, and let the extremities of the two fissures be from the two angles of the incision, until they meet and an angle is made of them; until when they are gathered together, the figure of them is made thus, and is the Greek letter Lambda.
Then remove that flesh by the amount which is the lower sharp side of it, from what follows the eyelid. Then gather the separated parts together; sew them with a wool thread.
Thereafter care for them with that which we have said, of softening medicines and poultices, until they are healed.
And if an laceration should occur from a cut or suture, or cautery: then it is necessary that you make the incision flat under the eyelashes, also according to what preceded. Then separate between the two lips with a ligature, as we have said.
And the sum of the discourse on the cure of a wound from an ax, whether it is above or below, is that the treatment should be carried out according to how the wound was prepared according to the form of the ax.
For it is of many varieties in form: and a prudent craftsman prepares the organ in every possible way until it returns to its natural form, or nearly so, and the patient himself, according to what is suitable for him from the operation and instruments in many dispositions.
Chapter XV.
On the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva, or cornea.
Sometimes this consolidation happens to many people, so that the upper eyelid solidifies with the white of the eye, and prevents it from its natural operations.
Therefore it is necessary that you insert the end of the radius under the eyelid and raise it upward: or extend it with a hook: then cut the adhesion with a spatula of fine subtlety, which should not be with the sharpness of the incision of spatulas, but rather blunt for a short while: so that the sick person may not be disturbed during the operation, and the spatula is removed, and does not damage the eye with its sharpness: and let your incision be as though you were excoriating that adhesion with ease, until when the eyelid returns according to its natural form, and the adhesion heals completely.
Then pour saltwater into the eye, and wash it with water; or distill into it from a sieve made, from linen dissolved with water.
Then separate between the eyelid and the eye with a linen thread: and place above the eye wool soaked in alum and white of egg: and on the third day use sifter, and with solidifying matter until it is healed.
On the cutting of the nail and the prominence of the flesh of the tear duct.
Chapter XVI.
Know that a nail is according to two modes. Or it is a nerve; and it is similar to the sinew, fine. Or it is not fleshy, like the frozen moisture of white: which when it touches iron or you attempt to grasp it with a hook, it breaks off and the hook does not catch in it.
And both kinds do not begin, unless from the greater tear duct, as far as it goes gradually, until it covers the desired amount, and prevents light and the movement of the eye.
And the mode of operating in the incision of it is: that the sick person places his head in your bosom. Then open his eyes, and lift his eyelid with your hand. Then collect a sponge with a hook of small duplication, and extend it upward. Then take a needle and insert into it a thread from horsehair, oxhair, or wool. Let the thread be strong and double the end of the needle slightly, and fix it in the middle of the hoof. And pierce it with the needle and tie the hoof with the thread, and extend it upwards.  And scrape with the hair the side of the hoof that follows the pupil, and if you bind it with hair to its very end.
Then cut the residue at the root of the lacrimal with a fine spatula, or with small forceps: and let go the natural flesh of the lacrimal, so that there does not happen from the incision of it a continuous flow of tears.
And sometimes a nail is extended with a hook, or with a thread, as we have said. Then it shall be scraped with a spatula of moderate shape.
And be careful, lest the spatula touch the corneal tunic and a rupture occur in it, and the pupil be pierced.
Then after the incision, put something of salt ground up, or from sifting made of zinia, into the eye and let it be until the next day. Then care for him with what is necessary until he is healed.
If the claw is not nerve-bearing and you cannot introduce a needle into it, nor does a hook fasten in it, then this kind of claw is cured many times with medicines, and heals as I have told in the division.
If you wish to cut it, then open the eye of the sick person, and take a spatula subtle, gentle, of weak sharpness, according to this form. And with a subtle shaving shave the area from above. If you see that it becomes thin and breaks apart, and a razor imprint is made upon it, then distill from your hour into the eye an extract made from ziniar, or from red sief, or from ground salt: and cover the eye until the next day.
Then iterate over the same operation until the whole of it is removed. Unless it is contrary for you in the eye a hot abscess: then indeed dismiss that, and take care of the abscess, until it heals.
Then repeat the operation on it with reason until it heals.
If the prominence of the lacrimal flesh harms the eye greatly, it should be suspended with a hook and a part of it incised; and do not make the incision to the end so that the flow of tears does not occur.
Then a drop of wine (or made from zinc) in the tear sac, until it heals.
On the incision of Alguardenic, and flesh born in the eye.
Chapter XVII.
Sometimes in some human eyes there is born flesh red in color, composed or superimposed, until it covers the pupil, or approaches it, or tightens over the eyelids, and perhaps turns the eyelids outward: and it resembles the stalk of a rose.
And the operation in this also is that the sick person put his head in your bosom. Then open his eyes, and collect that flesh whole with suitable hooks to it: and hold it with one forceps, or tongs.
Then cut the flesh first until the whole of it is finished with a cutting instrument. And guard your eye so that you do not wound it during the operation: and let your incision be with the same thing which you cut with, or with a spatula, which we have told of in the incision of the nail: or with small scissors, with which one gathers the asbel, according to what will come its shape after this chapter.
When therefore the incision is complete, and the whole algoudenici is removed, then fill the eye with salt ground; or distill into it a powder of red and similar to it from corrosive medicines; and place above the eye a cotton ball with the white of egg: so that it may be secured from a hot abscess.
If something from the blade remains and you have a hot blood flow, and fear a hot abscess; then release the eye, and treat it with that which will settle the abscess.
Then iterate over it with the operation until it is healed.
And similarly do with flesh added which happens in the eye of this kind.
Nevertheless, it is necessary that the treatment of every weak eye, whether by natural or accidental way, be performed with iron: and do not put your hand into any of these operations until you see that that member is enduring that operation.
On collecting the style from the eye.
Chapter XVIII.
There are red veins that are woven over the eye and prevent the sight from carrying out its function: and they weaken the eye as the days lengthen.
Therefore it is necessary for you first of all to consider if the eye in which there is a Sebel is strong and there is no other pain except Sebel; then collect the Sebel from it.
And it is, as you prescribe to the sick person, that he should put his head in your bosom.
Then suspend those veins with one hook, or two according to your judgment: and let the hook be a fine doubled one, according to this form, or let there be two hooks graduated in one body, according to this form.
Then collect with fine forceps those veins with subtlety, and wipe away the blood every hour until you see those veins removed from the eye, and resolved: and beware lest you wound the eye with the edges of the spatula.
And let your operation be in midday before the sun, and be firm in your operation greatly: that you do not cut except those veins.
And when you have finished, then distill into the eye a green or red ointment: so that its sharpness corrodes what remains of the scab.
If it is not possible for you to collect the whole thing in that hour, then apply a poultice to the eye with something that will soothe the pain of the eye; and leave it for several days until the pain subsides; and secure it from hot suppuration; then repeat over it in the same way until it heals.
And this is the shape of the forceps.
On the reduction of the arrisatus to the nose.
Chapter XIX.
Surgeons call a fistula an "Arissati," which they treat with cauterization, or with a sharp burning medicine, according to what has preceded in the narration.
And if it does not heal, then there is no skill in it: unless you find a collection of pus at its birth, and extract the entire moisture which is in it and pus, until the bone is exposed.
When therefore a bone is discovered, and you see in it corruption, or blackness, then scrape it with an instrument, whose form is this.
And it is called Asperi head.
Let it be made of iron, and let its head be round like an azir, which is to be marked with the point of a file, or the point of a chisel with a fine point: and put it over the place corrupted in the bone.
Then roll it between your fingers, and you press your hand briefly until you know that the corruption is already scraped away; and do this several times.
Then restore the place with medicinal dried astringents.
If therefore the place is consolidated, and flesh is born in it, and the course of healing is severed: and it remains for forty days, and is not renewed, and does not suppurate, and no accident happens in it: then know that it is now healed.
And if not, then there is no talent in him, unless he reduces the fistula to the nostril opening, according to this form.
And it is, as you remove from the bone with an iron instrument, or with a sharp medicine: and when the bone has been exposed, then take a borer according to this form, let its iron tip be triangular: and its wood be pyramidal extended to the end, just as you see.
Then place it over the bone itself: and let it be near the tear duct: and extend your hand away from the eye a little. Then roll your hand with the trephine until it penetrates the bone, and feel that it is yielding; by this you will find air coming out of it when you hold your hand over his nose.
Therefore then restore the place with hemostatic and drying medicines, as I have taught you.
When therefore flesh is born and the place hardens, then the matter which was poured out to the exterior returns to the nose; therefore it is lighter upon the weak.
On the reduction of the prominence of the eye.
Chapter XX.
When the eye protrudes with its entirety, and there is no harm or diminution in vision, then it is necessary to loosen the diseased part; then let him make a bloodletting; afterwards place vents on the neck without scarification, but with suction.
Then put over the eye a liniment made of acacia, and aloe, and frankincense, and sarcocolla. Then tighten the eye from above over the plaster with many pads.
And the method of a few is: that you may take soft cloths, many, and every cloth be doubled out of them over four: make many from these: then place them one upon another, according to the quantity which the Eye needs: then bind upon them with a hard cloth a strong binding.
And again wounds with suction without incision.
Then remove the wind-filled [parts], and leave the eye tightly closed day and night.
If, however, the ligature softens again, then it is necessary that it be tightened a great deal; then remove the ligature after a day and night, as we have said.
If therefore you see the eye already returning to its white tunic, it is good; and if not, then repeat the plasters, and poultices, and constriction, and fomentations, until it heals.
On the incision of the urinary tunic.
Chapter XXI.
When a rupture occurs in the tunic of the eye, then it protrudes and proceeds outside the eyelids like a grain of wheat, and the appearance of man is defiled because of it, and you wish to cut it: then the operation on it is, according to what I tell: and it is, that you introduce a needle into the root of the eye from the lower to the upper part.
Then introduce another needle into which there is a doubled thread from the lacrimal part, and make it penetrate: and let go of the first needle according to its position.
Then cut the place of the double thread, and tie with it the part of the vein on the circumference of the needle: and tighten it very much.
Then extract the point and put over the eye wool soaked in albuginea; and let it go until the thread falls with water.
Then care for the eye with that which comforts it, until the wound heals.
And sometimes the eye is bound by a circle of cloth from every part until it gives great prominence.
Then it is pierced with a fine spatula until it reaches the depth of the eye, and the albuminous moisture is scraped away, and the eye descends, and immediately enters; then it is tightened until it heals.
On the treatment of dislocations.
Chapter XXII.
This ailment called Alcomnati is nothing else but a pus that gathers in the eye, similar to water descending, and it is not itself.
And the mode of operation in it is that you make the sick person sit on the prepared seat, or directly.
Then take the head in your hands from two parts, and move it until you see the stream arrive at the lower parts with your eyes. Then it is fastened, so that it does not descend, and the opening is closed: and the sick person may see things as he used to see; and if it does not descend below, know that it is water.
If however the descent of the bowels is not prepared with that which we said, then make the sick person sit between your hands. Then take a subtle spatula and find with it above the skin tissue near the continuation of the conjunctiva with the corneal tunic in the circle, until pus comes out.
When he exits, then distill into the eye hot water in which you have mixed honey; or water in which you have already boiled hay and honey; then cure with the rest of the cure, until it heals.
On the treatment of water which descends into the eye, or cataract.
Chapter XXIII.
Now we have discussed the kinds of water in division, and which is suitable for piercing, having explained it with words, then take that same thing according to health.
Therefore you must make the sick person sit between your hands, square to the light, near the sun: and tie his healthy eye shut, and bind him firmly; then with your right hand, if the eye in which there is water is the left eye, or with your left hand, if the eye is the right.
Then take with your right hand the almond if the eye is left; or with your left hand, if the eye is right.
Then place the end of the probe near the crown through the thickness of the radius in the apparent conjunctiva of the smaller lacrimal eye; then push the probe with force and you will turn your hand with it until it penetrates into the conjunctiva of the eye, and you feel that the probe has already reached the hollow.
And it is necessary that the measure of the journey from the almond to the depth be the measure of the space which is from the pupil to the end of the blackness: and it is the circle of the eye. You will see it in the pupil itself of the eye, because of the clarity of the cornea tunic.
Then place the almond until it reaches the place where water is. Then press it downwards with one finger after another.
If therefore water descends, then the infirm person sees it above which he opens his sight immediately, and the almond in his eye.
Then rest for a little while. If therefore water should flow down, then put it aside second, except that it may go out from the almond.
When therefore it is set, and does not ascend: then extract the almond with ease: and you revolve your hand gradually.
Then dissolve something of clear salt in water: and wash the eye inside with it. Then place a whole wad of cotton wool or soaked wool steeped in rose oil, and with the membrane of an egg, and tie the eye together with it.
We, and those of our time, put upon it a mixture of ground cinchona with albumen of egg.
If the eyelid does not obey you at the entrance into the eye because of its hardness, because it is from men, whose eyes are hard indeed: then it is necessary that you take a spatula, which is called Alberid, whose shape this is.
Therefore, perforate with it only that conjunction and not the last of your hand into the hole; and there is nothing but to make a way to the almond place subtle.
Then introduce the almagd, as we have said.
And in the completion of your work, prepare for the sick person a room well-made, in which he may lie on his back in a dark house: and forbid him from all movements, and from coughing: and put food before him that soothes his nature: and let him not move his head right or left at all.
And let the ligature remain according to its arrangement up to the third day.
Then solve it in that dark house, and try to experience its sight; and make him see things. Then reduce the ligament up to the seventh day.
And it is not proper that he should do this at the hour of healing, or immediately after the incision: indeed, it is necessary that he transfer it elsewhere; because water ascends quickly through vigorous aspect.
If however a hot abscess occurs: then it is necessary that you loosen the eye before the seventh day; and correct it with what soothes the abscess, until it is soothed; then loose its vision, and place over its face an oral [potion] which regulates its vision under those few days, itself being in that dark house.
Then he shall go out of the house gradually, and turn in his affairs.
And know that in surgery a student is not excused from seeing often: then indeed he may dare about the operation.
And now indeed it has come to us from one of those who are from Al-Rayy, who said that a large hole was made in Al-Rayy, through which water is drawn.
Truly, therefore, I saw no one in your land who has done that; nor did I read it in any of the ancient books; and it is possible that it is new.
And these are the forms of the kinds from the book, so that he who does not know them may stand upon them.
Let them be made of metal, and let their extremities be triangular with this same fineness, and the tip acute.
Chapter XXIV.
On the treatment of flesh born in the nose.
Sometimes fleshy growths of a different kind are born in the nose, of which some resemble the legs of many scorpions: and from this there is cancerous flesh, stony, dusky colored: and from this there is also flesh that is soft, not dusky coloured.
In those things which are from these flesh is gentle, not deceitful, nor cancerous, it is necessary that you make the sick person sit between your hands opposite the solid one, and open his nostrils, and throw in a hook to those flesh: then draw them to the outside, and cut what you have grasped from them with a sharp fine spatula on one side, until you know that all the flesh has already been removed.
If something remained of it whose cutting is not possible: then carefully remove that with one fine instrument with ease, until nothing remains of it. If blood governs you, or if a hot abscess occurs, then oppose it with what is necessary. Or if it is from malignant abscesses, then hasten to cauterize it, until the blood is cut off, and all flesh is removed.
Then vinegar, water, or wine is poured into the nose after incision. If, however, the nose is opened and moisture proceeds from it up to the palate, then know that it is already healed.
If moisture does not penetrate as it should, then know that within it is flesh born in the upper bones of the nose, to which the instrument does not reach when cut.
Therefore, you must insert a linen thread which has some thickness, and many knots in it. And place between every knot the quantity of a finger, or less. And instruct the infirm man, that he may put one end of the thread into the nose with a stick, or as much as is possible to him after he has made himself a similar probe. And draw his scent until it reaches the cavity, and emerges above his throat.
And many times indeed boys like this Attomi make similar things in schools: and it is a thing easy for him who wants to.
Then put two ends of thread, one end which comes out over the nose, and the other which remains in the nose. Then and with a minister lock of flesh with knots that are on the thread. Do this until you know that the flesh is already with thread knots.
Then extract the thread and place it in the nose after removing the blood clot which you absorbed with the ointment of Egypt: do this for three days or more until the ointment corrodes all that remains of the flesh.
Then put a lead tube into the nose at the end of the matter for several days until it heals.
If however it requires treatment that will dry it out, use this.
To make a cauterizing head, with which oil and other medicines are distilled into the nose, make it of silver or copper: that it be like a small lamp, open like a shell: and its passage similarly.
And if you wish to make a closed tube, like a reed.
And the shell of the instrument for making a cautery should be full and open: which has a handle at its end, with which you hold it when you heat oil in it, or what you wish from juices, and flowing substances.
Chapter XXV.
Many times a tumor is born at the extremity of the nose, and it grows larger, and increases with days until its appearance becomes foul.
Therefore, it is necessary that at the beginning of its appearance, one should cut into it and eradicate its roots completely. Then place upon the site either a cautery or burning medicine which stands in its place.
If therefore the incision is delayed until it has become enlarged, then consider if it is stony, hard, dusky colored, with few senses; then do not touch it with iron, for it is a cancerous abscess.
Many times I have seen those who had a bloody abscess and because of it great evil happened to him who had it.
And if the abscess is gentle to the touch, not dusky in color, and you see that incision is possible throughout its entirety, then evacuate the sick person, and make an incision into it without fear or trembling: and care for the place with something that dries and constricts, until it heals.
Chapter XXVI.
On the suture of the nose, and lip, and ear.
Know that when a rupture of continuity occurs in one of these cartilages, little is accomplished by operating on it, except in some men.
Therefore, when something of that sort happens to someone, you should consider if it is a wound recent with its blood; and then gather the edges of the wound together with suture; then care for it until it heals.
And if the lips of the continuity have already been separated, and each part has been made healthy: then it is necessary that the curette be scraped over the entire part with its sharp edge until it bleeds. Then gather the two lips together with a suture, and tighten them, and pulverize on top of it dragon's blood and ground frankincense: and put above the powders a piece of diaphana plaster, and of some other solidifying plaster: and leave it bound for two or three days. Then dissolve it itself, and change the medicine: and dismiss it until the threads are cut by themselves. Then care for it with ointment until it heals.
The method of suturing is that you bring together the edges of continuity, either with awls, as we recounted in the suturing of the abdomen: or you bring together the edges with thread, as I have made you know.
Chapter XXVII.
On the extraction of lip nodes.
Many people experience abscesses on their inner lips, similar to hard grains of grain, some smaller and some larger.
Therefore it is necessary that you convert the lip and bind over every node of it, and suspend it with a hook, and cut it from every part.
Then fill the place after the incision with crushed zeger, until the blood is stopped.
Then it is to be covered with vinegar, and the place where there is clotting is to be cared for with it until the wounds are healed.
Chapter XXVIII.
On incisions in the flesh above the gum.
Many times there is born on the gum flesh which the ancients call an abulus.
Therefore it is necessary that you suspend it with a hook: or hold it with forceps, and cut it at its root, and let the pus run out, or the blood.
Then place on the rubbed wound site, or other styptic powders, drying ones.
If that flesh returns after treatment, for it often does return, then cut into it again and cauterize it: for it does not return after cauterization.
Chapter XXIX.
On the extraction of teeth with iron.
Sometimes they gather on the surfaces of the teeth from the inner and outer part, and between the gums, rough, coarse, and sometimes they become blackened, and yellowish, and greenish, until corruption comes from it to the gums, and the teeth become foul because of it.
Therefore, you ought to make the infirm sit between your hands, and his head in your bosom, and grind and gnaw, at that which appears to you as bark, and a thing similar to sand: until there remains nothing of them, and similarly do with blackness, and greenness, and yellowness, and others until they are purified.
If therefore that which is in them from the beginning of reason is taken away, it is good: and if not, then repeat upon them the reason on another day, second, and third, until the last study comes to that which you wish.
And know that a tooth needs razors of various forms and figures: according to how it is prepared for your operation; therefore, the razor with which a tooth is shaved from the lower part is different from the razor with which a tooth is shaved between teeth, according to other forms.
And these forms of razors should be prepared for you, which you see.
Chapter XXX.
On the Extraction of Tooth Pain.
It is necessary that you treat the tooth of its pain with every skill and delay in its extraction. For there is nothing that can replace it when it is removed: because it is a sublime substance: so that when there is no excuse for its removal, then when the sick man is forced to remove it, he must strengthen and make sure of the aching tooth with you.
For often pain deceives the sick person and thinks that it is in a tooth and pulls it out; then the pain does not cease until the bad tooth is pulled out.
For we have now seen that from the operation of bloodletting.
When therefore a tooth aching is certified to you, then it is necessary that you scarify around the tooth with a spatula, in which there is some virtue, until the gum is loosened from every part; then move it with your fingers, or with slender forceps at first little by little, until you shake it.
Then place the large forceps in that spot and the head of the patient between your knees: which you have already held well so that it is not moved; then pull the tooth according to its straightness so that you do not break it.
If therefore it does not emerge, then take some one of those instruments and introduce it under it from every side with facility, and endeavor its motion, as you did at first.
If, however, the tooth is perforated or corroded: then it is necessary to fill that hole with cloth; and bind it with the extremity of a fine wire, so that it does not break at the time of your constriction upon it with forceps.
And it is necessary that you act with great care when making an incision around the gum from every part very much.
And keep your strength, so that you do not break it, and may its part remain, and return over the sick from that evil which is greater pain of his first.
And beware, lest you do what ignorant bloodletters do in their haste and boldness over its removal, besides administering that which we have recounted.
Many times indeed they cause great illnesses to happen to men, the more difficult of which are those that break and the roots of them all remain, or some of them; or that they extract it with some bones of the jaw: as we have testified many times this.
Then it shall be covered after its removal with wine, or with vinegar and salt.
If it happens that blood flows from places (for this often happens), then smear something from the ziziphus and fill the place with it: and if not, cauterize it if you cannot apply ziziphus.
The shapes of the fine forceps used first for moving a tooth should have long handles and short grips, thick so that they do not fold when pressure is applied upon a tooth with them; like you see in thick handles, until when you tighten with them, they do not bend themselves nor fold; short handles, and be made of iron, or of well-crafted brass, straight handles. And at their ends should be teeth, some of which enter into others, and tighten by decent, strong constriction.
And sometimes extremities are formed like the beak of a bird called a stork, and they have strong constrictions.
Chapter XXXI.
On the removal of teeth and the extraction of fractured jawbones.
When something remains at the site of a broken tooth's removal, then you must place over the customary spot some butter for one or two days until the place softens; then introduce into it an instrument, or forceps: whose ends are similar to the beak of a bird that is called a stork. The shape of this is forceps.
Let their extremities be made like a file inward: or like an awl.
If therefore it does not obey you towards the exit with these forceps, then it is necessary that you dig above the root, and uncover the whole flesh, and introduce the instrument which is called the Small Lever, whose form is this.
Let the short end of the limb be rather coarse and not too wet so that it does not break.
If therefore the root comes out through that, it is good: and if not, it is necessary to help with these other instruments whose shapes are these.
The shape of the first triangular extremity, in which there is some thickness.
Shape of a triangular extremity fine.
And with other instruments and tools which preceded in the extraction of teeth.
Also let there be an instrument with this, which is similar to a large hook, of this form, triangular extremity twisted, in which there is something of thickness so that it does not break: and let it be not soaked.
And know that instruments for the teeth are many: and similarly the rest of the instruments are not counted, and a prudent artificer, subtle in his art, finds for himself the instrument, according to what the operation signifies to him and the pains themselves.
Because they are from agricultural implements, for which the ancients did not say what instruments, because of the diversity of their kinds.
If a bone of the jaw or other bones of the mouth is broken, or if it rots: then examine it in its place with that which is fitting to it from the instruments and forceps which I have said in the extraction of roots, and help with a clamp, whose form is this.
Let there be something of a size gradually inserted so that it is tightened or held with the bone, and not escape and leak, until the bone emerges; and restore the place with suitable medicines to it.
If there is a small amount of decay in the bone, then scrape away the decay from it, and take care of it until it heals.
Chapter XXXII.
On the closing of prominent teeth above others.
When teeth protrude not according to their natural course, an ugly form is produced: and especially when this happens in women and girls, namely those of the genitals.
Therefore you ought to consider. If a tooth is born after another tooth and does not fit, or it is possible to lock it, nor file it; then eradicate it: and if it is attached with another tooth, then cut it with this instrument, whose shape is this.
It is similar to a small beak, and it is made of Indian iron, with a very sharp tip.
And let your incision be such that you cut it on many days, and because of the hardness of the tooth, and so that another tooth is not moved.
If, however, it is protruding and exposed from its rasping, then scrape it with a rasp from within, whose form is this.
Let the entire instrument be from India, and the handle of it have many small punctures, like a file with which needles are made: With which one grinds the tooth gradually in many days with ease, so that the tooth is not moved, and falls out; then smooth it, at the end scrape it with certain razors.
If it is a tooth that is broken and injures the tongue, then it should be filed down until the roughness of the fracture is removed and it is leveled, smoothed, and does not injure the tongue or corrupt speech.
Chapter XXXIII.
On the binding of teeth with gold wire
When the front teeth are shaken and bruised by a blow or accident; and the weak one cannot hold it together when eating so that it does not fall out; and you have treated them with astringent medicines, and the treatment has not been effective; then the ingenuity is in them to be tied together with gold thread; because silver becomes green and rots after days; but gold never changes.
And let the thread be of medium thickness and coarseness according to the amount it will take between the teeth.
And the form of the suture is, that you take a thread, and insert twice its length between two healthy teeth: then weave with both ends of the thread between the moved teeth, whether one be, or more, until you come with the suture up to the healthy tooth of the other part. Then repeat the texture until you reach the part from which you began: and tighten your hand with subtlety and wisdom, until it is not moved at all: and let your constriction where you tighten the thread be at the roots of the teeth, so that it does not escape.
Then cut the ends of the superfluous thread with scissors: and gather both together, and turn them with a twist: and conceal them between the sound tooth and the moved tooth, so that it does not harm the tongue: then let it be let go as tightly as it remains.
If it is loosened, or cut, bind them together with another thread, and it will remain so throughout the whole time.
And this is the form of Teeth, and the figure of the rotation of two sound teeth, and two moved teeth, as you see.
And sometimes it happens that one or two teeth return to their places after an injury, and they remain as we have narrated, and this is done only by a skilled and experienced artificer.
And sometimes a piece of bone from the rib is fitted and shaped like a tooth, and it is put in its place, so that the tooth is fortified and held together, as we have said; and it remains, and serves with it for a long time.
Chapter XXXIV.
Sometimes this ligament which occurs under the tongue is either natural, with which man is born, or accidental, from a wound that has already healed.
And the operation on it is, that you open the mouth of the infirm person in his head in your bosom, and elevate his tongue; then cut that nerve ligament acrosswise until the tongue be loosed from its restraint.
If there is something hard and knotted in it, and it is from the consolidation of a wound, then throw into it a hook, and find it across its width, until you cut the ligament, and the knot is loosened.
And beware lest there be a fissure in the deep flesh, and you cut the artery there, and there occur a flow of blood.
Then the wound is to be rinsed with rose water, or vinegar, or cold water.
Then place under the tongue a linen ligature which the sick man should keep in throughout the night so that it does not harden again.
If blood flow should occur, then place a crushed piece of zege over the site. If blood overcomes you, then cauterize the place with a rusty cautery which is suitable for it; then care for it with the rest of the cure until it heals.
Chapter XXXV.
Sometimes a tumor occurs under the tongue that resembles a small frog, preventing the tongue from performing its natural function; and perhaps it magnifies so much that it fills the mouth.
And the operation in this is, that you open the wound of the sick person before the sun: and consider the abscess.
If therefore you see it of a dark color, or black, hard, which does not find the feeling of the sick person; then do not touch it, because it is cancer.
And if it is inclined to softness, in which there is moisture, then throw into it the hook and cut it with a fine spatula, and free it from every part. If blood overcomes you during the hour of your operation, then place crushed ziziphus on it until the blood is stopped; then return to your operation until you have extracted it with its entirety.
Then it shall be covered with vinegar and salt; and care for it with the rest of the appropriate treatment until it is healed.
On the treatment of an abscess of the two tonsils.
Chapter XXXVI.
Sometimes things happen between the throat and godad, that is, glands, which resemble algodad, which happen deforis: and they are named two Amigdalae: which when care with what we said in the division, and do not heal, then consider.
If therefore it is a hard abscess, dusky in color, with little sensation: then do not touch it with iron.
And if it is of ruby color and its root is thick: then do not touch it even with iron, fearing the flow of blood, rather let it alone until it matures. And then either you will pierce it yourself or it will burst open of its own accord.
And if it is white in color, round, and its root is fine: then it must be cut.
And the operation is this, that you consider before your operation, if its hot abscess has quieted and completed in quiet, or diminished by some diminution: then do so to make the sick man sit facing the sun, and his head be in your bosom: and open his mouth, and let a serving person receive between his hands, and press down his tongue below with an instrument, whose form is this.
Let it be made of silver, or copper, that is as fine as a knife.
When therefore you have laid the tongue aside with it, and an abscess is shown to you, and falls upon it your gaze: then receive a hook, and fix it in the tonsil, and draw it out to the outside as much as you are able, except that you pull along with it something from the tunics.
Then cut it with an instrument, whose form follows. It is like forceps: however, the extremities of it are curved so that the opening of each one is directly opposite to the other's sharpness; therefore care should be taken that it be made of iron tempered, or wood soaked in alum.
If this instrument is not present, then cut it with a spatula, whose shape is this. Be sharp on one side, not sharp on the other.
And after you have cut one tonsil, cut the other following this same type of incision.
Then after the incision, the patient gargles with cold water, or with vinegar and water.
If blood flow should occur to him, gargle with water in which the barks of pomegranate trees have been boiled: or myrtle leaves, and similar things from styptics, until the blood flow is stopped; then care for him until he is healed.
And sometimes abscesses are born in the throat, other than the two tonsils. Therefore, cut it according to what I said in the incision of two tonsils equally.
And I myself had treated a woman from an abscess that was formed between her throat, which she drew to the groin, few senses, because it had almost closed her throat; and the woman breathed through a narrow passage, and it had already prevented her eating, and drinking water; and she had already approached death, if she had remained for one day or two days.
And the abscess had grown so large that it had produced two branches until they emerged above the two nostrils.
I therefore hastened and fixed one of them hooks. Then I drew it out, and a large portion was drawn out from it.
Then cut it where it is grasped from the nostril.
Then I did that which had proceeded from the other nostril opening.
