And the color of an abscess is according to the color of the body, and no pain is found in it, and it is a serious abscess.
And perhaps it is a stone abscess, whose color is dark and there is no feeling in it.
And sometimes it happens from the tying of arteries, and their inflation: as I have already said in its remembrance: or from the inflation of veins.
What is from these by inflation of the arteries, is known by the arrangement of the abscess, when you compress it with your fingers. And you must not touch it at all.
That which is done by inflation of veins, does not disperse anything when compressed with fingers.
And now it is possible that a section be made upon this kind, and upon the remaining flesh: and it is, as to cut the skin of the testicles. Then stretch the omentum upward and draw it out with a white sponge: and free the suspensory ligament from the cavities, and tie the vessels: and cut the suspensory ligament after you have freed it from all parts of the parts.
If therefore the egg has become consolidated with those flesh born, then it is necessary that you draw the egg out, and cut it open.
And if adherence is between something from the siphon or in that which is between vessels: then it is necessary that you free the whole of that adherence, and cut it with a round incision.
If however it is a birth of flesh at the place of adhesion which is behind: then it is necessary that you cut the whole of it, and extract the egg, just as we have said.
When therefore your operation is completed, then fill the wound with wool steeped in oil of roses and wine; then care for it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
Chapter LXVIII.
On the care of branches, which is with the vine.
A boil is an abscess enclosed in a certain wrapping, like a tumor, with softening of the testicles: and it makes difficult movement and exercise on the weak and walking.
And in the healing of this illness is fear, because of remembrance of what preceded it.
Truly, it is necessary that we say the operation according to what the first ones did.
Therefore I say that it is necessary that you make the sick person sit upon a seat elevated. Then drive the testicular sling downwards. Then hold the skin of the testicles with your fingers, along with those vessels which are near the virga: and let another person hold it, and extend it with a strong extension.
Then make an incision with a wide, sharp spade across the straight vessels until the vessels are revealed.
Then skin is scraped from every part, as we have told you in the extraction of arteries that are in the temples.
Then make a point in it where there is a doubled thread, and tie it in the first of the places where the bites occur: and tie it again in the last of them. Then dry it in the middle incision straight according to the length of the body, and remove what is gathered from the fetid corrupted humidities.
Then care for wounds as with the cure of other wounds, so that a vein which nourishes one testicle may be generated to fall. It is not evil because of that.
If it happens that the veins are all swollen in the scrotum, then it is necessary to remove them together with the testicles so that the egg does not be deprived of nourishment because of the incision and waste away or wither, and so that there may be no benefit from them.
Chapter LXVII.
On the treatment of intestinal ruptures.
This injury of the groin is from a cut that happens in the extended sifach over the belly in the area of the mirach of the belly.
Therefore the intestines descend from that rupture to one testicle. And this rupture is either from a tear, or an extension of it.
And these two kinds happen from many causes. Or by percussion. Or by leaping. Or with shouting. Or by elevating a heavy thing, and similar things.
And the sign, when it is from the extension of the siphon. There is that which happens gradually over a long time, and does not happen suddenly: and it is an abscess equal toward the depth: because, that siphon compresses the intestine.
And the sign of it when it is from a section of sifach, because it causes a violent pain to happen suddenly from its beginning; and it is a different kind of abscess, appearing under the skin nearby: and this is because of the exit of the intestine, and its course to the exterior sifach.
And sometimes it emerges with a tumor of the intestine: and this is called Intestinal Ramp, and tumor: and sometimes it is done with windiness.
And sometimes stool runs into the intestine and is retained there: and this is harmful to the sick person because it causes bad pain, especially when pressed upon.
And in the treatment of these kinds of illnesses with iron there is fear: therefore it ought to be guarded against.
And the way of operating is, that you order the sick person to bring the intestine back with his hand into the cavity of its own body, if he obeys the return. Then, he is laid back on his neck between your hands, and his legs are raised.
Then extend the skin that follows the skin above, and cut the skin of the testicles entirely along its length. Then fasten hooks into the two lips of the incision, according to how much you need to open the incision with them: and let the incision be according to the quantity, as far as possible, that an egg may come out of it.
Then scrape the skin of the testicle and that which is under the skin of the testicles until when it reveals the white, hard sifach from every part: then introduce your index finger into that which follows the testicle, into that which is between the white sifach, which is under the skin, and between the second sifach, and separate with it the adhesion which is in the posterior part of the scrotum.
Then move with the right hand into the interior of the skin of the testicles: and with this extend the white sheet upwards, with the left hand: and elevate the testicle with the sheet to the part of the incision: and command the assistant to extend the testicle upwards: and you alone loosen the adhesion which is behind, complete solution.
And feel with your fingers so that there is nothing from the intestine entwined in the hard white cloth. If you find anything from that [place/object], then drive it toward the lower abdomen.
Then take a needle which has a thick thread, made of ten threads; and introduce it into the last sifach, that is, the tunic which is under the skin of the testicles following the incision: then cut the ends which protrude from the thread until there are four threads. Then compose some of them over some with a figure in the shape of a cross: and bind them with alsifach, which we have spoken of, because it is under the skin ligament of the testicles, strong, until nothing from the vessels, which nourish them, can send anything to them: so that an abscess may not happen there. And place again the second ligament beyond the first ligament, distant from it by less than two fingers. And after these two ligaments are released from the sifach, which is under the skin of the testicles, the quantity of the size of a finger: and cut the rest entirely according to the roundness, and take out the testicle from it.
Then cut dryly in the lower part of the skin of the testicles, from which blood and pus run, as we have narrated before.
Then administer wool soaked in oil, and place it in the wound: and administer the ligature that we have narrated. And sometimes when the white sifach which we have spoken of is cauterized after its incision, for fear of bleeding. It happens often indeed. And let go of the ligament until it falls of itself.
If, however, his case is delayed, then it shall be cauterized with hot water: and place upon it that which we have said, until it falls.
Then care for the wound with the rest of wound care, until it heals.
Chapter LXIV.
On the wind-blown lance.
In this treatise I did not find anyone who dared to treat it with iron.
And first they said that it is done as we narrated in the branch which is with the Vine. That is, as you have cut the vessels after the incision, easily carry them downwards; then dry them in the middle and take care lastly with that which opens them until the vessels fall; and take care of the wound, according to what we said, until it is healed.
Chapter LXVII.
On the rupture that occurs in the groin.
When a rupture occurs in the groin, as we have said, and the place is prominent, and nothing from the intestines descends to the testicles: and if it does descend, it is small, and returns within every hour; but if time is prolonged with it, a fissure is added in the scrotum, until the intestines descend into the scrotum.
And this happens from extension of the sifach, which is in the groins, as we said. Which is because it extends sifach: then it softens, and the place becomes suppurated and prominent.
And its treatment is with a cautery, as his narrative has preceded.
And sometimes it is cured with a knife, according to this manner: and let the sick person lie on his back between your hands. Then dry the place of the rupture and abscesses and prominent one, with a cut across its width, according to the quantity of three fingers.
Then tighten the alsifak and that which is under the skin until when it reveals white sifach, which is under the skin that follows it.
Then take a ray and place it on the prominent place from the siphon, and press it into the depth of the belly.
Then sew two prominent places above the end of the radius with a sifach, and connect one with the other with suture: then draw out the end of the radius.
And do not cut the intestines at all, nor pull out the bowel, nor anything else, as I have told you in the cure of a ruptured intestine.
Then comes the care for wounds. When therefore you cut the thread, cleanse it, and restore the wound until it consolidates.
If a hernia is contracted and no prominence is added.
And a cautery is better in this place; because it is closer to safety.
Chapter LXVIII.
On softening the skin of the testicles.
Often the skin of the testicles is softened in some men, because of which the flesh that is between them is also softened, and its appearance is disfigured.
Therefore it is necessary for one who is asked about the cure of this, that he make the sick person lie upon his back, and cut the entire skin which has been softened over the natural skin.
Then bring together the two lips with a suture: and if you wish, sew in the first place the excess of softened skin with three sutures, or four, and make a firm suture.
Then cut between the sutures.
Then care for the healing of the remaining wounds, until they are healed, and the threads fall away.
Chapter LXIX.
On Castration.
Know that castration is forbidden in our law: and for this reason it would be permissible for me not to remember it in my book; and it is not necessary to remember it except for two ways.
One of these is that a physician should know it when he is questioned about it and know how to treat someone who has it.
And another way is that we often need the castration of certain animals for our benefit, such as rams, goats, and cats, and similar animals.
Therefore I say that castration is done in two ways. Or by rubbing. Either through incision, and section.
In that which is done by attrition, the way of operation is: that we make the animal sit in hot water until its testicles become soft and pliable and hang down; then rub them with your hand until they dissolve and do not appear under touch.
In castration by incision and section, it is necessary that you hold the animal and press the skin of its testicles with your left hand.
Then tie the suspensory ligament, and dry above every ovum with one incision, until when two ova proceed: then cut them after having scraped both, and do not put anything above them from sifak except fine sifac which is over vessels.
And this method of castration is better when it is done by rubbing; because rubbing perhaps leaves something of the testicles, wherefore the animal desires copulation.
Then care for the wound until it heals.
Chapter LXX.
On the care of a hermaphrodite in two ways.
A hermaphrodite is from a man according to two ways. One of them is because it appears in what follows the space, or in the skin of the testicles, in what is between the two testicles, a figure like the vulva of a woman, in which there are hairs: and sometimes urine flows from what is in the skin of the testicles.
In women there is one type, and it is the vulva of a woman above the comb like the testicles of a man are small, altogether prominent to the outside: of which one is like the penis of a man, and two like two testicles.
And the treatment of three kinds, two from men, and one from women, is: because it is necessary that you cut the added flesh until the impression of it escapes. Then care for the wounds after these have been treated.
In this second kind of men, from which urine exits, which is in the skin of the testicles, there is no operation, nor is there any cure at all.
Chapter LXXI.
On the incision of the labia and the prominent flesh in all women.
Perhaps something is added to the natural thing, until its appearance becomes foul and ugly: and sometimes it is magnified in some women so much that it expands like in men, and even comes to copulation.
Therefore, you ought to hold the superfluity of the tendon with your hand, or with a hook, and cut: and do not go too deep in the cutting, especially in the depth of the root: so that there may not be an outflow of blood.
Then care for the wound itself, until it is healed.
The flesh that arises is the flesh that is born in the opening of the womb until it fills it; and perhaps it comes out to the outside according to the likeness of a tail; and because of this some first call the ailment caudal. Therefore it is necessary that we should cut into it as we cut into a tumor, and treat it until it is healed.
On the treatment of Alratica, which is the woman's vulva not perforated.
Chapter Seventy-Second.
The vulva of a woman should be unperforated, or have a small opening. And it is either natural or accidental.
An accidental condition is from a disease that precedes it; and this itself comes from flesh added which is born, or from sifach fine, or coarse; and it happens either in the deep of the uterus, or on its sides, or in its upper part, or lower part; and it prevents coition and impregnation, and childbirth, and perhaps it prevents menstruation.
And knowledge of it is obtained by the look of an obstetrician, if the swelling is apparent nearby.
If it is not apparent, then seek it with your fingers, or with a rod.
If however the abscess is from a subtle stitch and it is close to the two lips: then hasten, and break it open.
And it is, that you put over two lips little balls.
Then place your indexes of both hands together, and let the woman lie on her back with her legs spread. Then stretch the two lips with fortitude until the subtle fissure ruptures and the opening becomes apparent.
Then take wool and soak it in oil, and put it over the place: and let the woman every day apply it so that the place does not harden again with other matter.
If, however, the sifach is large and thick, then it is necessary to cut it with a spatula wide like a myrtle leaf.
If the abscess is born of flesh, then suspend it with hooks and cut into it; and let medicinal substances be with you that will staunch the flow of blood without biting, such as acacia, and dragon's blood, and thus the coagulated albumen of egg white.
Then administer a lead pipe, wide, so that the wound does not close quickly, hold it for several days; and administer licorice from dry linen, then cure with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
And sometimes there is a birth in the womb of another's flesh: it is therefore necessary that they be cut according to this manner: as long as it is not a cancerous abscess for an abscesses cancerous that is in the womb, do not oppose with iron at all.
Chapter Seventy-Third.
On the treatment of hemorrhoids, Athehelil, and red boils in women.
Hemorrhoids indeed are openings of veins, so that blood flows from them continuously in large amounts.
When hemorrhoids become severe, they become tethers: and I have said in the division of their kinds, and their signs; and I will say here what cures they receive, and which they do not.
Therefore I say that hemorrhoids which are in the opening of the womb and do not appear to the sense, then there is no cure for them with iron.
Those things which are in the opening of the womb over which sense falls, those are cured.
Therefore, you ought to introduce the woman into a cold house. Then extend the athehel with forceps, or with a rough cloth, and cut it from its roots.
Then pulverize upon them at the flow of blood from one powder of incisions without corruption: such as acacia, and dragon's blood, and so on, and similar things to these.
Then he drives his feet against the wall for an hour. Afterward, lay it in cold water.
If the bleeding persisted, then have her sit in a decoction of sumac, and rind of pomegranate, and galls, and similar things.
Then take barley flour, and mix it with honey and vinegar, and make a plaster with it over his back.
If therefore blood is cut with it, it is good: and if not, make to adhere to the back windy, and nipples, without scarification.
Then take wool and submerge it in the juice of tarathis, or the juice of arnoglossa, or the juice of alaulat branches, that is, aglantar, Pontic wine, and make it adhere to the lion.
When therefore an abscess rests, then care for the place with ointment until it heals.
Albarth, however, is like the heads of Alhaasse, and it has a rough appearance.
Therefore, you must cut what appears from it, according to what I said before; and take care of it until it heals.
Chapter Seventy-Fourth.
Of the perforation of the uterus which happens in a woman's womb.
Many kinds of abscesses occur in the womb, such as cancer, and stony abscess, and ulcers, and bedsores, and scabies, that is, herpes, aestiomemus, and fistulas, and hemorrhoids, and ragadiae, and thelil, and hot abscess.
And I have indeed already said all these ailments and their kinds, and their signs, and their cures in the division: it is necessary that I say in this treatise an abscess that happens in the womb when it is from abscesses that collect pus, and you wish to know how it is opened with a knife.
Therefore, you ought to consider. Then if the pain of an abscess in its beginning is acute with percussion of it, and flame, and fever: and you see it redden; if sensation falls upon it, then do not hasten with its perforation: and care for it with that which helps super maturation, until when it rests the sharpness of accidents.
Then it is necessary that you make the woman sit upon a seat which has two seats, and recline, or lean against her back, and bring her feet to the lower part of the belly: and let her hips be spread out, and put her arms under her knees, and bind each with a suitable bandage.
Then let the midwife sit on the right side and administer the instrument that opens the uterine orifice.
And it is necessary when you wish to administer this instrument, that you set the peg with a sound depth into the uterus of the woman; so that what enters from the instrument may not be more than the depth of the uterus, and damage the weak one.
If the instrument is more than deep, then it is necessary to place small pads on the roof of the deep matrix, so that the entrance of the instrument into the whole depth of the matrix is prevented: and it is necessary to place a whirl, which runs in the instrument, from the upper side; and that it may hold the instrument, and that the minister may revolve the whirl until the deep matrix is opened.
When therefore it appears that the fistulous opening is soft and smooth when touched by the hand, then it is necessary to cut it at its head with a wide spatula.
When the pus has been evacuated, then it is necessary to place a moistened linen cloth into the wound, submerged in rose oil: or in green oil, which has some astringency: and place the linen cloth, and remove the incision deep in the matrix.
And place externally over the womb, and over the wool comb submerged in water, in which mallows have been boiled.
Then it is cared for after two days with an ointment that is suitable for it, until it heals.
And sometimes the womb and wound are washed so that it is douched with water and honey; then with water in which lily root or long birthwort has been boiled; then return to ointment.
If a wound has been concealed within the womb, then it is necessary to refrain from curing it with iron, rather care for it with what we have said in the division.
Chapter LXXI.
Doctrine of Obstetrics, how embryos are treated who are born not in a natural form.
It is necessary for an obstetrician to know first of all the figure of a natural birth.
From the signs therefore it is, that when you see a woman compressing to the lower parts, and desires to smell the air, and is lifted upon her which is above her from childbirth, and hastes the exit of the embryo: then know that this birth is according to the figure natural, and is the exit of it upon its head, and secondary with it: or suspended, or hanging from the navel.
When therefore you see these signs, then it is necessary that the belly be compressed, so that the embryo descends quickly.
For when he lowers it over his head, the second one descends with him, and it is purified from those superfluous things by a good cleansing.
But that which is beyond this birth is unnatural, blameworthy.
For sometimes the fetus emerges on its feet, and its body comes out before its head, and its feet, and one of its hands, or one of its feet; or it emerges with its head and hands; or its belly; and perhaps it turns over on its neck, and similar remarkable figures.
It is therefore necessary for the midwife to be prudent, subtle in all these things, and to guard herself from error and neglect: and I am showing every figure of them, and how the mind is in each one, and it is signified by this, and stands upon it.
When the fetus emerges from its head during its natural exit, and it becomes forceful upon the woman, and labor becomes difficult for her, and you see that its strength is already weakened: then make her sit on a seat, or let women hold her; and vapors of willow bark boiled with moist oils.
Then the midwife puts a small spatula between her fingers, and finds with it the second one, and cuts it with her nail until it runs what is in it of moisture: and presses the woman's belly until the fetus descends.
If it does not descend, then a woman should be given an enema of linseed and sesame oil.
Then direct him after the enema, that he be compressed: and make him sneeze with a  paste, and hold his mouth and nose for one hour. For the fetus will immediately emerge.
If two hands of the fetus emerge first, it is necessary to join them so that you may gradually reduce them with ease. If it does not obey on entry, then place the woman over a seat and elevate her feet upward; then strike the seat against the ground; and the woman must be held so that she does not fall during the concussion.
If therefore the hands do not come out, and the fetus dies: then cut them, and extract the residue of the fetus: and bind the hand of it with a cloth, and extract it with equality: for it will itself come out.
The exit of the embryo onto its feet. When the fetus emerges onto its feet, then it is necessary to push them upwards; then turn the fetus gradually; afterwards level it out.
Then he should take one of his feet and extend it gradually.
When they go out as far as the hips, then command him to press it down and have him sneeze with a concoction; for he will come out if God wills.
And if it does not emerge as we have narrated, then bring back the fetus gradually until you place it according to its natural form. For he will then go out by an easy passage.
If this is forbidden with all that we have recounted: then take from the pulp of aloe, and sesame oil, and gum dissolved: and pound the whole in a mortar with good percussion; then put it on the wound of a woman's belly and lower abdomen. Then make her sit in lukewarm water until she reaches the hypochondria.
When therefore you see that the lower parts are now warmed, then make for him a sif of myrrh and lay it upon him. When therefore one holds the sieve one hour, then let him make it sit upon the seat. Then make him sneeze: and grasp his mouth and nose: and press the lower part of the belly with easy compression. For the fever will immediately leave.
The exit of the fetus over the knees and hands. When it emerges in this form, then ingenuity lies in introducing his hands little by little. Then the woman is laid on her back, and her feet hang down; and she lies on a bed, and folds her hands. Then press above his belly gradually. Then extract what is under his feet from the dustbins, until it is prepared on the lower part. If the fetus does not emerge, then take its feet and move them with a vigorous motion. Then press that which is above the hypogastrium gradually, until the fetus ascends to the upper parts. Then he inserts his hands and gently pushes in the fetus; and instructs the woman to contract until the fetus emerges.
The fetus hangs laterally from the hands. He will set the hand. If he cannot reduce it, then raise the woman, and make her walk.
If he cannot walk, then make him lie on a bed and shake his legs with a strong concussion.
Then apply the liniment which you have prepared from mucilages.
If she receives it in childbirth, then make her sit on a stool. Then study in the reduction of the hand, and equal fetus according to the natural form: and make her sneeze, and command her to compress herself, until it comes out.
The fetus emerges from its mother's womb with its arms outstretched and its face toward its mother's back. The midwife must grasp the fetus with her hand, then gradually turn it and make it even with ease.
When therefore it is made equal, then place upon its surface what we have recounted from oil and mucus.
Then direct him to be moved gradually. When therefore you have done that, then make her sit upon a seat, and command her to incline to the right side. Then order him to compress it: and make him sneeze with the condiment.
The prepared fetus is placed on its side. When it descends to the opening of the womb in this way with the second instrument, then cut the second instrument with your finger until it is evacuated from moisture.
If therefore it is suspended over the face of the fetus, and its neck, then cut it from the navel: so that it may not be retained, and die.
Then drive the fetus inward until you place it as is necessary: and command the woman to compress herself several times; for he will himself emerge by the light exit.
The exit of twins, or of many fetuses. Know that twins often are born, and sometimes three, and four. Nevertheless, this is foreign and rarely happens.
There are five things outside of nature, and they do not live at all: and it contains them, even if they multiply, according to one: and they are separated by a siphon, distinguishing between them the ligament in the navel of each of them.
And sometimes they emerge like one fetus from the natural exit, easily; and they emerge through the praiseworthy exit, as we have recounted.
If some of these things should prove difficult during childbirth, and they come to that which we said from unnatural figures, then care for them as we have narrated: and let the midwife be subtle, and do what she does with ease and delay, and devise according to every figure with that which is prepared for her from skillful guidance to safety.
A reminder of what is formed in the womb from embryos and miscarriages.
Sometimes one, two, three, four, five, six, seven, and more than ten are formed in the womb: and it has now been verified to me that a woman, aborting, emitted seven, and another fifteen, all formed.
What has been done by the measurement of the glorious wise man.
Therefore, this whole thing ought to be in the science of midwifery. Perhaps it will come upon that one similar to this: let him therefore oppose it with what is fitting.
Chapter LXXVI.
On the extraction of a dead fetus.
When you treat a dead fetus with what I said in the division of medicine, and it does not come out, and you are forced to use an operation with your hand, then it is necessary for you to consider.
If therefore the woman is of sound virtue, and is not with any of those accidents, for which destruction fears her: then it ought that the woman be laid upon the bed, upon her back, and let her head be inclined downwards, and her legs elevated: and let the minister hold her by two parts, or be tied to the bed, so that her body may not be pulled out during the extraction of the fetus.
Then moisten the womb's bone with wet oils together with althea mucus and fennel seeds and flaxseed.
Then let the midwife anoint her hand with these oils and ointments: then let her insert it into the womb with ease. Then he should seek out a place where he can insert hooks from the fetus and suitable locations for it.
Consider therefore then, if the fetus is prepared to descend over its head: then fix hooks in its eyes, or in its neck, or in its mouth, or in its palate, or under its beard, or in its clavicle, or in places near the ribs, and under the hypogastrium.
If the descent is upon his feet, then in the hip bone and middle ribs of him.
And it is necessary that he hold the hook with his right hand and place its curve into that which is between the fingers of his left hand and introduce it with his hand easily and fix the hook in one of those places which we have said, until it comes to the empty thing.
Then he places another hook, or a third one if needed, opposite it, so that the contraction is equal and does not lean to one side.
Then it shall be extended with an equal extension, and let the extension not be according to straightness alone, but rather moved to every part, so that the exit of it may be relieved; as is done with a tooth when it is being eradicated.
And it is necessary that in what is between them, it be softened extension. If a part is retained from that, then it is necessary for the midwife to anoint some fingers with oil and insert them from the side, so that she may turn what is retained with them.
If therefore a part of the fetus comes out, then let the hooks be changed to another place which is higher: do this until the whole fetus emerges.
If therefore the hand emerges before other things, and it is not possible to reduce it because of its constriction: then it is necessary that a cloth be wrapped over it. Then remove it until it comes out entirely, cut it either at the joint of the foot or at the elbow: and thus you ought to do with your other hand and both feet.
If, however, the head of the fetus is large, or there is a constriction at its exit, or there is water collected in its head: then it is necessary that he insert between the fingers the Spatumile spinosum, and find it with the head, and extract the water: or break it with an instrument called Almisdac: whose form comes with the forms of the other instruments in the chapter which is after this.
Similarly, let him do if the fetus has a large head, then it is necessary that he cut the afterbirth; or take him out with forceps, as we have said. Then pull out the bones with forceps.
If it protrudes from the head and is drawn together at the fork, then it should be held until the moisture in the chest is expelled.
If it is not complicated, nor does it submit to an exit: then cut off the head piecemeal, according to what is possible for you.
If a boil or dropsy is in the lower abdomen, then it should be pierced until the moisture flows out of it.
If however the fetus is on its feet, then its extraction is easy, and its direction to the maternal orifice is light.
If it is bound near the abdomen or chest: then it is necessary that you extract it with the cloth which you have wrapped over your hand: and bind the abdomen, or chest, until what is in them heals.
If the remaining limbs are removed and the head returns, then the midwife will insert her left hand. If therefore the maternal opening is open, then he shall insert his hand into the depths of the womb, and with it seek the head, and draw it with his fingers up to the maternal opening; then he shall insert one hook or two hooks into it, and draw it with that.
If the mother's bone is constricted, because of a hot abscess that happens to it; then it should not be labored over, rather it should administer an effusion of humid and fatty things; and apply plasters; and sit in waters which soften and moisten.
If the fetus is on its side, then it is possible for it to be made equal; then that which we said regarding a living fetus should be administered.
If that is not possible, then it shall be cut in pieces and extracted.
And it is necessary that you do not let anything out of the second bowel at all.
When therefore you have completed your operation, then administer what we said about hot abscesses that occur in wombs.
If bleeding occurs, then let it descend into styptic water and administer the remaining treatment.
And I myself have seen a woman who had a dead fetus in her womb; then she became pregnant again over it, and afterwards another fetus died as well. Therefore, after a long time an abscess occurred to him in his navel, and it swelled until it opened; and he began to discharge pus.
I was therefore called upon to treat him, and I treated him for a long time: and the wound did not heal.
Therefore, I placed over it some strong drawing plasters: and out of the place bone came. Then after several days another bone came out. I was therefore amazed that there is no mouth in the belly.
I thought therefore, because they were from the bones of a dead fetus.
Therefore I investigated the wound, and extracted many bones, a woman being in better dispositions of her own. And now she has lived such as this for a long time, and sends out a little pus from the place.
And I did not set this foreign thing down, unless because there is science in it, and help for that which the physician-craftsman administers with his hand from healing.
Chapter LXXVII.
On the forms of instruments necessary in extracting a fetus.
The form of dizziness when the womb opens.
Here is the shape of the  Strictor, which books are equated to, which have two whorls on both ends of two pieces of wood. However, this vertigo ought to be a more refined constriction, and it should be made, either from ebony or from boxwood.
And let the width of the whole piece of wood be like two fingers; and the margin of each of both be like one finger, and the length of each of them is a palm and half; and in the middle of the two pieces of wood are two additions of the same kind of wood, which are already fastened on both sides, whose length is half a palm and a little more; and the width of both is like two fingers, or a little more.
And these two additions are put into the opening of the womb, so that it may open with them when you revolve two whorls.
Another form similar to this, more subtle and lighter.
Make it out of ebony wood, or boxwood, according to the likeness of forceps. Nevertheless at the end of it there should be two additions, as you see here above: and the length of the whole addition from them is like a palm: and the width of both is, two fingers.
When therefore you wish to open the womb with it, then make the woman sit upon a bed, with her feet hanging down, and that which is between her legs open. Then introduce these two adjuncts joined into the uterus: and you are holding the lower extremity of the instrument between his hips. Then open your hand with an instrument, as you do with forceps, evenly according to the quantity that you wish from the opening of the mouth of the womb, until the midwife does what she wants.
Form of Vertigo of another kind which ancients called Form of the Driving Force by which the fetus is driven.
The form of an Almisdac, which is used to crush the large head: and it is similar to a forceps, which has teeth at the end, as you see.
And sometimes it is made oblong, like forceps according to this form, as you see, which has teeth like saw teeth, with which one cuts and crushes.
The form of a wound having one spine.
The shape of a two-spined wrench.
The shape of a spatula has two extremities for cutting off the head.
The form of two spatulas also called probes.
All these instruments are practiced, and the more their kinds multiply, and they are prepared by the craftsman, the faster it is to operation, and the higher among men, because of their quantity.
Therefore do not disregard from them an instrument, that it may be prepared with you. It is indeed an inexcusable necessity of theirs.
Chapter LXXVIII.
On the extraction of the Second (tooth) .
When Secondina is retained at childbirth, then it is necessary that you instruct the weak one so that she may retain her breath. Then make him sneeze with the condiment: and hold your hand over his mouth, and his nostrils. If therefore it comes out through this, it is good.
If not: then take an olla, and pierce a hole in the bottom of it: and put in it herbs that open the womb, such as  mallow, rue, anethum, chamomile, sage, cassia twig, and centaury: take all these herbs, or some of them, and submerge them with water, and put them on the fire.
Then put a reed bandage over the opening of the pot's handle, and the other end into the mothers womb: and hold it until steam reaches the concavity of the womb.
Then make her sneeze, as we said. For Secondinus will go forth with ease.
If it remains after this treatment and does not come out: then order the Midwife to submerge her hand in sesame oil, or thick alum. Then he inserts into the wound and seeks with it the second layer. When therefore he has found it, then let him take it and extend it gradually.
If it is attached deep within the womb, then he shall insert his hand, according to what we have narrated, until he finds the Secundina and extracts it gradually along a straight line, so that the womb does not fall upon violent pulling: indeed, it ought to be moved with ease to the right and left sides; then add to the quantity of the traction. For it then obeys, and is preserved, and departs from adherence.
If the maternal bone is contracted, as we have already narrated: care for it with sneezing and from the treatment of a pot with herbs.
If it does not come out with all that we have narrated, then beware of tiring it beyond measure in the repetition of extraction.
But it is necessary that what exits from the mouth of the womb be brought to the hip of the woman. Then administer an enema with ointment of four drugs. For indeed it will rot it after days, and it will dissolve, and it will come out.
But when it rots, a bad smell arises from it to the stomach and head, and harms the weak. Therefore, it is necessary that appropriate fumigations be administered to it.
Some of the ancients already experienced fumigation with nasturtium and dried figs.
Form of the instrument for fumigating a woman during the retention of the Second and menstrual flow.
Make a wick like incense from glass or wax: and place the wide end over the fire: and let fumigation be placed over prunes: hold it until the fumigation is consumed: and repeat another.
Chapter LXXIX.
On the treatment of an unperforated anus.
Many boys are born, and their anus is not perforated, whom already subtil sifac has cured. Therefore, it is necessary for the midwife to perforate that sifac with her finger; and if not, she should perforate it with a sharp Spatula; and she should beware of the laceration, so that it does not touch it.
Then place upon it wool soaked in wine and oil. Then care for it with ointment until it heals.
If you fear that it should be filled, then put a lead pipe into the opening for many days, and remove it when the child wishes to heal.
And now it happens that a suppuration of the anus also occurs from the consolidation of a wound, or abscess. Therefore it is necessary that this consolidation be cut, then it should be treated with what we have said regarding treatment and lead.
Chapter LXXX.
On the treatment of fistulas that occur in the lower part.
Fistulas which occur in the lower part are a Knot and Lumps that occur near the anus of the opening: or in the space of one part: and it is one fistula, or several.
When therefore that Knot becomes obsolete, it is opened and a watery moisture, white, or a subtle pus runs out of it.
And sometimes these are penetrating fistulas. Therefore, they are recognized as penetrating through that which comes out from defecation and windiness, when the sick person uses defecation: and perhaps it comes out of them worms.
And there are among these fistulas when they are in penetrating space up to the bladder, and up to the male organ's passage. And some of them penetrate as far as the hip joint, and as far as the tail bone.
From this it is known that a fistula penetrating to the anus, even if not penetrating, is so that you introduce your index finger into the anus: and introduce a thin probe into the fistula from bronze or iron; when there is no twist.
If there is a twisting in it, then introduce into it a thin lead probe, or a shaft made of horsehair, until you feel the probe, or the shaft in your finger.
If you do not feel it completely, and nothing comes out of the hole by discharge, nor windiness, nor worm as I said: then know that it is not penetrating: therefore hasten to the cure.
And when a fistula is penetrating to the bladder, or to the passage of urine: then its meaning is the exit of urine from it, and its prohibition so that this place may be solidified with medicines.
If it penetrates up to the joint of the hip, or to the bone of the hip: then the sign of it is that the touch reaches there, if there is no tortuosity up to the bone; and the finding of pain around the part, and that pus issues from it always; and that medicine does not avail in it, nor does it solidify with ointment.
And in all these penetrating fistulas there is no cure at all; and their treatment is labor and vanity to him, from foolish physicians, upon whom it is formed.
In that which is not penetrating and not ancient, healing is hoped for with a suture, according to what I tell you, and I have already proven it: and it is, as the sick person lies between your hands on his back: and raise his legs up to his hips, and let his hips be inclined towards his belly. Then they introduce lead, or air, if there is a twisting in the fistula, until you know where the probe arrives.
If, however, he feels her weak around the anus, then it is necessary that you introduce your index finger into the anus.
If therefore you feel in your finger that something has already penetrated itself, besides what you feel between your finger and the skin, or flesh; then certainly know that it is penetrating; do not therefore labor on it, because it is not its healing, as we have said.
They said, however, that in some people flesh heals and from the medication which is hoped to be helpful, it is as if fine cauteries are applied according to the width of the fistula, as was previously stated, and you introduce this ignited into the fistula until it reaches around the anus. Then repeat this twice, or three times, until you know that all those added and filtered meats which are like the quill of a birds feather have been burned.
Then care for it with cloths wrapped in butter, until those burned meats come out; then care for it with ointments that generate flesh and consolidate it.
If therefore it is healed, it is good.
And if not, then it is never healed with any other cure.
And if it emerges (the probe) and does not reach your finger, which is in the anus, and there is a thick layer between it and the probe, either of flesh or of cicatrix: and you see a fistula in what follows the skin surface, then cut the skin from the beginning of the fistula, and you go cutting with the probe that is in the fistula, until the incision reaches where the probe's extremity reaches, and the probe is freed and falls.
Then purify those filtered meats which are similar to the quill of a bird's feather, so that nothing remains from them.
If blood conquers you, and loosens between you and your operation so that you cut those flesh, then it is better, and of swifter help, to cauterize it with fire, or with a sharp medicine.
Because a cautery with fire gathers good properties, the burning of those added flesh tissues, and the cutting off of blood, and the drying out of moistures.
Then the wound is treated with linens wrapped in butter or crushed cabbage until it discharges pus and the charred flesh comes out into the pus.
Then care is taken of the wound once it has been consolidated with ointment that produces hard flesh; and there are medicines in which there is astringency and drying.
For when they are restored, then it is healed and you should not fear a repetition.
If however a fistula has already reached deep into the anus, and extends from the excess of the body: then introduce your finger into the anus, and investigate with it.
If therefore you feel a wound, and between you and it is a covering of silk or flesh, and it is near to penetration; then there is no operation in that, except according to the way of trust and hope.
That is, because it is administered in one of three ways: either that you cauterize it, as we have said; or that you cut it until you find its concavity so that it is possible for you to introduce a ligature and cure from close by. And do not proceed much with the cutting, so that you do not cut the ligament holding back the anus, and that it happens over the weak exit of excretion without will. Then take care of it as we said.
Perhaps it will heal: or you may lengthen the bandage, and cleanse it after drying it nearly to the anus, as we have said, either with a probe or with some other instrument having a sharp extremity. Then purify those filtered flesh that are in the incision, as far as you can with scrutiny.
Then attempt restoration of the entire wound with a section and an upper opening where it closes; and let there remain an open fistula in the inner part of the anus, and it will be lighter on the weak side.
And sometimes a fistula is treated in this way: and it is when you insert a probe into the fistula: and it is on the side of the anus near the surface of the body with the skin and edge of the anus: then take a probe with a perforated end, which is like an awl, that is, prepared leather, according to this form. And introduce into it a thread wrapped from five threads, and about it. Then introduce a probe with a thread into the fistula until you reach its cavity.
If therefore it is penetrating in the margin of the anus inwardly near, then extract the thread from that hole: so that you may introduce your finger into the anus, and draw out the end of the thread, and gather together the two ends simultaneously: and tighten both, and release it on one day, or two days: and as much as the thread sinks into the flesh, tighten it well, until those fleshes which are between the two extremities of the thread sink in, and fall: then take care of the wound, until it consolidates, and heals.
If however the opening of the fistula is not penetrating, then make it penetrate in any way that you prepare for yourself, unless it is deep within. For it is not fitting for you to do that because of the cut so that you do not cut it.
Then make with it as we have said, until it heals.
The shape of a knife for cutting into the spine where a fistula is found, whose cure is very acute, and the other part is not sharp, so that it does not cut what is not necessary, as you see.
Chapter LXXXI.
On the suture of hemorrhoids from which blood runs after incision, and care for fissures.
There are hemorrhoids in two ways. Or they are inside the anus, like pieces of charcoal or plums: or as if they were grains of a grape. And of these there are small ones and large ones: and blood flows from them always, and there is one, and there are many. And they are outside the anus and at its extremities.
However, those that are outside the anus are, for the most part, few in moisture, of which runs yellow water or little blood with a steady flow: and they are according to the color of the body.
And the cure of this which is within the anus, is that you instruct the sick person to fart, and expel it until it comes out of the anus, and appears to you white. Therefore, also suspend with hooks: or hold them with your finger. Then cut them at their roots.
If they are not held by the hooks because of their moisture and softening: then take them with a rough cloth, or with a wad made of double wool fleece, and remove them with your fingers. Then cut them, and pulverize upon them after the incision some hot powders, so that they may stand in their place as cauterization. Or cauterize them according to what preceded in the chapter on cauterization. Then care for them with the rest of the cure, until they are healed.
If the anus does not obey defecation, then a clyster should be administered to the sick person in which there is a small bite, so that it may wash what is in the anus and obey defecation quickly when the sick person expels himself.
If they are external from the anus, then their condition is light and easy; and it is as if you would take them with your fingernail or hook, and cut them. Then care for them with that which we have said, until they are healed.
And if he shuns cutting with iron, then it is necessary that you administer a suture of them according to this method. Take a thread wound up, and introduce it into the needle. Then draw the athil upward and make it penetrate with a point at its root from the other side: and involve the two extremities of the thread, in the lower part of the awl, and let them be laterally placed: and tighten the athil tightly. Then knot the thread, and draw out the point.
Do this with all aethile and release from them one: and do not let its own, so that it runs out of excess blood.
Then place a cloth soaked in rose or red oil over the anus, and place it over the anus. And command the sick person to rest. Then let it go, until it falls.
When therefore the athelia fall, treat them with ointment, and the rest of treatment, until they are healed.
On the care of fissures. Many times indeed they happen from drying of excrement and constraint of nature.
When therefore they are ancient and medicine does not avail in them, then it is necessary that you cut with points of a spatula, or with your fingernail, until they become moist, and the upper cortex which prevents them from consolidation be removed from them. Then care for them until they are consolidated as is proper.
If they do not solidify, then treat them again with greater vigor than before, until they bleed much and are scraped, and then take care of them until they heal.
On the treatment of inverted and non-inverted  Almirmar, and Athelil ulcers, and Formicae.
Chapter LXXXII.
Know that Almirmar is nothing but a round knot according to the color of the body, similar to the head of a nail: and it occurs in the whole body, especially in the lower parts of the feet, and fingers: and pain happens from them when walking.
Therefore it is necessary that what is around the nail be cut and held with forceps or a hook, and be removed from its root.
Then care for that: and if you wish cauterize it according to what preceded in its chapter.
From the armpit there is one small, rough, filtered eminence, round on the surface of the body. And many times they happen in the hands of children.
And its treatment is, that you extend athelil and cut it; or bind it with silk thread; or with hair, until it falls off. And if you wish, cauterize it according to what has been said with fire, or with a sharp medicine; and now the athelil are eradicated, both inverted and not inverted with the instrument which I shall tell you about.
A bite from an ant is also a small elevation, rough, on the surface of the body, penetrating deep down; and when it finds cold, it feels like an ant bite in it, and happens throughout the body; and most of it happens in the hands.
And its cure is, that you take a tube from the feather of a goose, or the feather of a strong vulture: and put that tube over the Formica, until it contains a circle of the tube from its parts. Then turn your hand with a probe until you cut what is around the ant's form; and place it deep in the flesh, and eradicate the ant from its root.
And if you wish, make it with a tube of air, or iron, according to this form.
Let the upper part of the tube be drawn up to a height so that the grip on the finger is lessened and its turning made easier.
And if you wish to cauterize a  Formica according to what was previously said in the cauterization of Athelil in the chapter on cauterization.
Chapter LXXXIII.
On the shapes of instruments which are administered to the urethra in affections of the anus, and the loosening of the belly, and colic.
Sometimes a clyster is made of silver, or brass, or cast or hammered copper.
And sometimes these instruments are made small and large, according to those in which they are administered.
Therefore that which is administered in the care of very young boys, let it be small; and of those whose anuses are weak, or painful, so also their testicles.
Large clyster.
The tube of it, that is, the upper part in which the uterus is tied, should be wide according to this method: and let there be a distinction so that the uterus can be tied as you see, and its lower end which goes into the anus, should be closed, that is, not open, soft, smooth, gently pulling towards fineness: and on one side of it there should be two holes, and on the other side one hole.
And let the width of the foramen be according to the thickness of the rays, and a little thicker, and let it be in which medicines are put from the bladder of an animal: or from sheepskin, which is according to the form of a small asophore: whose root is one palm.
And it shall be pierced with many holes as it is turned, and let the space that is between every hole be the thickness of a finger. Then introduce into those openings a strong thread of raw thread, which is made of ten threads, or about that many, and gather it with it the uterus like an asofratus. When therefore it is placed in it, let it be tightened at the head with a clyster between two distinguishing constrictions with strong force. Then give a clyster with it.
And this is the form of the circle of the womb here depicted, and the hole.
The quality of cleansing with this, is: that you fill the rectum, or bladder with medicine, which should be heated according to the amount tolerated by the patient: and beware that it is not too hot, or too cold.
Then the patient is laid on his back and his legs are raised to the upper part; and beneath him a cushion, that is, skin upon which men lie, or cloth, is placed.
Then anoint the extremity with oil, or with egg white, or with flaxseed mucilage, or the like. Then introduce it into the anus with ease. Then let the attendant take the rectum with both his hands and press it forcefully until everything that is in the clyster is evacuated into the intestine.
Then remove the clyster and have the patient raise his legs against the wall.
If medicine compels him to an outcome, then let him hold as much as he can. And if he sleeps with it throughout the whole night, it is more helpful.
If, however, you give him enemas in complaints of belly disease, and his death hastens, then repeat the enemas until he is healed.
If there are hemorrhoids or painful abscesses in the anus, then a fine, gentle enema should be used, like those with which children are cleaned.
And this is the form of a thin enema for when the skin is tight.
Chapter LXXXIV.
I have said in the division of the cure of wounds what was convenient for the book, and I will say here the cure of them with hand and with medicines according to complement.
Therefore I say, that wounds differ according to the manner in which they are inflicted, and according to the place upon which the wound falls.
The things therefore by which wounds are made very often are such as a throw of a stone: or incision with a sword, or knife: or striking with a spear, or wood, or arrow: and similar things from many others.
Wounds that are made according to the location of the body are such as a wound that falls upon the head, or neck, or chest, or belly, or leg; and similar ones from those limbs.
And I am relating cures of certain wounds, so that I may place that reasoning and rule over the rest of the wounds.
And I begin with simple wounds of the head: for the recollection of compositions will come at the beginning of the third chapter.
Therefore, I say. When a simple wound occurs on the head, and there is no bone fracture: consider then, if it is from a stone throw, or something similar, and it has only broken the skin: and the wound is large, and you fear an outcome of hot suppuration: then make haste, and perform cephalic phlebotomy immediately: and do not delay this: and draw out blood according to the quantity of its power: if nothing forbids phlebotomy, especially if the blood that comes out of the wound is little. And let the incision be made on the opposite side from the wound.
And let the incision be made on the opposite side from the wound. And let the sick one beware of repletion, and wine, and heavy foods.
If something prevents reduction, then it should be lessened from food, and it should not approach meats, nor wine.
And put over the wound if it happens to him a hot abscess, apply a decoction infused in rose oil alone, or with wine in which there is astringency.
If you are sure about the hot abscess, then place on the wound, if it is recent with its own blood, and has not yet changed its air, the powder which I shall tell you a little later: and if it has already changed its air, then place upon it some ointment until it heals.
If, however, the wound is large, or is from a sword cut, and similar: and its lips do not come together by means of pins, then gather them together with suture, according to what I will say about wounds of the abdomen regarding their sutures.
If now the wound is already discovered from the bone, and hangs, and there is no retention of it, except in a small suspensory: then cut into it. Then care for the wound with medicines, and restore in it the hard flesh in the place of skin, and order the sick person to be fed with foods which are thick, such as heads, and cooked wheat, until it is like porridge; and similar things.
If you find in the skin rotting, and it does not adhere to the bone: then cut out the whole rotten part, and throw it away; then care for it.
If in these wounds there is an artery or vein from which blood flows and it is not cut off by medicines, then investigate above the artery. If therefore you find it not cut, then cut it with a spatula, or bind it. If necessity requires when that which we said does not help, then cauterize it until the blood is cut off.
If however these wounds are small, simple, then the matter of them is easy, light: so that you put this powder on them: because it consolidates them before they change in air.
The Method of Incarnate Powder. Take one part of olibanum, two parts of dragon's blood, and three parts of unburnt chalk: mix all together well and sift it.  Fill the wound with this and bind it tightly with a good bandage until it adheres well to it and clots with the blood.
Then bind it above with pads and let it go, and do not loosen it while it remains so that it does not come undone. For the medicine is not removed from it until it is healed.
And sometimes it does this alone the plaster, when you do not have frankincense at hand, and dragon's blood also does the same operation in many of the large wounds, when it is convenient to be laid upon them and bound tight, and are fresh wounds with their own blood, which has not been altered by air.
If it has already been altered by some kind of change, especially when it is in the summer time, then there is no intention for this powder to be in position: therefore take care of it with what we have said, so that you put some ointments on it which are digestive; or put poultices on it made from barley flour, made with water and honey, until they emit pus. Then care for the rest of the treatment, until they are healed.
If it happens with a wound and fracture in the bone, and it is small, then remove it with the Gesti: and I will now say the cure of fractures of the head in what will come next.
On Wounds of the Neck.
If a wound occurs in the neck, there is no difference in treatment between it and a wound on the head when it is simple.
If a vein of the neck or an artery has already been cut: then if it is a nerve, there is no power in it unless it be treated with that which makes staunching vehement, such as ziniar and zegi and things similar. Because they harm the nerves and contract them.
And do not place anything cold at all upon the wound: because the substance of the nerves is cold, and its continuation is with the brain, which is the most sublime part of the body: and let it be cured by medicines gently, such as chalk washed with sweet water several times infused with oil, or with rose oil, and thyme, when it is also washed with sweet water: and other mineral stones, in this way, and moist ointments.
If however the wound is large, then administer a suture: or constrict its lips with little bits of wool. Then care for it until it heals.
If, however, the depth of the wound is great, or a sinus has occurred in the lower part of it, in which pus is already gathered, then pierce it in the more depressed place within it.
If an artery has already been cut in a wound and its blood flow has continued, then separate or dry it out, and tie it up: or cauterize it if necessity requires it.
If however the wound has occurred in the throat, or all of it, and the al-hadege have been saved: then gather together the lips of skin with suture above the tube of the throat, and do not touch the throat, but rather equalize it, and bring it back to its natural form. Then tighten it with a suitable ligature and leave it for several days. Then treat it with ointment which contains astringency and drying power, until it heals.
If the wound has already made an impression on bone, then examine it: and if there are fragments of bone in it, remove them all, or as many as you can.  And what cannot be prepared, let it alone until the wound decays, and its exit is separated and relieved.
If the wound is recent, then apply the powder I have described upon it. If, however, a bone appears in the wound and a fistula is formed, then care for it should be taken with that which is remembered in its chapter.
On a wound in the shoulder blades.
If it is a wound from a lance or knife, and you see its depth, then consider it. If therefore from a wound there issues windiness, when he gasps from it, then know that the wound is mortal.
If it is not deep and is fresh with its own blood, then you shall not put powder on it from the first hour, nor bind it tightly so that blood is not held in its depth, and returns to the heart, and kills the patient: but place ointment attractant upon it, and loosen it every day twice.
If, however, ointment is not present to you, then place in the opening of the wound old cloth, so that it may draw out that which flows from it of humidities: and make that the sick person sleep upon the wound, so that it may cure what is gathered in it.
If now three days or more have passed since the wound, and no spasm has occurred in the infirm, nor a bad tremor of the heart, nor stricture in breathing, and you see the rest of its dispositions good: then know that the wound is safe. Therefore care for it itself and with licenses, and the rest of the cure, until it is healed.
If, however, healing is excused, and it always opens then you shall know that a fistula has been made. Therefore care for it from its own chapter.
And if the wound is from a sword or knife incision, and it is simple on the surface of the chest or back: then treat it with what preceded concerning suture, if it is large; or with powder, if it is small.
If however he has already made an impression on the bone, and a fragment has fallen out of it: then inquire about the wound, and hasten to extract those fragments, if they are separated. If they are not separated, then leave them until the wound has rotted, for extraction will be made easier.
The judgment of the accidents of wounds in the remaining limbs is in the cure, as we said.
On wounds of the abdomen, and the exit of the intestines, and their protrusion.
A rupture that happens to the belly, is great, and medium, and small.
Therefore sometimes a great quantity of intestine emerges, or a number of intestines.
Therefore, the introduction of them is made, and their suture more difficult and strenuous.
And even a small rupture can be difficult in another way. Therefore it follows that the reduction is more difficult if you do not hasten in its introduction to its place immediately; because it becomes inflated, opened, and enlarged; therefore the entrance into it becomes difficult.
Therefore, a middle disruption is better for ruptures because with it, the reduction of the intestine is not difficult, as it is difficult in these two kinds.
Know that suture of the vagina is done in four ways.
Two common sutures are used in the suture of the abdomen and in the suture of other wounds on the body; and two other sutures are proper to other wounds. Nevertheless, these are more appropriate for stitching up a wound in the abdomen: and I will tell you each one with explanation and declaration.
Therefore I say, that when a wound is small, and something comes out of it from the intestines, and it becomes difficult to reduce it: then it is because of one of two ways. Either because of the smallness of the rupture, as we have said; or because there is inflation in the intestine because of the coldness of the air.
When therefore that is so, then it is necessary that you heat it, so that you may submerge a sponge, or a piece of cloth wet in lukewarm water only. Or boil it in water with squill and cypress and saffron and embrocations with the intestine until the swelling resolves.
Sometimes it makes a wine with astringency; and it is better than water alone in resolving swelling.
When therefore you see that the swelling has already resolved, then moisten the intestine with water in which the althea or marshmallow has been boiled. For that, the entrance is eased with a small thing.
If, however, his return is excused after this treatment, then find in a small incision, with the instrument by which hemorrhoids are cut.
And the first cure is better when it is prepared than when it is cut; and there should not be compulsion to cut after necessity and lack of instrument.
Let there be a part of it that is twisted, sharp, and the other part not sharp, and the fine extremity not be with the fineness of Spatula, but rather be somewhat blunt: and it is that which resembles a spatula, as you see.
When therefore a wound is enlarged and enters the intestine, then it is necessary that there be a reduction of it according to its natural form in its own place, if it is possible above it: because it is better.
When a rupture is wide in the lower part of the abdomen: then it is necessary that you make the sick person lie on his back, and put his legs higher than his head. And if it is in the upper part of the abdomen, then place its head and chest higher than its lower parts.
And similarly if there is a rupture in one of the two parts of the belly, then put your attention and your consideration always in this, that the part in which is the wound be higher than the other part.
And thus it is necessary that you administer it in large wounds, and in medium-sized wounds.
In small wounds, place it according to what is prepared for you.
Then have a devout assistant present between your hands who will hold the entire rupture with his hand: and bring together its lips. Then uncover from it he who follows the suture something after something: and this is the manner of a common suture of one.
And it is, that you take a needle, or a number of needles according to the quantity of the width of the wound. Then let go from the end of the rupture the quantity of the ring finger, that is, of the doctor: and fix one point with an awl, besides which you insert a thread into both extremities of the skin, with both extremities of the fascia that is under the skin from the interior, until you make it penetrate from the other side, and you have already gathered together two extremities of the skin, and two extremities of the fascia: and there are four rows.
Then bind with a double thread around the area of the needle several times from two sides simultaneously, until the two lips of the wound are joined together by a decent gathering.
Then let go of the thickness of a finger also, and set in them another point; afterward bind it with thread, as you did with the first point. Therefore, do not cease doing this with it as you need from the awl until you complete the constriction of the whole wound.
And let the awls be of medium thickness between coarseness and fineness; for fine awls cut flesh very quickly, and coarse ones are difficult to enter the skin: therefore they ought to be of medium thickness and fineness.
And it is not necessary that the needle be inserted in both extremities of the adjacent skin too much; so that flesh does not quickly cut, and the wound opens before its consolidation, and also do not lengthen with suture, so that the wound is not hindered from consolidation.
Then cut the extremity of the needle so that it does not harm the sick person in their sleep; and place small pieces of cloth on every side of them, which hold the extremities of the needles; and leave them until you know that the wound is already healed.
And this kind of suture with needles is more suitable for small wounds. Since one or two or about that many needles suffice for sewing them up.
The second kind of suture is common, as it joins the extremities with four sutures: two extremities of skin and two extremities of fascia in one vice, with a needle in which sits an equal thread in fineness and thickness.
Then when you have penetrated with the point these four extremities, withdraw the point from the part where you began, so that the thread may fall from the upper part of the wound: that it may be a suture, as a suture of cloth which is tightened on cloth, and place between each suture the quantity of thickness less than the smallest finger.
These stitches men know.
And with this suture I treated the wound which had happened to a man in his belly, who had been wounded with a knife, and the wound was a rupture one palm wide: and about two palms of intestine had come out from the middle intestine, and there was a rupture in the middle of the belly.
Therefore, after it stood outside the wound for twenty-four hours, I reduced it.
Therefore, the wound was consolidated in a space which was about fifteen days: and I took care of it, and it healed: and he lived after it for many years, unchanged in his habits.
And the physicians had judged that it would not be healed.
And it was a miracle that I did not treat it with ointment. Because I was in a place where no ointment was found, nor anything from medicines. Therefore I placed upon the old ulcer a poultice twice a day, and diligently washed it with water of honey until it was healed.
I shall speak of two kinds of suture proper according to the account of discourse.
And it is, as you begin with a sharp suture: and insert the point from the outer to the inner part.
When therefore a needle penetrates the skin and entering the flesh according to the length and width of the whole body: let go the extremity of the sifac on the side in which you introduced the needle, and make it penetrate into the other extremity from the inner part to the outer in the other extremity.
When therefore you have made it penetrate, then make it penetrate according to this in the same extremity miraculous from the outside part to the inside: and let go the extremity sifac, which is in this side, and make it penetrate the point into the other extremity from the inside part to the outside: and make it penetrate with penetration your own that you make it penetrate in sifac extremity miraculous, which is in its part, until it penetrates into the whole of it.
Then begin also from this same side, and sew it with the extremity which is from the opposite side, and extract the point from the skin which has pierced through.
Then bring it back into that skin, and suture its end, which is on the other side, with this end marvelously; and draw it out of the skin which is on its part, and make one of them, and the other; and make one after another, until its entire outside according to this example.
And the sum of this suture is that you sew with sutures of softer skin than these skins; so that you stitch one side of one extremity of the skin and let it go on the other side until you complete it.
The second kind of suture, which Gale called his own.
This speech of his is narrated so that he would imitate it according to the likeness with which some physicians do this, that they gather all the part to its own part fitting by nature: and they apply the end sifac to its other end, and the end mirach to its other end. And this is done according to what I tell you.
It is necessary that you place a pointed knife at the edge of the limb near you from the outside part, and make it penetrate to the inside there: and let go of the two ends of the knife. Then turn the point and make it penetrate from the outer part to the inner in two extremities, that is, on both sides.
Then turn both sides, even make them penetrate from the inside to the outside, into two extreme parts, which are on the opposite side.
And this method of suture is a better suture than the common one, and it is a suture whose previous explanation has been given.
This is therefore also the speech of Galen.
And some said. The suture of the abdomen should be made with two needles. That is, to introduce into both one thread: and begin with the introduction of a needle, from that which is with you, and make it penetrate to the other side, according to the suture of those who sew skins equally.
And know that when a rupture is in the middle of the belly: then its suture is more difficult than the rest of the places of the belly.
The judgment of healing a wound is the judgment of other wounds.
That is, because when you find a fresh wound with its blood, before the air alters it: and you reduce the intestine, and your own, and fit it: then place on top powdered charcoal.
If the air has already changed the wound: then put on it some ointment which is present to you, until they make pus, and the threads fall away, and the wound be closed up, and healed. Then care for it as is the treatment of other wounds until it heals.
If medicines are not present to you, then put upon it immediately, from where it begins to discharge pus, a poultice of old, rotten cloth; and change it twice a day, as I have taught you, until it is healed.
For you do not need other medication in many cases when wounds are simple.
If you fear that a wound may communicate to the principal members in the lesion, then it is necessary that you submerge soft wool in oil tempered with moderate heat, or in rose oil: and place it around the areas which are between the groins, and under the armpits.
If he feels pain, or pricks, or putrefaction in his bowels (for this often happens), then cleanse him with wine, thick, black, lukewarm: especially if the rot has already reached the intestine and made an exit passing through to its hollow cavity.
And know that that which is from the large intestine is of easier healing, and that which is from those [parts] is of more difficult healing.
The intestine called the jejunum does not accept healing from a wound that happens in it at all; and this is because of the multitude of its veins, and their size, and because of the subtlety of its body, and proximity of its nature to nerves.
If what comes from the wound is a protrusion, and you find it fresh: reduce it according to your method of reduction, by which you reduce intestines equally.
If however the space has passed and it is already green or black: then it is necessary that you tie it with a thread above the place which has darkened from it, so that there does not happen a flow of blood. In the finger are veins and arteries.
Then cut what is beyond that ligament, and put two hanging ends of thread from the lower part of the wound outside it: so that the traction of it above you may be relieved, and its extraction in case of zirbi, and the discharge of pus from the wound.
A remembrance of a wound that happened in the intestine.
When rupture occurs in the intestine and is small, it is possible that it may be restored in some people; for I have seen one who was wounded in his belly with a lance thrust, and the wound was on the right side of the stomach, and the wound was old; and a fistula was made, and wind and excrement came out of it.
Therefore I made treatment, until I was not confident in his healing: and I did not cease to treat him carefully, until he was healed, and the place was consolidated.
After I saw that the place was already consolidated, I feared for the weak part, lest an evil accident happen to the weak part in his belly; and it did not happen to him from that evil accident. And he remained in better dispositions of himself, eating and drinking, and going to the bathroom, and entered the bath, and exercised on his diets.
And now some experimenters have said that when a wound happens in the intestine and is small, then it ought to be bound up according to this method. And it is, as you receive ants of great heads. Then bring together the two lips of the wound, and place a single ant that has an open mouth over the two lips of the wound. When therefore he seizes it above and tightens its mouth, cut off its head, for it adheres, and does not separate.
Then place another ant near the first one, and do not cease doing this with an ant after each ant according to the quantity of the whole wound.
Then reduce it and sew its wound. For these heads remain attached to the intestine until the intestine is nourished and healed, so that no harm is done to the weak person entirely.
And now it is possible to draw out the intestines even with a fine thread: which has been removed from the animal's intestine, attached to it after it is inserted into the wound.
And it is, so that you receive the extremity of this thread from the gut, and it be much cleaned. Then bind a thread of fine linen wrapped at the end of it. Then introduce that thread into the point and sew with it the intestine. Then bring it back to its break.
And this way of suturing with ants and intestine is nothing but according to the way of trust and hope.
Know that a wound that is large and wide, especially if it is in one of the small intestines, then there is no remedy except subtlety, and its healing is not at all.
On the treatment of a Zochen (or possibly Zychemen) and fistulas.
