Know that every wound, or abscess when it ages and becomes old, and becomes an ulcer, and does not heal, and always emits pus, and is not cut off: then it is called universally in any member a fistula, and we call it Zuchemen.
And a fistula indeed is according to truth a knot filtered, hard, white: with which there is no pain, which has the concavity of a bird's feather: and because of this some call it Risati, that is, a feather.
And there are some hours humid, which emits pus. And perhaps moisture divides in certain hours. And sometimes this humidity is much and little, and is thick and subtle.
And I have already stated in the division, when I spoke of wounds, that every ulcer which does not heal and no flesh is born in it, this is not due to anything but one new cause.
One of these is, or because of the small amount of blood in the body; or because of its evil; or because between itself and above its lips is hard flesh, preventing the birth of flesh; or because it is full of dirt and filth; or because the ulcer on itself is putrid, and the matter that is sent to it is of bad quality; or because the medicine is unsuitable in its treatment; or because the corruption falling in the region is a kind of pestilence; or by the property of the region, as happens in Caesaraugusta: in which difficult digestion of diseases occurs, and the healing of abscesses is delayed by nature; or because it has one bone, or a number of bones.
And I have now shown all these causes and signs of them, and their cures with medicines in division: and it is necessary here that I make you know the cure of Fistula and Zuken, which is done by way of operation with the hand.
Know therefore that a fistula occurs in all members of the body. For some of these are fistulas that reach the large veins and arteries, or nerves, or fascia; or to the intestine, or bladder, or to one of the ribs; or to one of the vertebrae of the back, or to one of the joints formed by a composition: such as hands and feet; and from these there are some that reach the proximity of the main member and similar members.
Therefore, those fistulas according to this manner are from difficult diseases to heal, and do not receive treatment, and occupation with them is labor and ignorance.
If it is from a fistula of which the depth does not reach these places which I have said: and you hope and trust in its cure, and you wish to come to know it, then take a probe made of copper, or iron, if the fistula extends in a straight line, and explore it with it.
If, however, there is a tortuosity in the fistula, then investigate it with a probe of fine lead. Because lead, with the softness of its body, is raised and bent around a twist.
If a fistula has many openings, it is not possible for you to feel over it with your finger. Therefore, a clyster is administered from them through their openings.
For humidity which you use for douching it enters through other openings and flows out of them.
Then carefully inquire in whatever way is possible to you, so that you may know whether there is a bone or a nerve, and whether the fistula's concavity is distant or close: or whether it is one fistula which has many openings, and stand upon all of it with your utmost strength. With your questioning, as you interrogate the sick person, and by finding pain upon compression with your hand over the place, and by similar signs and accidental causes of the abscess.
When therefore you stand upon all these true stations, then go to cure according to confidence: and it is, as you consider then, if there is a Fistula near appearing, or in a healthy place far from the joint, or nerve, or artery, or vein, or one of the places which I have told you: then dry the Fistula according to what was said before from my narration, and remove what is in it from filtration, and corrupt flesh, which is born in its opening from flesh, or pus, and similar things: and care for it until it heals.
If however a fistula is of great depth and is straight, then it is necessary to cut it deep as far as you are able. Then cleanse it thoroughly of all its corrupted flesh.
Then administer a linen cloth rolled up in sharp medicines: and introduce it as far as the cavity of the fistula which you have grasped with iron: do this several times until the sharp medicine corrodes everything that remains in the cavity of the fistula from corruption.
Then restore it with ointments that generate healthy flesh, until it is healed.
If it does not heal through that, then cauterize it according to what we have narrated.
If therefore the cause of a fistula is bone, and you certify this to yourself; then examine that bone, and dry it according to what I have told you; if nothing prevents you from a vein, or nerve, or principal limb, as we have said.
If therefore a bone is discovered by you, and there is something of corruption and blackness in it, then scrape it until the whole corruption is removed. Then care for him with that which has become incorporated into him, until he is healed.
If it does not heal, and discharge pus as it did: then you know that you have not come to the complete eradication of corruption.
Therefore diligently cleanse it a second time, and make an exact reason of it, and its purification with your utmost fortitude.
Then restore it. If therefore it heals, it is good: and if not, then know that that corruption is in the deep part of the body, which you have not reached. There is therefore no need for you to have ingenuity in it, unless you let nature take care of it.
If a small bone is broken, and it is possible for you, draw it together with fine forceps that are suitable for it.
If there are many bones, then make a very careful traction of them all, and do not let anything escape from them with your strength.
If something from the moist things does harm to you, and there is no fear or error in cutting them: then cut those moist things and the flesh if they are there, and free the bones.
If you do not have the preparation to remove the bones from your hour, then place over the wound that it may rot; and let it be until it has rotted what is around those bones from flesh and moistures.
And guard the wound so that it does not close up. And tighten the section which you cut in your operation. If you wish, place on top of it a cloth submerged in sulfur ground with oil; or a cloth submerged in Egyptian ointment; or some ointment of viridarium.
For when those humidities decay and the bones separate from them, the removal of them is eased, and their drawing out.
If it is one large bone, such as the bone of the leg or the bone of the hip, and similar to these, and if that which has become corrupted from it is a large part: then carefully cut away the last part, until the black and corruption are removed. Then restore the wound.
If however what is in it is corrupted, and the part is large, and the corruption has already reached the marrow of the bone, then it is necessary to close it up and cut it entirely until the place where the corruption has arrived: then take care of it until it solidifies.
And I tell you a wound which happened to a man in his leg: that you may put it as an example and help to your operation.
There was a young man about thirty years of age to whom pain occurred in his leg, caused by something that moved into him from within the body, that is, from the insides, until the matter reached the leg, and it became a large abscess: and there was no external cause for him.
Therefore, time was extended with him in error of physicians, until an abscess was opened, and many materials converged with it. And after treatment had been applied, until the leg was healed and many openings were made in it which all discharged pus and bodily fluids.
Therefore, a multitude of physicians treated him for about two years, and there was no wise man in the art of hands until I was called, and I saw his leg, and the matter that flowed from those openings in great flow: also all the body of the man had become wasted, and its color was yellowish.
I therefore inserted a probe into one of the openings and it reached the mouth.
Then I examined all the openings, and found that they communicated with each other from all parts of the thigh.
I therefore proceeded and cut above one of the openings until I discovered the part of the bone: and I found it corrupted: which was already corroded and blackened, and rotten, and perforated until it had penetrated to the marrow.
Therefore I have seen what was shown to me, and prepared from a corrupted bone.
And I determined that there should not be in the bone except for that corruption, which was cut away and sawed. And I had already removed it.
Then I placed restoration of the wound with consolidating medicines over a long space. And it is not united.
Then I examined and found above the bone, second to the first detection, a continuous decay with the bone, and I sawed what appeared to me even from that decay.
Then I tried to restore it. And it has not been restored.
Therefore I examined it again and did not cease to cut the bone piece by piece, and attempt its restoration: and it was not restored until I cut out of the bone about one palm's length, and extracted it with its marrow.
Then I restored it with medicines, and it was consolidated quickly, and healed; and this repetition in his operation and section was not necessary except because of the disposition of weakness of the sick man, and the smallness of his tolerance, and my fear over him about death; for he fell into syncopes every hour from the excess of evacuation.
Therefore he was healed with complete healing: and flesh hard as bone was born in the place of the bone, and a good arrangement was made in his body, and his strength returned: and he turned back to his arrangements: and he did no harm whatsoever to him in walking.
If however the prominent bone in the place of the body has already been broken: then it is necessary that you fasten it, according to this manner: and it is: that you take a ligament, and tighten it at the extremity of the prominent bone: and order someone, who extends it upwards: and put another ligament out of wool thicker than the first ligament. Then bind it upon the flesh which is under the bone: and extend the extremity of it, so that you draw the flesh downward: and you will expose the flesh over the place which you wish to suture, so that it does not wound the flesh with the saw.
Place a piece of wood, or a board beneath the bone in a central location; for when you do this, it does not prevent the cutting of rotten flesh.
And it is necessary that there be a lock on the place corrupted for a short time, so that there may not be corruption in the concavity of the bone, and it may not appear in its appearance to the sense, and it may force to the lock itself vice another.
If the bone is broken and not protruding, rather its part continues with another part, and the break is in the middle of it, or in a part of it, then cover the flesh from all parts entirely. Then place the lower stick.
Afterwards, saw it from the first part, where there is corruption, until the saw continues on the other part: and let the saw be according to the length from the corruption a little, as we have said.
If corruption is in a joint, cut open the corrupted joint itself, and saw the bone where it continues from the other part.
If a corruption is in the continuity of two joints, then there is no remedy in it except scraping.
If corruption is in the breast of the hand, or in the breast of the foot: then the matter in it is very difficult. But truly you ought to inquire how the corruption appears to you. And having scraped it and cleaned it according to whatever arrangement you can manage, and with whatever ingenuity you correct it for you: when it does not make opposition to you Vein, or Nerve.
And know that saws and cutting instruments for cutting these bones are many, according to the location of the bones, and their preparation, and their thickness, and fineness, and size, and smallness, and hardness, and their rarity. Therefore it is necessary that you prepare instruments suitable for each kind of operation.
And know that these operations signify to you concerning the kind of instrument with which you will have need when it is with you a long study. And knowledge of the modes of this art is a testimony to the modes of diseases: for then indeed you will find by yourself that which is suitable for each illness from instruments.
And I am confirming to you at the end of this chapter the number of instruments, which you will place examples upon which you will cut, and a trial which you will try on others.

And it is not fitting unless they are rubbed with it, heads of joints, when they rot or a bone is wide and large.

On the incision of limbs, and the setting of bones.
The extremities putrefy, either from an external cause, or from an internal one.
When therefore you have treated that corruption with medicines, and the treatment does not help: and you see the corruption has affected the limb, which is not struck by anything: then it is necessary that you cut into that limb until where the corruption follows, so that the sick man may escape through it from death, or from the evil which is greater defect of the member.
And the sign of this happening is that the limb darkens until it is thought to be burned by fire or rotting after blackness: until that rot reaches what follows that limb, and spreads throughout the body.
Therefore, it is proper with the cutting of it, and similarly if the cause of corruption is from the striking of some venomous ones: such as the scorpion of the sea, and viper, and adder, and similar ones.
If therefore decay or a blow is in the extremity of the finger, then cut the finger, and do not make space for decay, that it may proceed to the rest of the hand.
And similarly if it happens in the hand, then cut it from the root of the finger, and do not leave space for corruption until it takes hold in the arm's focus.
And if it strikes him there, then cut the arm at the elbow at its joint.
If however corruption passes through and you see him about the arm: then there is death of the sick man in that. And in addition to that from healing, is the quantity of virtue.
And similarly do so with the foot, when corruption invades the finger, then cut it at one of the joints: and if it seizes in the arch of the foot, then cut the whole foot. If it ascends to the knee, then cut the leg at the knee joint.
If corruption has already come above the knee, then there is no remedy except dismissal, and to leave the sick person to death.
And the way of cutting a limb, or sewing it up is, that you tie one ligament below the place you wish to cut. And tie another ligament above the place. And let one assistant extend a ligament downwards: and let another assistant extend a ligament upwards. And you separate the flesh which is between the two ligaments, with a broad spatula, until the whole flesh is uncovered. Then cut, or suture.
And it is necessary that you place a linen cloth from all parts, and let the saw not touch the healthy place: and may there happen to the wounded one an added lesion and a hot abscess.
If a hemorrhage occurs internally while you are operating, then cauterize the place quickly: or put some powders on it that stop blood. Then return to your treatment until it is complete.
Then bind the wounded limb with a suitable bandage, and care for it until it is healed.
And I tell you what happened to a man in his foot, this same thing that I tell you.
That is because a blackness occurred in his foot, when it was burned and assimilated to the burning of fire; and that corruption first occurred in his finger, until it began the whole foot.
Therefore a man healed himself after he saw that corruption had fallen upon the limb with violence, which he found from the pain and burning of it: and it fell upon him at the joint, and he was healed.
And after a long time had passed, the same corruption befell his index finger of his hand.
He then came to me, and I tried to recover that superfluity with what I had placed on his hand from medicines after the purification of his body: and the superfluity was not found, and it began to walk into another finger; until corruption began in the hand.
Therefore he called me to cut his hand: and I was unwilling, hoping for a surplus of his recovery from me: and I also feared about him death at the cutting of his hand. For the man's virtue was above the wound.
After that, when he despaired of me, he returned to his land: and it came to me about him, because he hurried, and fell with his whole hand: and he was healed.
And I have not brought forth this narrative, except that it may be an aid regarding that which falls under the genus of this ailment: and that it may be a sign by which it is made known, and according to which it is treated.
On the care of sinuses, and the quality of clysters with medicines.
When a boil occurs in certain fleshy limbs and the space of the boil is prolonged until it forms a collection of pus: then it bursts or is perforated, and what is in it of pus comes out, and a hollow place remains as if it were a vessel: and the skin which is above it like a cloth that has already been finely woven: and does not reach the ultimate stage of corruption, nor presses into bone, nor into nerve, nor into ligament: it deserves to be called a Sinus: and it is not called a Fistula: however, if its space is prolonged until corruption presses into any of these members, then it is called a Fistula, or zuken.
And the care of it is that you cut that skin entirely as it revolves; and especially when it has been smoothed out and made like cloth; and trust that it will not adhere to the place because of its corruption.
If you hope that it will adhere to the skin, and last corruption does not follow from this; and if there is thickness in the skin from flesh, then care for it with a clyster, and it is, as you should consider, to the sinus.
If it is large, and the pus that comes out of it has a foul odor: then cleanse it with an Egyptian medicine, which is vinegar, oil, and ziniar, taking equal parts, they are gathered in a vessel; and they are boiled over a fire until the medicine boils; and it comes to the thickness of honey.
Then take from it what is necessary for you; and dissolve it with water and honey, and administer a clyster into the sinus with it. And bind the opening of it, and let the medicine remain in it for the quantity of two hours.
Then remove it with expression: do this for so many days until the sinus is purified and the stench is removed.
And sometimes it is given as a suppository with ash water, when this medicine is not present to you; and it is, that you take the ash of willow wood or ash from holly trees; and pour it upon the water. Then treat it with a suppository, and give a clyster with it, until you are confident that the sinus has been emptied.
If the patient cannot tolerate the Egyptian medicine or water of ash, then cleanse him with water and honey to which has been mixed something of crushed zinc. Or cleanse it with a clyster of honey and wine mixed together, because the property of honey is to purify and wash; and wine causes the wound to adhere, especially when there is an abundance of astringency and dryness in the wine.
When you have done this several times, and you trust that the corruption of the sinus is already removed: then irrigate it with that which makes flesh grow there.
And it is, as you receive from the plaster dyafinicon: and dissolve it with rose oil, and sticking wine: or clysterize it with certain medicines, and other ointments, whose properties I have written in the treatise of ointments.
If the opening of the sinus is narrow, in which a clyster cannula cannot enter: then widen it with a small knife: or put a linen cloth smeared with Egyptian ointment or basil ointment into it, until it widens.
And similarly if the opening is also wide, then gather its lips together with a suture: and release from it as much as can enter a clyster without addition.
And similarly if the opening from which the pus runs is elevated upwards: then dry it in a lower place which is within it, so that the pus may run out of it downwards: because when pus is constricted in the depth of the sinus, it prevents flesh from being generated in it.
If it is not possible for you to cut the sinus about lower part of it as you wish; then try to prepare the member with a preparation which will drain pus from it easily as it is prepared for you. Try so that nothing of the pus is retained in it at all; and take medicinal substances which are placed over the sign, suitable to what you wish. And spread them upon linen cloth. And let the cloth be of such quantity that it may communicate the whole of the Sinus. Then cut with forceps at the direct opening of the sinus foramen, a little larger than the opening of the sinus. Then extend from the ointment soft on another cloth, according to the quantity of the hole: and place it over it in a position far from the opening of the sinus, so that the exit of pus from it may be relieved: and do not remove the large cloth unless after some days once.
He who is over the opening of the Sinus is he whom you remove at every hour so that what has gathered there from pus may go out. And so that you know whether that which runs out of it is much or little; or digested or undigested.
And with this again consider the place of the Sinus, whether the lord feels pain in it or not, and whether there is an abscess in it or not.
When therefore your treatment is extended thus, and you see the matter which comes out above the opening of the Sinus, then know that the Sinus is already adhering, or approaching adherence: then therefore dissolve it in the morning, or on the third day; and when you dissolve the wound, then change the cloth, and renew the ointment.
If, however, a subtle virus emerges from the sinus after a long time, then do not despair of its adherence, because its healing is approaching, because there are some men who delay the generation of flesh in their wounds, and there are some who act contrary to this.
If after many days undigested pus exits from the sinus, then know that the sinus does not adhere.
If therefore the adhesion of the sinus is delayed and its matter is prolonged: then in the drying of the medicines, truly let physicians do that they be moist in their substance and dry in their virtue: such as unguent dyafinicon, when there is added to it superfluity of addition from zegi.
And from the last cure which is made, it is that you receive diaconical ointment and dissolve it with rose oil and return over it ancient wine equal in substance to its own.
Then make it with him, and use it.
And now he does something similar to this action, when you do not have dyafinicon ointment at hand, honey, when you have boiled it until it thickens, and use it. Or take frankincense, and aloes, and myrrh: pound the whole thing, and pulverize it over honey while it is over fire. Then a linen cloth over the same pad, and tighten it over the sinus: and a linen bandage with honey that you have boiled until it is thick, and dust it over the top, and place it on the pad, and tighten it.
And sometimes it is administered with long birthwort and roots of heavenly lily, and lily: and the herb's flour, and centaury. Use these simple or combined ones, rub them, and also the sieves themselves: and pulverize above honey which I have narrated, and use it.
If the matter of the sinus prolongs itself and does not heal with this treatment: then know that there is corruption deep within it; or it already makes impression on the bones, or on the other hard members, or on the ligaments: then cure it with the treatment of fistulas, according to what has been said before.
On the treatment of a felon and an abrased nail, and the addition of a finger digit, and sectioning.
A panaritium is an abscess that is born under the nail of the finger of the hand, or of the foot: and perhaps it is born in the remaining fingers.
When therefore the matter is prolonged and its cure is neglected, and it becomes inflamed with a hot abscess; or it decays and emits pus until it corrodes the root of the nail; and perhaps it destroys it entirely, and perhaps the decay reaches the bone until it has a foul odor; and the extremity of the finger becomes wide, and its color becomes dark.
When therefore you treat the flesh, with that which we said in the division, and your treatment does not help: then it is necessary that you cut with a knife all the superfluity that remains from the nail. Then cauterize the wound after that. A heated cautery is very useful.
If the bone is sound and the nail is also healthy, and the additions of the empty nail already press the flesh to the insides, and they begin to prick it, and wound it: then it is necessary that you place a thin Radicum under the angle of the nail which pricks the flesh, and drive it upwards: and cut into that flesh with ease, and place on top of that which remains of flesh from burning medicines, corrosives, until the whole thing is removed. Then care for it with ointments until it heals.
If corruption has already set into the bone, then it is necessary to cut into that bone and remove it; for it is never cured while the corrupted bone remains in place.
If you see that decay and corruption are advancing in the finger, then cut it at one of the nodes, according to what has been said before about its recollection. Then care for it until it heals.
If it happens that a nail is rubbed or struck and there is severe pain in it, then it is necessary to bleed the patient first. Then dry the fingernail with a sharp spatula, making a transverse section from top to bottom as desired.
And guard that the cutting does not reach the flesh which is under the nail, for you will cause to happen through it an intense pain on the weak one, and it will be a cause to the birth of flesh added in place.
Then care for the place with that which allays pains.
The finger which is born in some hands of men, perhaps is fleshy throughout: and perhaps there are bones in some of them, and perhaps there is a nail in it: and some of them are born at the root of the joint, or its nativity is in some nodes of the finger. Therefore, he who is born in certain knots of the finger, does not move: and he who is born at the joint of the finger, perhaps moves.
Therefore, the section of that which is fleshy from those things is easy. That is, that you cut it at its root with a wide Spatula.
The cure of him whose nativity is at the root of the articulation is difficult. Therefore, abstain from incisions of it.
In that one who is born in the finger at the last of the knots, then it is necessary that you cut first his flesh with a round incision unto the bone. Then saw with one of those saws suitable to it. Then take care of him until he is healed.
The solidifying together of the fingers, which often happens either from birth, that is, it is born with it, or from the closing of a wound, or burning with fire, and the like.
Therefore it is necessary that you cut away that consolidation, until the fingers return according to their natural form. Then place between them strips of linen or cloths soaked in rose oil so that they do not coalesce too quickly, and separate them; or place between them a thin lead sheet until the place has consolidated according to what is necessary.
And similarly if it happens that a consolidation occurs with some fingers and palm, then dry that consolidation according to how it is prepared and convenient for the figure of the limb.
On the incision of a tumor.
These are veins that are wrapped up, large and full of superfluous melancholic humors which occur in many parts of the body: and most frequently these happen in the legs, especially in the legs of those who travel and labor, and carry things.
Therefore it is necessary that you administer an excision of the body from black bile sometimes with a strong excision.
Then the surgeon should cut open a vein of the patient.
The cure of them with iron is according to two modes. One of them is that they are cut and black blood is extracted. And another way is, that the vein may be lifted up and taken out entirely.
The section is as follows. First soak the leg in hot water a great deal until the thick, foul blood dissolves. Then bind the leg of the infirm from the upper part of his hip, as far as the lower part of the knee with a band. Then dry out the vein in one place, or two, or three with a wide incision.
Then elevate, that is, draw blood from the lower part of the leg up to the top, and the phlebotomist should first attend to those who are ill. Then dry the nail with care until the amount of blood that you see is sufficient and which the strength of the weak person can tolerate.
Thereafter bind it: and order him to abstain from foods that produce black bile; and repeat evacuation and phlebotomy, when the veins are full, and this harms the sick person.
Its elevation is according to this manner. If the leg is sick and there are many hairs in it. Then introduce it into the bath: or embrocate it on his leg with hot water, until it turns red and the veins swell: or exercise it vigorously if there is no bath present, until the limb heats up.
Then dry the skin in a direct vein incision along its length: or at its end at the knee, or in the lower part of it at the heel.
Then open the skin with hooks, and scarify the vein from every part until it appears to the sense: and at its appearance, you see it red to blackness declining. When therefore it comes out of the skin, you see it white as if it were a string.
Then introduce a probe under it until it is elevated and emerges from the skin; suspend it with a curved hook, gently.
Then make another incision nearby the first incision three finger-widths away. Then scrape the skin from the upper part of the vein until it appears. Then elevate it with rays as you did, and suspend it with another hook, as you did at first.
Then a dry incision, or several incisions, if you need it so. Then elevate and make an incision at its last section near the heel.
Then extract it to the incision which is above it.
And do this until you have removed it from the incision which is higher, or higher incisions, until when it comes out completely: then cut it.
If it does not obey you to be extracted and lifted: then insert a needle with a strong double thread, and tie it, and extract it: or insert a probe under it: and revolve your hand to every part, until it comes out. And beware that it not happen. For if it is cut, it will be difficult for you to remove it well, and it will enter over the weak one harm from it.
When therefore you have extracted it whole, then place over the wound's location wool soaked in wine and rose oil, or oil: and take care of it until it heals.
If the vine is contracted, having a twisting which wraps around its parts, and it is not as we said according to straightness; then it is necessary that you find on every part of its twisting and the locations of its wrapping.
Then suspend it with hooks until you have lifted the whole thing up; and be careful at your incision upon it that you do not cut a vein or wound; for it will be difficult to lift or remove it from above you. Therefore, preserve your prudence.
The form of incision for healing a wound.
The form of an inguinal hernia.
It is not to be cared for like the rest of the hooks, nor does it have a sharp tip, so that it may not wound a vein; and it is of thick flexibility, gentle: because if it were fine, it would wound a vein with its fineness; indeed let its excess thickness be as we said.
On the Extraction of the Vein of the Leg.
This vein is generated in the legs in warm lands, such as Al-Jazira and the land of the Arabs, and in dry, hot lands, with little moisture, that is, where no grass grows nor trees.
And perhaps it is generated in other places of the body besides the legs.
And its generation is from putrefaction that happens under the skin, just as it happens inside the bodies of snakes and roundworms, and worms generated between the skin and flesh.
And the sign of the beginning of this vein's condition is that there is a vehement inflammation in the leg. Then it is sewn up. Then begins the vein to exit from the place of that incision, as if it were the root of a plant, or an animal.
When therefore the extremity appears, then it is necessary that you envelop upon the very same piece of lead, whose weight be from one drachm to two drachms and knot it: and let go the suspended lead from the leg.
Whenever anything issues forth from it to the outside, envelop that in lead and tie a knot in it. If it is prolonged much, then cut a part of it, and wrap the remainder. And do not cut it root and branch before it has entirely come out. For if you cut it, it contracts and enters into the flesh, and causes a boil to occur, and rot in the place, and a bad ulcer.
Therefore it is necessary that it be gently treated and extracted gradually until it emerges from its last part, and nothing of it remains in the body: and from this vein there exits in some men what is five palms long and ten. And now it has come to me because a man came out of twenty palms.
If it happens to you during your treatment time: then insert a ray into the hole of it, and perforate it with a long hole, with the body, until all that is in it from matter is evacuated; and administer the putrefaction of the place with medicines for several days. Then care for the healing of abscesses.
And this vein sometimes has many branches, especially when it appears at the ankle joint, or in the foot itself: and it happens to have many openings, and a branch emerges from every opening.
Therefore, treat the wound, as we have said in the division, and with that which has been previously stated.
On cutting out worms generated under the skin, and it is called the ailment of cattle.
This ailment is called in our region "Bull's disease" because it happens often to oxen and there is a small worm which is born between the skin and flesh.
And it moves throughout the entire body, ascending and descending: and it is shown to the sense during its walking from limb to limb, until it ruptures wherever it ruptures in the skin, and exits.
And its generation is from the rotting of some humors. As worms and serpents, that is, roundworms and pinworms, happen in the belly.
And it is feared because of its malice. Because when he enters the body and is raised to the head and arrives at the place: then perhaps he opens in it and exits and destroys the eye: and this happens many times.
Therefore, when you wish to treat him and remove him, then this is done only in his walking and appearance to his sense.
Therefore, it is necessary that you bind what is above it and what is below it with a strong ligament constriction. Then dry it over itself, and remove it.
If it goes deep into the flesh, and you do not find it, then put over the place a cautery with fire, until it dies.
And that which is feared more, is its corruption, which corrupts the eye, as we have said.
If you see it having arrived at the head near the eye, then tighten under it the eyebrow with a good constriction. Then dry over it, and remove it.
And it is necessary that the sick one use purification of his body, with medicines dissolving bad putrid humors.
And let him beware of foods that produce decay.
On the cutting of a wound for illness according to Nakir.
This ailment is called Nakir in our land: and it is a pain that occurs in certain limbs: then it changes from limb to limb.
And I indeed have already seen him, according to what I tell you. I was called to a woman who was ill with certain pains. And he exposed his arm, and I saw a slight swelling in the vein of the arm's cord.
And after I had waited for an hour, I saw one swelling move with a worm-like motion, ascending toward its shoulder faster than is possible; like quicksilver when it runs from place to place.
Therefore pain receded from that place, and it was fixed in the shoulder.
Then he sat for an hour, and it ran into the rest of his body until it reached the other arm.
Then he told me that he was turning over his whole body, as I saw.
I was amazed at the speed of its transposition from limb to limb. And I truly before that had not seen this illness with my eyes in this way according to this manner. Nevertheless I have seen many who find pain to be shifted from limb to limb: and I have not seen it with my own eyes, as I saw it in a woman.
And I do not think that this is anything other than because the woman was from those who have been found with dry veins of the body; therefore, it appears to the sense this alteration by wind.
And it is necessary that they not appear according to this reason in those who are delicate and have moist bodies, the hidden veins.
When therefore you wish to cure it, and the patient feels that pain: then if it appears to you, as we have said, then tightly grasp above it, and below it quickly: and dry above it, until that windiness has been driven out; and cauterize the place.
If you do not see it with your own eyes, then treat it with an expulsion of the body, and with that which expels winds, and carminates them with foul pills, and pills of sparrow, and similar things.
On the Extraction of Arrows.
Arrows differ according to their kinds and according to the places in which they fall from the body.
Their diversity is: because among them there are those that have large points, and small ones: and concave, and dull. And of these are those that have three angles. And from those who have tongues. And from those which have stars.
Those which are according to limbs in which they fall, are according to two modes. Or else the parts are concave from the main parts, such as the brain, and the heart, and the liver, and the lung, and the kidneys, and the intestines, and the bladder, and things similar to these. When therefore it falls into any of these limbs, and the signs of death appear to you, which I tell you after, then it is necessary that you avoid the extraction of that arrow from them, for death follows him in whom it is, in many dispositions. And when these bad signs do not appear to you, and the arrow is not hidden in the depth of the limb, then extract it, and care for the wound.
When an arrow falls into the brain and penetrates it, and exits through the foramen above the cerebrum, these are the signs: because violent soda, scotoma, vertigo, redness in the eyes, inflation, redness of the tongue, spasms, and a mixing of the intellect, and vomiting of cholera occur from it. And perhaps blood comes out of the nose, or ears. And perhaps speech is cut off and the voice is destroyed. And moisture white like a poultice comes out from the wound's place. And it exits from there like a watery consistency of flesh.
If therefore these signs appear to you, then abstain from treatment, and remove the arrow if it does not exit.
The signs of an arrow when it falls into the heart are: that it is near the left breast: and he feels as if it is fixed in a hard thing, not in a hollow thing. And perhaps the motion of an arrow is similar to the motion of a pulse: and blood black runs out of the wound. And follows that coldness of the extremities, and cold sweat, and fainting spells. Know therefore that death certainly comes.
And the sign of an arrow wound to the lung is the outflow of frothy blood from the wound; and the vessels which follow the neck become suppurated, and the color of the sick person changes. And expires great spirit, high: and seeks attraction of cold air.
If an arrow falls into the membrane that is in the chest: then it is near the ribs: and a great sigh is made with violent pain: and all the limbs of the shoulders move.
And if an arrow falls into the stomach, then perhaps something indigested will emerge from the wound along with food.
And if an arrow falls into the belly, and lodges there, something of feces will come out of the wound, or a piece of the intestine which is already torn. There is therefore no hope of healing it, nor of extracting the arrow.
If a shaft falls into the bladder and urine flows out, and something proceeds from it to the outside, and the pain is stronger over the weak, then you shall know that it is mortal.
The remaining limbs, such as the face, and neck, and throat, and spatula, and aid, and dorsal vertebrae, and collarbone, and hip, and leg, and similar parts of the limbs are cured according to various arrangements: when it does not find the artery or nerve; and there is no poisonous arrow.
And I tell you what I saw of these arrows, that you may know by this about your cure.
That is because a certain man had an arrow fall into the tear duct of his eye, at the root of his nose. Therefore I extracted it from the other part, under the fat of his ear: and he was healed, and no harm came to his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Jew, which had fallen into the fat of his eye under his lower eyelid; and the arrow was already hidden, and I did not extract from it except its small end, with which it adheres to its shaft; and it was a large arrow from the arrows of the Turkish bows, with a blunt iron, in which there are no ears.
The Jew was therefore healed, and no evil accident happened to his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from the throat of a Christian.
And there was an Arabic arrow, and it is that which has ears. Therefore I followed it upon its own between two guides: and now it was submerged in its throat. Therefore I worked on it until I extracted it: and Christian was healed.
And I extracted another arrow from a man who had fallen into his belly: and we thought that he would die from it.
After thirty days had passed, and nothing had changed in his condition, I cut above the arrow.  I worked on it and extracted it. He was healed, and no harm befell him.
And I saw a man who had an arrow wound in his back, and the wound had closed over it. After that, seven years having passed, a arrow emerged from the root of his hip.
And I saw a woman to whom an arrow fell into her belly, and the wound was consolidated upon her, and the arrow remained inside her; and nothing of her dispositions was changed, and the woman did not find harm to her in any of her natural operations.
And I saw another man to whom an arrow had fallen in his face: and the wound was consolidated, and remained, not finding much pain in him: and similar things like these many.
And I extracted an arrow from a man of the king's princes, which had fallen into the middle of his nose, and had already declined to the right side a little: and the arrow was entirely hidden.
Therefore I was called to his care after a gunshot wound for three days. Therefore, I found a wound caused by an arrow quite severe.
Therefore I inquired about it with a fine probe, and I did not feel it: and he himself found a prick and pain under his ear on the right side. Therefore, I believed that the puncture was from the end of the arrow.
Therefore, I applied a plaster to the place with a plaster that had attractive and digestive power, confident within myself that the place would suppurate and that there would appear to me the sign of an arrow so that I could cut above it: and it did not happen in the place an accident signifying that the arrow had followed the place.
Therefore I treated him diligently with a poultice for many days: and no accident happened, and the wound was consolidated in the meantime: and he remained weak from its extraction for a space of days, until one day he felt the arrow inside his nose. And he told me this.
And he told me this. Therefore I placed on that wound a medicine acute and corrosive for many days until it opened; and when I felt it, and sensed the subtle point of the arrow adhering to the wood.
Then I added to the incision of the wound with that sharp medicine until the tip of the arrow appeared visibly to us; and about four months passed for me with him.
Then after the wound was enlarged, and it was possible for me to introduce forceps into it, and to move it, and it did not obey to exit: then I did not cease to be subtle and ingenious upon it with modes of ingenuity, until on one day I began upon it with forceps properly made, according to what will come in the form of them at the end of the chapter, until I extracted it. Then I restored the wound.
And physicians judge that cartilage of the nose is not restored. Therefore I restored it, and the wound was consolidated, and the sickly man was healed in perfect health: nothing harmed him at all.
And I tell you the quality of extracting certain arrows: so that you may place that rule and meaning over that which I did not say.
Because the parts of this art and their distinctions are not encompassed with the narrative; nor does that book comprehend it, and a wise artificer compares with little to much; and with what is present to what is absent; and finds a new operation and a new instrument among the descents of foreign things of this art when they happen.
I say therefore that arrows do not come out of members in which they are fixed, except according to two ways. Or by drawing it out from the place where it entered. Or conversely, of the other part.
And that which goes out where it entered, is either an arrow proceeding in fleshy place: and it is drawn and goes out.
If it does not yield to removal by the hour in which it falls: then you ought to let it alone for several days, until the flesh that is around it has rotted, and the attraction of it has been lessened, and its exit made easier.
And similarly if it is situated in the bone and does not obey you for its exit, then let it be (released) even for several days; and repeat it with traction and motion every day until it emerges.
If it does not obey you at the exit after days, then it is necessary that you perforate around the arrow in the very bone from every part with a fine borer until the arrows are enlarged. Then draw it and extract it.
If now the arrow is already hidden in the place of the body, and is concealed and hidden from sense: then inquire for it with a probe. If you feel it, then draw it out with certain instruments which are suitable for its extraction.
If you cannot remove it because of the wound's constriction and because of the arrow's length deep within; and there is no bone, nor nerve, nor vein there: then leave it there until the wound is enlarged and it is possible to extract the arrow.
If the ears are those that are held fast, then free the flesh fixed in them from every part, with every means you can, and means, if it cannot be done by evacuation of the flesh in fractures of the ears and their torsion, until it is evacuated.
And you when you administer the extraction of the arrow, wherever it may be, then use the twisting of your hand, with forceps to all parts, until you make it come out: and with the subtlest last subtlety, so that the arrow does not break, and its extraction becomes difficult for you.
If you cannot operate on it within one hour, then release it for several days until what is around it rots. Then repeat it: for if the wound is relieved. But if the blood flow does harm to you, then administer what we said about the cure in its chapter: and guard your virtue from incision of the vein, or nerve, or cord.
And administer the skill in every way that is possible to you for the evacuation of the arrow, and let it be with ease, and tranquility, or slowness, and firmness, as I have told you.
And it is necessary that you do when drawing a shaft, that the weak point be according to the figure, according to how it was in the case of the shaft itself. Because it is more useful. If it is not possible for you to do that, then administer what is possible for you from the figures.
The arrow that comes out from the opposite part or something already proceeds from it to the outside: or it makes a hump of the extremity of the arrow by sense from the upper part of the skin nearby, and you see it prominent: therefore dry it above, and let the section be according to the quantity which they grasp in it with forceps.
Then draw it out: for indeed it becomes easy to extract.
If it is held in bone, then turn your hand according to the roundness until the awl presses into the bone and enlarges itself; then draw it out.
And if not: let it be released for some days; then repeat it, until it comes out.
If a piece of wood from an arrow is in it, then drive it out with it.
If a stick has fallen and you wish to administer a thrust: then insert into it a concave instrument so that the concavity of it enters the tail of the Arrow: then drive it with the same.
If however the arrow is concave, then drive it in with an instrument entering it. The arrow is therefore lifted by that.
If the arrow is poisoned, then it is necessary to cut out all the flesh in which the poison is; if it is possible for you to do that. Then care for it as is fitting.
If the arrow is falling in the chest, or in the belly, or in the bladder, or in the side: and it is near because you touch it with your finger, and it is possible for you to cut above it: then cut, and beware of incision of the vein, or nerve, and remove it.
Then sew the wound, if you see it, and care for it until it heals.
And this is the form of Forceps, with which arrows are extracted.
Let the extremities be like the beak of a bird, which are made like iron: when they grasp upon the arrow, they do not let it go.
And many kinds, great and small and medium, are made of these, according to the quantity of magnitude of the Arrow, and its smallness, and the width of the wound, and its constricting.
Shape of the Concave Implement.
And the form of the Impeller for deafness.
This is for a deaf limb like the radius, so that its entrance may be relieved into a concave arrow, and it may be driven in with it.
On Bloodletting of Veins.
There are thirty veins that are commonly bled in the body.
Of these in the head there are sixteen: two pulsatile Veins, which are behind the ears, called the two Herbs: and two Arteries, which are in the two temples apparent: and two Veins which are in the two lacrimals of the two eyes, which are called Aspicientes: and the prepared Vein in the middle of the forehead: and the Vein which is in the extremity of the nose. And two guideges, which are in the neck. And two veins that are in the upper lip of the mouth. And two veins that are in the lower lip. And these are the veins that are called Algecherich. And the two veins that are under the tongue.
The veins that are opened by bleeding in the arms, there are five veins in each arm.
One of these is the Cephalic vein, and it is from the sylvian side: and the common people call it the head vein. And Fusca. And there is the Middle vein, and it is composed of the Basilica branch and the Cephalic branch: and the common people call it the vein of the body. And it is called Basilica, and is placed in the domestic side, and is also called Tilillicaris. And the common people call it a vein of the belly. On the utility of phlebotomy, and the precautions to be observed in it. The quality of phlebotomy, and its accidents from it, about which you ought to think carefully.
First, therefore, you ought to know that bloodletting is not administered unless for the preservation of health and its perseverance, and caution against the approach of diseases.
And let a bloodletting be made in one of the three veins that are in the elbow, namely Cephalic, and Dark, and Basilica. And that venesection be done at the beginning of Spring on an empty stomach, in summer, and autumn, and in winter after a meal, when signs of fullness appear.
And let a bloodletting be done on the first day, or on the third, after three hours have passed.
Bloodletting, which is administered in illnesses, does not have a determined hour; but when necessity and convenience call for it, at night or day, and at every hour, and at every time.
And boys should not be bled until they come to fourteen years of age. A pregnant woman shall not be bled, unless from the fourth to the seventh month. The elderly who have passed sixty years should not be bled.
When therefore someone wants to have a bloodletting for any reason, it is necessary that he cleanse his bowels before the bloodletting with a gentle enema, if there is much retained feces in them: so that the veins during the bloodletting do not draw up putrid superfluities from the intestines which are harmful to the principal members.
Nor should a patient who is vomiting from fullness be bled, nor one who is drunk or suffering from drunkenness until that has passed from him. Nor one with dropsy.
And let phlebotomy be avoided again after colic passion, and vomiting, and flux of the belly, and multiplication of intercourse, and labor, and exercise, and vigils, and fasting, and all that dissolves virtue from the bodily or animal substance.
Then consider in the subtlety of humors before that, if blood is thick with foods and drinks, and medicines: if it is possible for him to do that.
Then he enters the bath, if nothing prevents him; or he is exercised with some exercise, so that the blood may be made more subtle.
And place the phlebotomy of him in his chest, about the third day, as we said: and endeavor that his chest be evacuated on that day from all accidents of evil animals: such as anxiety, and anger, and fear: and from all bodily accidents, such as labor, and punishment, and superfluity, and those similar.
And let him make present to his seat things of which use men have grown accustomed from fragrance and scent and excitement and similar things as much as possible.
Then the bloodletter should sit on a stool, which is higher than the patient's bed; afterwards he takes out his arm and rubs it with his hand twice, or thrice. Then tighten the bandage with corrections and wrap it twice.
And let the constriction be moderate, for when a constriction is unequal, either through an excess in the constriction, then it prevents the course of blood; or in softening, then also it prevents the course of blood.
Then after constriction, it is necessary that the surgeon rub his hands together with a cloth until the veins swell and are apparent to the sense.
Then the phlebotomist shall clean the lancet with a little oil, an old one in particular.
Then he places his index finger of the left hand upon the vein in the place where he wants to perform phlebotomy, carefully so that it does not slip away and the blow is not missed. Because of the veins there is which you find like a fleeing chord among the bloodletters: and from them is that which is full of windiness: and when you place the bloodletter upon it, it contracts under the bloodletter, and the bloodletter is deceived, and does not open the vein: and if he opens it, it is strict: therefore it is necessary that the bloodletter be firm, and be solicitous in all these things.
Then he should put down the lancet. If therefore a vein is opened from one place, then it is good. And if this does not happen, then repeat the same thing in another place nearby, or above it with haste: if the place does not suppurate.
If it is suppurating, or the patient grows weak, then dismiss him on one day, or two days: and let him not tighten a bandage. Perhaps it will draw a hot abscess. Let him not enter the bath.
Then let a bloodletting be done, if he desires.
If the incision is small, or if blood runs subtly and you fear that not the quantity of blood which you wish will leave; then repeat the phlebotomy in the very hole according to straightness, and add a little in the operation.
And do this with speed, before the place becomes closed up, for an infected place is in many men at a small operation.
If therefore you see the abscess itself, then you do not go over it at all, for it does not excuse anything to you. And place something of the oil of feces upon it; because it will lessen the course of blood. And it is better in this place than oil itself, and than the rest of the olive.
And similarly administer the feces of oil in all your bloodletting, where you bleed veins at the stopping of the flow of blood; and they do this with tyriaca alfarach, and sagzena, when it is placed from one of them on the spot.
Blood is refined and dissolved when it is thick.
If it happens that a large abscess occurs at the site of phlebotomy, for often this happens, especially to those who are not subjected to phlebotomy except when necessary, or whose vein opening is small, then immediately place a sponge soaked in water and salt, heated briefly, over the site; and bind it for one hour: because it will be resolved.
And it is necessary that you do this after the blood has exited from the vein itself, with its complement, or from another vein.

Nevertheless, they weaken the body greatly, and cause syncope to happen in many people.
And the healing of the windpipes is close to the healing of the two leg winds.
The quality of the placement of the vents is that you place the vent first empty, and draw (with) a moderate suction: and do not prolong the position of the vents, but place them quickly, and remove them quickly, so that humors come to the place with equal arrival: and do not cease to repeat this and continue, until you see the place already red and swollen, and the redness of blood appears: then therefore scarify, and repeat suction gradually. Then consider the arrangement of the bodies.
For he who is of men tender flesh, rare pores, it is necessary that he scarify him once, no more: that the place be not ulcerated.
And tell the Ventusator to enlarge the incision and make it only slightly deep: and temper the suction in ease, and speed, and his own movement.
If there is a thickening in the blood, then it is necessary to scarify it twice. In the first incision indeed there is opened a way to subtle bloodletting and its moistening; and in the second indeed, examination of delicate extraction of thick blood.
If the blood is foul indeed, then let a third scraping be made, so that it may reach its end.
And lastly, when we wish to draw blood gradually, or a little, one incision is sufficient for us. If we wish to make an extraction of blood, we shall scarify many times with a multiple scarification.
And if we measure that the blood is thick, then it is necessary that it be scarified with a deep incision; and the equal limit in the incision is the depth of the skin.
What thing ought to be administered from oils at the positioning of Winds, and what should a Wind-stopper beware of.
In that case, whose skin is thick, hard, dark, and its pores are narrow, it is necessary to anoint the places with Ventosare with open, soothing, resolving oils: if it is in summer time, such as alkeiri oil, or violet oil, or almond oil, or gourd seed oil.
And if it is in wintertime, then oil of narcissus, or oil of lily, or oil of chamomile, or syrup: and similar ones.
If, however, the surplus is coarse and cold, then the oil should be bitter almond, or balm oil, or white oil, or anise oil: and similar ones.
If a large incision is to be made in the tender flesh, it must be prohibited from oil, and this place must be washed after the operation with rose water, or cold water, or solar water, or water of cucumber, or water of purslane, and similar ones.
He whose blood is of much moisture, wash wounds with vinegar, or myrtle water, and sumach, and the like.
If these superfluities are coarse, he should wash his windy parts with old wine, or with wormwood water, or an infusion of anise or chamomile: and the like.
And it is necessary that ventilation be avoided in the bath, or in the vestige of the bath, indeed it is necessary that it be administered after exiting the bath for an hour, or for two hours.
And it is not necessary for someone to sleep after venesection.
What is that which ought to be regulated before venesection, namely phlebotomy?
And after this it is necessary to consider first: then if he be suffering from a wind or phlebotomy and is choleric, and his blood is overwhelmed by sharpness and inflammation; then it is necessary for him to receive cooling things, such as pomegranate juice; and also endive with vinegar and lettuce, and secanabin, and iuleb; and similar things.
And place his food chicks and meats of sheep, and foods cooked with herbs: and similar things.
And if its complexion is cold, it is necessary that it take in drink syrup of honey, or of althea, or asclepion made from seeds: and it should take medium fragrant wine, which is between old and new: and it should be prescribed that it use little food. And put food for him of chicks, and canebir: and sparrows, that is, small birds: and collared dove chicks as well.
And it is necessary that the drink on the day of venesection and phlebotomy be more than food; and it is necessary that in some men in the hours before venesection,  tyriaca alfaroch or medicine of musk, that is to say muscata, or alesselithe be given before venesection: and before phlebotomy, or after: so that the main limbs are strengthened and the blood is refined. And it is not right that they should take it in a heated drink.
On varices without incisions. Those incisions which are without incision are called evacuations which are placed over the liver, and spleen, and foot, and over the abdomen, navel, and places of the kidneys, and hip joint; because these members do not allow incisions upon themselves.
And it is not sought through these, except for either attachment from a member of blood to a member: such as we place the Ventose upon the nipples in the affliction of nasal blood flow. And we use them to loosen coldness of wind unseen in a member: as when we place a poultice over the belly and navel. For it indeed thins out the limb, and heats it, and removes pain because it dissolves that windiness.
It is also placed over the kidneys when there is a swelling or stone in them. For with the force of its pull it perhaps opens up a wound, or removes a stone from its place.
And similarly he does when it is placed over the liver, and the spleen in case of inflammation in both.
And these ventouses are administered with incisions only. And sometimes they are administered with fire.
And sometimes they administer tepid water filled in affections of the pleurae: and that is so that it may be filled with a ventouse, and be large, hot water only, or water in which herbs which are suitable have been boiled.
The form of a pessary administered with fire.
Let the width of its opening be two finger-breadths, according to what we have formed: and let its quantity in depth be half a palm: and let there be on its side, in the place which is about the middle of it, a small hole, according to the quantity which would enter it with a needle: let it be made simultaneously of copper or brass, of gross margin, soft, equal, and smooth: so that it does not injure the limb when it is placed upon it: and let there be in its middle a transverse rod of copper or iron, where a candle is placed with fire.
And now this is made wind-filled large, larger than we have narrated, and somewhat less than that, according to the ailments and age of him who uses it.
For boys and thin people there are other kinds of ventosaes than those for men and those with large bodies.
The quality of placing this wind-blown one with fire on the limb is, that you light a torch with fire from linen properly, or a small candle from wax: and place it above the middle of the transverse bar which is in the middle of the wind-blown: so that the fire be kindled about the lower part of the wind-blown, so that the body of the sick person does not burn.
Then a finger is to be placed over the limb, and the finger should be over the opening that I have said, until when we hold the windpipe: while we need it, remove the finger, and vapor will emerge over that opening, and the windpipe will immediately loosen.
Then apply the fire lick according to its measure, and repeat it if needed.
In the Ventous however which is administered in the pain of pleuritis with water, there is no transverse rod, nor hole: and it is not administered unless it is filled with water, and placed upon the limb only.
And this is its form.
And this suction cup, the larger it is, so that it may take much water, is better.
On the suspension of bloodletting.
Leeches are not administered in many cases, except in those members where the position of venesections is not possible, because of their small size, such as the lip, and gums, and similar ones. Or in a denuded limb, such as a finger, and nose, and things like them.
And the quality of using them is: that they be obtained from leeches which are in sweet waters, pure from corruption.
Then let them be placed day and night in fresh water until they are famished, and nothing remains in their belly. Then the body shall be evacuated first with phlebotomy and with cupping. Then wipe the limb of the infirm until it turns red. Afterwards they are to be placed upon it.
When they are therefore filled and fall and there is possible suction of the place with a windpipe, then it is more ultimate in healing: and if not, then wash the place with vinegar. Then with much water it is rubbed and squeezed.
If the course of blood continues after the application of leeches, and it is still oozing, then place a linen cloth soaked in cold water on top, until the bleeding stops.
If the blood multiplies, then pulverize upon it  zegi or ground gall, and similar things from styptics, until the bleeding is stopped.
Or let half beans, their outer skin removed, be placed over the place: and they be left until the beans adhere to the place, for the blood is cut off.
And it is necessary if repetition of bloodletting is necessary, when possible, to change them.
If leeches are forbidden from suspension, then the place shall be wiped with fresh blood. Either fix a point (of) an awl in places until something comes out of the blood. Then they are to be placed, for when they themselves feel something from the blood, they immediately adhere.
When therefore you wish them to fall, pulverize upon them something of crushed aloe, or salt, or ashes: and they will fall immediately.
This Chapter is also many times necessary in the art of Medicine, and it is the restoration of fractures and dislocations of accidents in bones.
Know, O Sons, that those who boast about this Chapter are the foolish doctors and the common people, and those who have never looked at the book of ancients or read a letter from it.
And because of this cause, this mode is deprived of sciences in our land. And I have never found anything good in it whatsoever, and I have not attained from it that which I have attained except because of the length of reading ancient books, and study above understanding them, until I extracted knowledge from them.
Then by experience and study throughout my life's length.
And now I have described to you in this Chapter all that contains my knowledge: and above what has passed experiment, after I have abbreviated it for you, and freed it from the occupation of elongation, and abbreviated it at the end of abbreviation, and showed it at the end of declination: and I have formed in it many forms from the forms of instruments which are administered in it, as it is by addition of declination, just as I did in the two preceding Chapters.
Head II.
It is necessary, as we say at the beginning of this Chapter, that the greatest discourse and chapters, to the understanding of which the artisan first must be brought to the station over their truth: namely, that you, and those who are ready, may know or learn this noble art, and others from you.
I say therefore, that when it happens to anyone a fracture, or dislocation, or torsion, or fall: then it is necessary, that you hurry first to bleed him, and release him, or both together; if nothing prevents it, such as weakness of strength: or he is one who has happened something of those children, or an old man decrepit: or the time is vehement heat, or vehement cold indeed.
Then let him be content with his food, with cold herbs and the flesh of birds and hares: and forbid wine, and coarse meats, and filling oneself from foods and all food that fills the veins with blood. Until when you are secure from the hot abscess, and do not fear the effusion of matter to the limb: then let the sick person return to his first regimen with which he used to custom.
When therefore a broken bone begins to heal, it is necessary for the sick person to be fed with nourishing foods, much, thick, heavy, containing viscosity: such as food made from boiled wheat with water, and rice, and heads, and feet, and bellies of oxen, and eggs, and fresh fish: and thick wine, and things similar to these. For with this treatment clotting of a fracture occurs more quickly and better.
And know that fractured bones, when they are in old men who are in the first old age, and decrepit, it is not possible for them to be continued and consolidated according to their own nature always, because of the drying out of their limbs; and those that are consolidated and continued from them are at the end of their softness, like the bones of young children.
Nevertheless nature attaches something like glue to a broken bone from every side, which has thickness, so that it adheres and binds it until they are joined together, and bound together, until it comes to the end of strength and firmness, as it was in the first place, until nothing prevents it from its operations: and because of this cause it is necessary that you put food for the sick with heaviness, viscosity, and thickness, as we said.
And know that the kinds of fractures are diverse, according to the diversity of the limbs; for a fracture of the thigh is different from a fracture of the bone of the head: and a fracture of the bone of the chest is different from a fracture of the bone of the back: and similarly all the other limbs exist diversely one from another: and I have come with remembrance of every kind of fracture laid out in its own chapter distinct from another. And there are various kinds of bone fractures even within themselves.
Because it is fractured with an arc, besides which stars occur in it. And sometimes a fracture of it is made according to the length of the bone: and there are fractures of it stars, that is, splinters, and additions proceeding, as they are in a reed, when it is fitted with a knife to write. And if it does not proceed: and there is a fracture with a wound and disruption in the head: and there is a small fissure fracture.
And every kind has its own natural talent, or proper instrument in the restoration of it, according to what comes to mind, distinguished in its places.
And from that which is known to be a fracture of bone, is its crookedness, and its prominence: and its appearance to the sense, and its sound when you compress it with your hand.
When therefore there is no apparent twisting in place, nor a sound at your touch when you touch the bone with agitation, nor does the patient find great pain: then there is no fracture there, indeed it is possible that there is a twisting, or a small fissure: therefore it is not necessary that you move it with your hand and compression at all, rather put on top of it medicines of which remembrance comes, after the time that is convenient in place: then the constriction is tightened finely.
And know that when the place is bent and split into two, and besides this abscesses occur in it, nevertheless, all parts are removed from their counterparts: then you must begin at your hour for its rectification, by leveling it before a hot abscess occurs to it.
If a hot abscess occurs, then let it alone for several days, until the abscess is quiet. Then equal it with whatever means it can by gentleness and skill.
And know that its restoration and its equalization is easier than bones, in which wounds occur often.
Then bind it, according to how you will remember it.
If there are splinters in the bone, then it is necessary to extend this limb fractured from two parts, if it is a hand or foot: or with your hand, if the limb is small: or with two ropes, or with a rope and a hand.
And let your position be such that you place the limb upon a level place, according to its natural figure, until when the parts of the broken limb are extended; then endeavor, that you may return those additions with all skill, and according to whatever way you can in their places. And beware, according to your strength, lest there happens super infirmum through your operation pain, nor injury: and endeavor, according to your strength, that you apply one of the two bones with its companion, according to better forms.
And it is necessary at that hour, that you test and touch him with your hand. If therefore you see something different, rectify it, and equalize it by the quantity of your virtue.
And beware of vehement extension and strong compression, as many fools do. For many times they cause a hot abscess to happen by that operation of theirs, or a contraction in the limb: as we have already seen from their operations many times.
Then bind after setting, and a proper operation, and constriction of that limb, and rest, and tranquility: and warn the sick person about it. And make him guard himself so that he does not move himself at the hour of his vigils, and in his sleep, and at his revolution, and agitation, and at his excretion, and all his movements with his last virtue. And that it may prevent (the member) from being placed in a position that follows with pain. That is, because when he feels pain or injury in the arrangement of a limb's position, then it is necessary to change it to another arrangement from that with which he does not feel pain.
And be careful with it, that its position be equal and straight, so that there does not happen crookedness in the limb when it is restored.
And the quality of the constriction of the broken limb is, according to what I relate.
Know that broken limbs are diversified in their smallness, and their magnitude, and their form.
Therefore, those which are small, such as the arm, and finger, and foot, and similar to these, then it is necessary that there be a wrapping of soft cloth, fine. Those which are gross among them, such as the hip and leg, ought to have a wide, tough bandage applied to them.
Because the broad ligament connects the large limb and binds it on every part with an equal constriction so that it does not enter upon it a stain.
Then after your evacuation, begin to apply a suitable liniment to the equation, on soft wool over the place of the fracture itself. Then begin to wrap the bandage over the place of the fracture itself with three or four wrappings, according to what the limb deserves: and tighten your hand a little with the bandage.
Then go with him to the upper part from the place of the fracture, and tighten less than was your constriction, which you constricted the broken place. Then stretch yourself with the winding from the place of the fracture a little, and soften the stricture gradually, until you grasp a large thing from the healthy place.
Then take another strip, and again wrap it over the broken place with bandages. Thereafter go with the wrapping towards the lower part from the fracture. And let your operation be on stricture of involution, and its softening, according to what we said in the first superior involution.
Then place between the bandages lint or cloth, with which the curvature of the fracture may be matched, if it has curvature; and if not, do not put anything in it.
Then wind a bandage over the wound itself. Afterwards, equal superpositions of these involuted stars are made centrally, immediately, if there is no inflation in the place, nor a hot abscess.
If there is inflammation or an abscess in it, then place over it what sits upon that abscess and remove the inflammation, and leave it for several days. Then bind above it the very stars.
And let the Astrellae be from the middles of broad canes, scraped and wisely shaped; or let them be from wood from which sieves are made, which are made from pine, or geraid, that is, palm branches, or calingi: and let the breadth of every Astrella be according to this form, and this same figure.
Nonetheless, the asterisk placed over the very area should be thicker and a little wider than the rest of the asterisks: and the length of the asterisk should be according to what is permissible for the limb in terms of size and smallness.
Then tighten over the Astellas with another witta, according to the first tightening. Then bind above with threads properly, according to what we said about constriction: and let it be, that your constriction be above the place of the fracture more: and the less the constriction, the farther it is from the fracture.
And it is necessary that threads be of medium thickness and fineness, and that they be made of soft linen.
Because if the threads are thick, as I saw from the operation of foolish surgeons who put their threads from twisted hempen cords, it is a great error because the constriction falls with them outside of quality.
And very fine threads are not suitable again; because you do not attain through the stricture with them what you wish.
And it is not right that there be less than a finger's width between star and star.
If a sick person is injured by the extremities of the stars after constriction in healthy places: then put under them soft cotton or carmine wool until nothing is hurt from it.
When, however, there is a fracture with a wound, and rupture occurs in the skin, I shall come to it with remembrance of it in the second chapter according to singularity.
And know that not every broken limb ought to be bound with splints from the first day. That is, because when a limb is large, it is not necessary to place stars on top of it, unless after five days, or seven, or more, according to your security from the event of a hot abscess.
And this is the way of plasters which ancients used to restore, which are placed upon a fracture and dislocation.
The Method of a Common Plaster, Shortened for Restoring a Fracture, Suitable for Many Complexions, Especially Women and Children; because it is from that which does not conquer heat, nor cold.
And it is, as you take from the flour of the mill, and there is marrow of flour which hangs on the walls of the mill near the movement of the millstone: and you make it like this, except that sieves it with the membrane of an egg white: and put its preparation not thick, nor subtle. Then use it.
One method of poultice for restoring a fracture, dislocation, and torsion. Take  of mass, and laudanum, and acacia, and enula, and mugath; and juice of all, each  X, myrrh, and aloe both, each  V and alethil  XX. Grind all together, sift them, and mix with alethil water; or with egg white, if the patient's condition is heated. Then use this plaster. For it is of good composition, it restores broken bones quickly, and helps many people.
Another mode of plaster which also helps with fractures and twists. Take powdered mugwort, and meal, and white clay, each in quantity of X grains; myrrh, and aloes both in quantity of V grains; acacia VI grains; Armenian bolis XX grains; grind all together with much trituration, and sift them, and make into a paste with water, or egg white: and administer.
A method for plasters on joints and bones separated from their places, and it soothes pain that happens to them, and a crack in the bone, and a fracture. Take wool fleece and immerse it in vinegar and boiled oil, and place it over the area.
