And in this plaster there is no virtue of restoration, but it is good in the calming of a hot abscess; and it expels pains properly.
The Method of a Plaster for Restoring a Broken Bone. Take leaves of fig tree and leaves of wild poppy; and pound all, and make a plaster from both.
The method of abbreviated plaster application, administered at the restoration of a broken bone; and when you desire the resolution of the remaining abscess. Take the root of althea, and chamomile, and violets' flowers, and herb flour, all one part each: grind everything together, and mix it with wine, if the limb is not heated.
If it is heated, then apply it with moistened coriander water, or with water, and use it.
A stronger mode of plaster for resolution, than this first one, is used when a hard abscess occurs at the restoration of a limb. Take root of althea, and flaxseed, and balaustiae, and melilot, and tansy, and violet flowers, and chamomile, each one part, grind them all together, and make them with willow water, or with sweet water, or with wine. The whole thing according to the heat of the limb, and the quietness of its heat.
However Hippocrates did not say in his book that upon the restoration of a broken limb, anything should be placed upon it except for a plaster made from linen and oil: nothing else; and he narrated that it should be a medium between thickness and thinness.
Galen thought it was necessary to place on the broken limb during its restoration things that have dryness of heat, such as myrrh, and aloes and frankincense, and similar things to those which we have narrated.
A method of a poultice which is applied to injury and pain. Take mugwort, chickpeas, and human hair cut or feathers of a bird,  and plantain, and salt in equal parts; grind them and sift them, and make a plaster from it.
The quantity however according to what is proper, when the bandage should remain, then be dissolved, is, that you consider then, if it does not happen to the sick person pain, nor itching, nor is the broken bone moved from its place: then do not dissolve it for many days. If it happens to him in the place itching vehement, or unrest pain, or inflation: then hasten, and solve in an hour him, and remove the plaster from him: and take a soft cloth, or a soft sea sponge, humid: and submerge it in tepid water, and wash with it the place, until itching calms down, and sits the pain.
If it happens to him in the place itching vehement, or unrest pain, or inflation: then hasten, and solve in an hour him, and remove the plaster from him: and take a soft cloth, or a soft sea sponge, humid: and submerge it in tepid water, and wash with it the place, until itching calms down, and sits the pain. Then let the limb rest for an hour. Then place over it a cloth soaked in vinegar and oil, or rose oil: and bind over it at night one, until you are secure from the hot abscess, and the swelling of the limb rests, and its pain is removed.
Then bring him back again to the slight stricture, and a small plaster; and do not tighten him with your first constriction, and more subtle with him, until he heals.
If you see that the abscess, redness, pain, and swelling, and all accidents have been completely removed, and it is no longer painful from plaster or constriction, then treat it again as you did at first, equally well.
If nothing of those things which we have said happens to a limb, then do not unwrap it, unless after three, or four, or five, or seven days. And when it is released after twenty days, the whole thing, according to what appears to you from the solution of the limb, as we said, until when the fracture adheres and coagulation of flesh approximates upon it: add also then in stricture more than your first stricture altogether: and add also in the increase of the sick person's food, according to what preceded recollection of it.
If you see that the place of a fracture has already dried up and widened more than is necessary, then know that nourishment is prevented from reaching it. Therefore, embroider it with warm water at every turn where you have dissolved it, and let it be in all three days, and alleviate the stricture a little.
Therefore, by this operation nourishment runs to the limb and it is healed quickly.
That which foolish restorers do from a broken limb with another vice, when it is not restored at first, according to what is right, and restored according to crookedness, is an error from their operation, and great fear: and if it were straight, they would have said this in their books, ancients, and operated upon it.
And I did not see anywhere among them a trace of it, and it is better that it not be done.
On the making of an accident in the head.
There are diverse kinds of fractures, and its figures are many; and its causes are distinct.
For from a fracture is made which happens by a blow with a sword: and the blow takes away the bone entirely as far as it reaches to the siphac, which is under the bone, just as an ax does in wood: and for this reason this kind of fracture is called Axial.
Or if a sword strikes something of the bone and strikes its face, and the incision does not penetrate to its very end: this kind of fracture is called Kalaan, that is, absolute loosening.
And there is a wound of two fractures, either great or small.
And from a fracture is, which is crushing, or attrition: and its cause is percussion with a stone, or a fall onto a stone, or things similar to these.
And this fracture is also either penetrating, which already approaches the flesh under the bone; or it is on the face of the bone; and is a wound of these two fractures even small, or large.
And from a fracture is this one, which is hidden in the bone in the fineness of hair: and it is a small crack, and because of this this kind of fracture is named capillary.
And from these is a fracture, which is made by chance, or by the striking of a stone, and things similar to it, which cause the layer of bone to go into the inside, and concavity is made: as happens to kettles of air, when a blow occurs to them, and part of them goes into the inside.
And most of all this happens in the heads of damp bones, such as are the heads of children.
And with all these types of fractures, splints are made separate, and I come with a recollection of the whole thing in its place.
And we recognize all these kinds of fractures by detection upon it, and inquiry into them with probes, and removal of corrupted flesh which is upon them.
The type called capillary is recognized by this, that it is discovered over the bone, and removed, and smoothed over it with encaustum; for indeed a fracture appears black.
In the treatment of a fracture, first consider the patient's condition: then if you see from the conditions that signify clearly dangerous symptoms, such as vomiting of bile, and stretching, and loss of intellect, and cutting off of voice, and fainting, and acute fever, and discharge from the eyes, and redness of them, and similar to these in many dispositions, undoubtedly he is not healed: and if you see non-earthly conditions, and hope for salvation: then begin his treatment.
That is, if a wounded person comes to you first when he is wounded, and it happens on cold days, then you must strive for the removal of the bone before the fifteenth day according to all arrangements.
And if it is in the summer days, then it is necessary that you hurry to remove the bone before the seventh day, before what is under the bone from the flap spoils and those accidents happen which we narrated.
If a fracture of the bone has already reached the membrane touching the brain, and it is with small pieces, and attraction: then it is necessary, that you cut the broken part, and rubbed, according to what I tell you.
And it is, as you cut the head of the wounded: and cover over the bone according to any manner possible to you; and according to the figure of the wound, and what is light upon the weak.
If it happens to you in the detection of a bone that there is bleeding, or a hot abscess; then oppose it with what is fitting. And it is, that you fill the place with cloths soaked in wine, and rose oil: and make the wound rest until the abscess sits, and be secure from blood flow. Then begin in the cure of bone, and its removal.
And this is done according to one of two modes.
One way is to cut the jaw with a sharp, straight knife: and this is its form.
Then administer another incision after the incisions, a little wider than the previous ones: and this is its form.
Then administer also another incision wider than the second.
And the second method is that you have a number of different incisions, some wider than others, and some shorter than others: and let them be at the end of their sharpness, and let them be made of iron or lead: and use ease in striking upon the incision so that it does not strike the head and harm it.
If the bone is strong and hard: then it is necessary that you perforate around its edge, before you administer the cutting instrument, with boring instruments which are called Trebelles which do not go deep: and they are not named thus, unless because they themselves do not pass through the boundary of the bone to that which is behind it; for Terebellum has a rounded end above that which is below its sharp head, similar to a margin, and a small circle prevents submerging and passing through the thickness of the bone.
And it behooves you to take from these terebrums a great number, each one corresponding to the quantity of thickness of the bone, until you have for the present skull all terebrums: whose acute extremities' quantity in length and shortness shall be according to the quantity of thickness of that skull.
And this is the form of three kinds of Borers, or Trephines.
The quality of piercing around the broken bone is: that you place the borer over the bone, and turn it with your fingers until you know the bone has been pierced. Then move the terebinth to another place, and put it where it is between every hole the quantity of thickness of a radius, or about that.
Then cut with the Incisors which is between every foramen out of bone: and make it more delicately than you can over it from the end of levity, as we have said, until you eradicate the bone, or with your hand, or with some other of certain instruments which you have prepared for it: such as tenaculae, and fine forceps.
And it is necessary that you carefully avoid letting the terebrum or scalpel touch anything from the sheepskin.
When therefore you surround the bone by perforating it, and having separated it from the soft tissue if it has been continuous with it, and having extracted it, it is necessary that you smooth, and make equal the extremity of it which remains in the bone with another similar instrument incisor. Nevertheless, it ought to be thin and subtle more than other scalpels.
If anything remains of the small bones, and bandages: then take those with ease, with what you have among your instruments. Then care for the wound after that with ointments and what we have said.
And another mode of operation is easy, far from fear: which Galen said, and praised him with great praise: and this is his word. It is necessary for you first of all, to begin to uncover the part of the bone from the place in which the fracture is more violent and more evident: until when you have uncovered that part, and placed under it the extremity of this lenticular Incisor, whose this is the form.
And the lens-shaped part, it does not strike anything gently: and the pointed part of it, cutting into two sides of itself lengthwise, as you see: so that the lens-shaped part rests against the sphenoid bone, its sharp face incident upon the bone.
Then strike upon the incision with one side of a small hammer until you cut through the entire bone with ease, as it turns; and be secure from the cloth that no mishap will occur in it which you should fear at all.
And if the surgeon were not more foolish than men, and less ignorant of this, and were not asleep, then if something remained attached to the bone from the flesh in some places, he would cut it off from there with the pointed end of a lenticular knife, and free it from there with ease. It can be removed from him without harm, and without fear.
And if the bone fracture does not penetrate to the subcutaneous tissue, and what is cut off from the bone is its surface, and there remain in it roughness and subtle chips: then it is necessary that you scrape that roughness, and eradicate those chips with fine razors of which you have prepared a number of different sizes, so that it will be possible for you to administer them in every suitable place, and make them straighter, according to what the operation leads you to, and the broken bone, and its shape. And it is necessary that you administer in your cutting, where in first rays of bone, wider those of razors. Then use a finer one after that.
And it is necessary that you administer in your cutting, where in first rays of bone, wider those of razors. Then use a finer one after that. And do not cease doing this according to continuity, until you come to administering finer and more subtle of them all.
In every small fissure or capillary fracture, one must administer care according to that which leads to its straightening.
And there is a thing that is not hidden from everyone who studies this art, and it stands upon what we have written and shown in major fractures.
If however the subcutaneous tissue is exposed and remains when you eradicate the bone, then it is necessary that you take a linen cloth, according to the quantity of the wound, and submerge it in rose oil, and place it over the opening of the wound. Then take another doubled or tripled cloth and immerse it in wine and rose oil, and place it over the first cloth: and do this as easily as you can so that it does not chafe it.
Then administer above this wide ligament: and do not tighten it unless by the quantity which holds the wound only: and release it with this care on one day, or two days, until you are certain about the absence of a hot abscess. Then dissolve it and administer after that medicines with which there is superfluous dryness; such as lily roots, and herb flour, and frankincense powder, and asarabacca, and things similar to these: and let it be, so that you make a powder out of these medicines, which is powdered over the wound like those that are dry.
And universally administer in it all medicine of its property that it may cleanse and not bite. And care according to your skill, so that the wound in your treatment may be clean; and let there not be in it filth of ointment, nor uncleanness.
And care according to your skill, so that the wound in your treatment may be clean; and let there not be in it filth of ointment, nor uncleanness. Do not allow the virus to gather in it at all; for when the virus gathers above the brain's padding, it spoils and putsrefies it, and a great evil happens to the sick after that. And this happens in the head's siphac, when it is cleansed from it os, especially when care of it is neglected, blackness on its surface.
Therefore consider, if misfortunes of the kind we have said happen to the sick person, know that it is undoubtedly harmful.
And if blackness does not occur, unless from the medicine which is placed upon it, and it is in the virtue of that medicine, to make that blackness: then it is necessary, that you take one part of honey, three parts of rose oil, and linen cloth with them. Then place it on a sieve: afterwards care for it with appropriate kinds of treatment until it heals.
On the restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that the parts of the nose are not broken except the two upper ones together, or one of them; because the lower part is cartilaginous and does not break, and it happens to it only attrition, and twisting, and things like these.
If one of the two parts is broken, then it is necessary that you introduce a small finger into the nostril opening and equalize that fracture with your index finger and thumb until the nose returns to its natural shape; and let this be done skillfully and easily. And beware lest through your operation that pain befalls the weak.
If the fracture is in the upper part of the nose and the finger cannot reach it, then it is necessary to level it with the end of the radius, where there is a small thickness.
If a fracture is in two parts, then make a likeness of it; and hasten with its restoration on the first day of the fracture, if it is possible. And if not, then after the seventh or tenth day at rest of the hot abscess.
Then introduce into the extremity of the Nose a liniment made from linen cloth, if the fracture is in one part: or introduce two ligatures if the fracture is in two parts: and let the ligature be of such thickness as to fill the nostril opening.
And some of the ancient restorers said that they should be infused with linen in butter and changed every day. It does not appear to me, indeed it ought not, that licinium should be poured into the albuginea, after being mixed with mill powder. Then dismiss the splint until the bone is firm and the cartilage hardens.
And sometimes a goose feather is inserted into the nostril in place of a splint, after it has been wrapped with a soft cloth: and it is done so that it holds the fracture of the nose more firmly, and so that the sick person is not prevented from breathing.
And this thing is not necessary. If you wish, do this; and if you wish, do that other thing.
If it happens that the nose again becomes hot during the operation of a boil, apply a plaster to the nose with cauterium, or with cotton wool soaked in rose oil, or a small amount of dyacylon.
If a hot abscess does not occur, then it is necessary to cover it with a plaster made of sesame meal and the powder of frankincense mixed with egg white. Then place a soft wad on top of it, and do not apply anything at all to the nose.
If the bones of the nose are broken in a small fracture or splintered, then it is necessary to cut over them and remove them with instruments which are suitable for this purpose. Then suture the incision, and treat it with what is becoming, that it may become flesh and consolidate from suitable plasters or ointments.
If a wound occurs between the nose, then it is necessary to cure it with linens: and administer a lead tube until it heals.
On the restoration of the lower jaw when it is broken.
When the lower jaw is broken, and the fracture is not with a wound, then look if it is only a break on the outside, and does not break in two, and is curved inwards: then its knowledge is easy.
Therefore, if there is a fracture in the right part, you must insert your left index finger into the bone of the injured: similarly, if the fracture is in the left jawbone, then introduce the index of your right hand: and drive with it the prominence of the fracture inward with ease to the outside with your other hand from the inner part of the bone centrally, and with it equalize it.
If the fracture of the jawbone has already been separated into two parts, then it is necessary for you to administer extension from both parts, according to the straightness; until it is possible to equalize it.
If a tooth has been shaken or broken, then bind those of which you hope will remain with a thread of gold, or silver, or silk. Then place a splint over the broken jawbone. Then place upon that doubled cloth, and place above the cloth a large splint made centrally: or a piece of sole leather equal in length to the mandible.
Then bind it above, according to how the bandage is prepared for you, and it is fitting that its application should be made until it is destroyed: and order the sick person, that he may have peace and quiet: and put food of his intake soft.
If, however, you estimate that something is changing in form in some way: then hasten, and dissolve it on the third day. Then correct what has been altered from it, and apply a plaster to it from ground powder, with albuginea or with sesame flour, after you have removed the caper. And place wool on top of the plaster.
While therefore that plaster remains attached to it, and the arrangement of the bone does not change, let him release it, so that he may not loosen it, until it is healed: or bind the fracture. This fracture is often set in three weeks.
If meanwhile a hot abscess occurs, then administer that which we have said many times for its suppression, until the abscess is removed.
And if the fracture is with a wound, consider. Now the fragment is separated from the bone, or from the saw: then be more careful in this, so that you may remove those fragments of the saw with that which is convenient to you in their removal from the instruments.
If however the opening of the wound is narrow, then widen it with a surgical knife according to the quantity of necessity.
Then when you have removed all the scabs and there is nothing left of them, then close the wound opening if it is wide; and if not, place over it from cloths which are suitable for it, and cover it until it heals.
On the restoration of the collarbone when it is broken.
The collarbone is broken most frequently from the front part from the side of the shoulder, and its fracture occurs according to one of three modes.
Or else it is broken and separated into two, besides which things may happen in it; and it is easier to restore it.
Or so it happens in bone fractures; and it is more difficult to restore.
Or so, which is a fracture with a wound.
And the operation on it when there is a fracture without a wound is, that you have two assistants present, so that one of them holds the instrument that follows the fractured fragment, and the other extends the neck towards the side.
Then equalize the fracture with your fingers until it forms its proper shape, so that there is neither prominence nor concavity in it.
If the indiges extends more, then it is necessary that you place under the patient's sphere a piece of cloth, or wool: and let its size be according to the quantity of your necessity: and extend, and elevate the furculum, and constrict the sphere with your hand, until the fracture is equal, as is necessary.
If you are unable to draw the extremity of the clavicle outwards because it has reached deep inside, then it is necessary for the patient to lie on their neck and place a pillow medium in size under their shoulder. Have an assistant press the patient's shoulder downwards until the clavicle that is in the depths is raised upwards. Then straighten the fracture and align it with your fingers.
If you feel a splinter from the clavicle, and it moves: then it is necessary that you find it above it, and extract that splinter with ease.
If it is held in the bone, then devise an incision into it with one of the Incisors that you have prepared for it, after you have placed under the Furcula the instrument which saves sifach; and it is an instrument out of wood, or out of iron. And this is its form.
It is similar to a drinking horn that has no concave shape.
And let its width be according to that which you require it from the magnitude of the bone, and its smallness. The length of it also according to what is possible for you to the operation.
And it should have two extremities, as you see; and one extremity be wide, and the other narrow.
If, however, the wound that you opened when you extracted the bone fragment is wide and you fear an abscess from heat, then bring together the two edges of the wound with a suture.
And if the rupture is small, or a hot abscess threatens: then fill the wound with pads and lint according to the size of the wound's cut.
If a hot abscess occurs, then pour linen dipped in rose oil, vinegar, and wine onto it and place it on top.
A stricture of bone when it is without a wound and without a section, is, as you put upon the bone a plaster made from mill flour with the white of egg. And place a soft plug upon it. Then place a sphere under its [titillicum], if it does not fit. Then take a hemamat, that is, a wheel which the Saracens hold, which should be long quite a bit; and let its width be that of one palm, or about that. Then place a small doubled pad over the cotton and plaster. Then take a star from a fine board, whose width is three fingers, and in length likewise. Then wrap it in a cloth. Then wrap that cloth with a star in the place of the splints which falls over the fracture and place. Then place a splint on the fracture as it revolves, and wrap it over its neck, and under its healthy collarbone, and under its injured collarbone: and reduce it over all parts, and how you see that constriction holds the fracture, constricting properly: and this is what is not hidden from you, and the whole, so that it is not removed star from above the broken bone. And for this reason it is necessary that you look at the sick person every day. Whenever therefore the ligament is softened, and you see the starlet already being removed: then straighten it, and tighten the ligament.
Then place the sleep of the patient upon his back, and place under his pillow near his sleep a small cushion at night, so that when he is lifted it may aid him from his side. Therefore, a fracture of the clavicle is raised by elevation of the shoulder.
Either bind the arm to the neck of the person: and do not loosen the bandage unless an accident happens in the place from itching, or from a boil, up to twelve days.
Then renew the dressing, if you see its face suitable for it. And add in the stricture, and release it, until it is restored and coagulated.
And a fracture of the clavicle is bound and supported for as many days as twenty-eight; and sometimes it happens in some men in less time than that.
On the restoration of a spatula fracture.
A spatula breaks rarely, and in a wide place of it: and the margins of it only break from it.
When therefore that place is broken from it, and broken in the middle of it, and it is not known except by touch: then according to what the fracture's figure is, attempt its preparation. And reduce it back to its natural form in every way possible to you.
Then place over the place powder of mill with albumen of egg, and a soft plug, and place on top a small piece of doubled cloth. Then place upon it a broad board from a table according to the quantity of the whole spatula, or slightly larger than it.
If, however, there is a hollow in some places of the spatula under the star, then level that hollow with soft cotton wool until the star descends according to equality.
Then bind it above with a long linen cloth, using a tight but not excessive constriction, and firming the splint until it is no longer movable from its place.
And consider the ligament every day. Whenever therefore the ligament is softened, tighten it, and equal the bone if it is moved from its place; and let the sick person lie on his healthy side.
And a spatula indeed is restored in twenty days, or in twenty-five days.
When therefore this number is complete, then loose the ligament: and you be in safety. For it is from bones whose union is not feared, nor their separation.
If it proceeds from bone in a fragment and punctures under the skin: then cut over it and remove it. And do what we have said for a fracture of the clavicle from the heat of an abscess, if anything happens from it.
On the restoration of a broken breastbone.
The chest sometimes breaks in the middle of itself, and this rarely happens. The extremities of this are indeed broken and splintered very much by accidents to it.
When the middle of it is broken, which inclines to the lower parts, and there happens to it a severe pain, and difficulty in breathing and coughing: and perhaps he vomits blood, and the concavity in the broken bone is declared, and is not concealed from the sense, Restoration of it is, that he who is ill lies on his back: and put between the spatulas of his ribs.
Then press his shoulder bones and bring the ribs together from two parts or sides with your hands: and with subtlety in its alignment, according to every way that it is possible and fitting, until it returns to the shape of the bone, as it ought.
Then place upon it a dressing and a plug, and place above it a plate from thin willow board, or calamine, and from things similar to these in lightness, after you have wrapped it in cloth. Then bind it carefully over the fractured bone, so that it does not move: and go with the bandage according to the roundness towards the back several times, and tighten it with a decent constriction.
Then consider the bandage at all hours, and whenever it softens, tighten it. And if necessity causes it to be loosened because of itching, or corrosion that happens in the place, or pain, or abscess: then hasten, and loosen it, and eradicate the plaster, and correct what happened with the way of its treatment.
Then reduce the plaster if you see it forming that face: and bandage it tightly until it heals.
On the restoration of ribs when they are broken.
Know that a fracture of the ribs does not occur except in the thick places which follow the back, and from their extremities on the front part. And they do not suffer attrition, unless because they are cartilaginous. And its knowledge is not concealed from the sense during examination with the fingers.
And restoration of it is made so that the fracture with the fingers is equal according to the possible manner, until the figure is equal, according to what is necessary. Then apply plasters, and bind the broken bone with a splint if it is needed.
And if the fracture of the ribs is sloping inwards: then there happens to the sick person a very severe pain, and a piercing, like the piercing which happens to him who has pleurisy: because the bone pierces the membrane, and also it happens to him difficulty in breathing, and coughing, and vomiting blood much: and this is difficult to cure.
And now the ancients have been ingenious in this matter with many ingenuities: and among them are those who say, that you should place foods in the sick man which generate inflammation and windiness, so that the belly may be inflamed, and extended, and the fracture driven to the exterior.
We however abhor that it not be a cause for quickly making a hot abscess, if it does not happen. If this has already happened, then he adds to it.
And some of them said: a wind-filled vessel should be placed over the place, then it should be suctioned with force, and this is similar to reasoning. Nevertheless, it is feared that it will attract windy superfluities to the place because of the predisposition of weakness.
And some of them said that the place ought to be covered with wool which has already been immersed in hot oil: and let little pads be placed between the ribs until it is filled: so that there may be an equal bandage when you wrap it according to the roundness.
Then the sick person is treated with treatment for pleurisy from food and medicine.
If the weak body is distressed by something too great for it to bear and the bone pierces the flesh with a piercing point, and we fear for the weak one: then it is necessary that we cut above the place and uncover from the broken rib. Then we place upon it an instrument that preserves the wound's edges, of which the mention preceded; and we cut the bone with ease, and cut it.
Then we shall bring together the two lips of the wound, if it is large, with a suture: and we shall care for it with plasters, until it heals.
If it happens that a hot abscess occurs in the meantime, then hasten and pour in grains in rose oil, and place it over the spot: and let the patient be treated with what is fitting for an abscess.
Meanwhile, he is also laid on his side on which he sleeps until it heals.
On the restoration of a fracture of the spinal vertebrae of the back and neck.
Sometimes a fracture of the bones of the neck occurs, and rarely attrition happens to them; and similarly with the vertebrae of the back also. When therefore it happens to someone, and you want to know, whether he will be healed or not, then trust. If you see his hand already softened and numbed and dead: and it cannot move them, nor extend them, nor constrict them: and when you constrict them with your fingers, and prick them with a sharp point, he does not feel it, and does not feel pain in them: then know that it is not healing, and in many dispositions it is mortal.
If he moves them and feels a tightening in them with his fingers and pricking, then know that the nape of the bone is safe, and that the sick person will heal with treatment.
If it happens to the vertebrae of the back, similar to this, and you wish to know if it will heal or not: then consider his feet: then if you see that they are softened, and what happened in the hands has happened to them: then when he lies on his back, wind and involuntary defecation occur: and when he lies on his stomach, involuntary defecation occurs: and when he lies on his back and wants to urinate but cannot: then know that it is mortal. Therefore, do not labor in its cure.
If nothing of that sort happens to him, the matter is lighter.
And the cure for this which happens from it is, that you try sedation of the hot abscess: so, that you put upon the rubbed (or, anointed) spine only rose oil: or with beaten eggs saturated with wine.
Place on top of it several times a day until the hot abscess settles. Then place one comforting, drying plaster over the spot and bind it firmly with a bandage: and order the patient to use rest and tranquility. Nor should he sleep upon the part where he found pain until it is healed.
If it happens that in rubbing a bone is shattered, or something which is already separated from it: then it is necessary, that you cut above the skin, and remove it. Then gather together the two lips of the wound, if it is large, with suture. Then care for it with some ointment until it heals.
If the last bone of the thigh is broken and reaches the end of the tail, then you must insert your thumb of your left hand into the anus, and with your right hand, equalize the broken bone as best as possible.
Then place upon it, and remove it: and care for the wound with the care that preceded, until it heals.
On the restoration of the anus.
Chapter XI.
Bones of Anchars are rarely broken. If these are broken, then their fracture is not, except for a crushing at the extremities of them: and they are split lengthwise, and they incline inward: and there occurs in the injured place pain and a puncture: and the leg that breaks becomes numb.
And the restoration of it is that you stretch your hand over it until you stand upon the fracture as to its figure.
If the fracture is only in the extremities of it, then equal that fracture, according to what is prepared for you from the equation, until it assimilates to its natural figure.
If, however, the fracture is lengthwise, or already inclines inwards: then make the sick person lie on their stomach, until you prepare the restoration of that fracture. When therefore you have leveled it, place on top of it a plaster; then place on top of it a splint of wood or skin: and bind tightly with a bandaging which you do not fear will cause rearrangement of the fracture, nor removal of the splint.
And an equal concavity of them with that which fills it, until it takes it tight according to equality: and order the infirm, that he sleep upon his back, or upon his healthy side.
If however it happens that a hot abscess occurs to him, then cease from its extension and restoration until the hot abscess settles: and place upon it that which has been said before, then return to its restoration: and tighten it as is fitting.
If, however, a splinter has entered the bone, or it is broken, remove it.
On the restoration of a broken bone with assistance
That which is between the hip and the head is a spatula helpful.
If therefore it is broken, then its restoration is according to one of two modes.
One of these is: that you take a plank of wood of medium thickness, according to this form which follows.
And two ligaments shall be tied at both extremities.
Then let it be suspended from an elevated place: and let the sick person sit upon a seat. Then he should throw his broken arm over a piece of wood until it reaches the place where the curve of the wood is attached in the middle.
Then suspend something heavy from above so that it extends the instrument downward. Then the physician sets the fracture with his hands until the fracture returns, as is necessary.
And another way is that the sick person be laid on his neck, and his hand be suspended from his neck with a band. Then command two assistants to hold one of them who is above the fracture with his hands: and let the other hold below it, and let each of them extend to his own part.
And if you wish it to have stronger extension, then bind below the fracture with a ligament, and above it with another ligament: and each of the two attendants shall extend it to their own parts.
If therefore the fracture is near the extremity of the shoulder, then it is necessary that one ligament be placed under the little bone, and the other under the fracture towards the elbow.
And similarly if the fracture is near the elbow: then it is necessary that a ligament be placed over that place, and over the elbow itself.
Then equalize the fracture with ease without effort, until the fracture is made equal as it should be, and apply a good application: then bind it, if there does not occur a hot abscess.
If a hot abscess occurs, then release the constriction until the seventh day. And place upon it wool dipped in vinegar and rose oil, until when the abscess is quiet: then bind it; and the way of binding it is, as you would place a plaster over the fracture.
Then place a bandage made of fresh cloth over the dressing. Then he will bring the arm above his helper, and place his open hand upon his shoulder.
And place a cloth, and bind over the auxiliary arm, so that the arm may stand in place of the stars; if nothing prevents you from this, and nothing is altered upon you from the broken bone.
If you are afraid that something will be changed on you from a broken bone, then use splints; and it is, that you place over the fracture itself the splint which is wider and stronger than the other splints. And place between every splint the width of a finger: and let the length of the splints be according to the fracture with an addition of three fingers from every part.
Then tighten over the stars the constriction that I said at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is, that your constriction be above the place of fracture stricter. And the more elongated the fracture is, let the constriction be less.
If therefore you see the position of stars and constriction, as we have said, in the hour when you restore the limb immediately do this: and if you fear a hot abscess, then release the constriction and stars until the seventh day, as we have said.
Then consider the ligament in all three days, so that it does not happen in the place itching, or inflation, or food is prohibited from reaching the limb, because of the arrangement of superfluity of constriction. Therefore straighten it as we have said.
If you are over confident, so that nothing may happen from it: then do not loosen the ligament, unless after three days, or more.
And let the patient's sleep be upon his back, and his hands upon his stomach: and put under the assistance a cushion full of wool equally.
And consider at every hour of night, or day, that the figure of a broken limb be not loosened, nor the ligament softened. Therefore rectify it with your virtue.
And place food for the sick according to the order which we have previously given, so that it be fine food at first, until he begins to tighten his mouth; then it is necessary that you make his food coarser.
According to custom, the aid and leg should be bandaged for forty days; therefore, it is necessary that it be untied, and a bath be administered, and it be medicated with ointments which straighten it.
If the fracture is foul and rubbed, then you do not separate it from its ligaments and splints for up to fifty days, and up to two months.
On the restoration of a broken arm.
The arm is composed of two bones which are called the two radii.
One of these is small: and it is that which follows the thumb. And another is large, and it is that which is placed below the small one from the lower part.
Perhaps therefore a larger weapon breaks alone, or a small one: and perhaps both break at once.
When therefore a small upper bone is broken, its restoration is easy, and its healing is easier.
And when the lower bone is broken, it is a bad fracture, and its healing is difficult.
And it is worse when two bones are broken at the same time.
If therefore the bone that is broken is little above the socket, then it behooves the physician at its restoration, to place a small extension with ease, until it equalizes itself.
If however the fracture is large, then it is necessary to put a stronger bandaging.
And it is necessary that he place the figure of the hand at its restoration, and its extended extension over the pillow. And let the thumb of the hand be higher than all the fingers; and let the lower joint be higher than all the fingers, or the rest: and let the weak one sit square upon itself, and let the pulley be before it, or in its direct line, in altitude, so that the weak one does not incur labor.
Then let the assistant extend the arm from the lower part, either with his hand, or with a bandage. And another assistant shall extend similarly from above.
Then the physician aligns the bone until he restores it to the best possible shape.
If, in a fracture of bone, there are fragments, then try to reduce every fragment back to its place with your skill.
If however it appears to be separated and stabbing the skin, and there is no hope in its restoration, then find over it, and remove it according to the manner which we have said in those things which precede.
If, however, the fracture is with a wound, I have already done so to that particular chapter, therefore take care of it from there.
If it happens at the beginning of your restoration that a hot abscess occurs, then line cloth with a cauterized piece made from rose oil and white wax, and let it be medium between thickness and thinness: and tightly bind the cloth over it with a fine bandage until when the abscess settles: then remove the cauterized piece, and place a plaster prepared from ground powder with egg white. Then place splints, and let the splint placed over the fracture itself be somewhat wider and stronger.
And know that the number of stars on the arm is six in various arrangements: whether it be a fracture in one fossa, or in two fossae at once.
Then place your splint over the fracture site stronger and more firm, and a little more with the splint to the upper part of the fracture, or to the lower part of it; or place a softer splint, its edge slightly less, according to what was remembered before at the beginning of the chapter.
And let cloths be wound over the fracture, soft, moist cloths, and not hard, rough ones. And let the thread for tightening be made of linen, of medium thickness between fine and coarse, as we have recounted.
And consider the limb and the ligament after days. If something happens which requires correction, such as an itch that happens to a limb: then it is necessary to embrocate the limb with hot water until the itching subsides, and let go of the limb not constricted, until it subsides, then repeat the constriction.
If the constriction has already been softened, and the bone has already been removed, and similar things to these: then correct it all with your skill, and consider it again.
If nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb because of the excess tightness, then it is necessary to soften it a little and let it go for a few days until it heals to the nourishment, then tighten it again.
If, however, nothing of what we said happens to the sick person, then it is not necessary to loosen (it), unless after twenty days, or about so many.
Then the hand of the infirm person shall be suspended to his neck. And let its neck be equal, and may it be guarded by its virtue from agitated motions. And make him lie on his back.
And know that this fracture is restored in thirty days, or in thirty-two days: and perhaps it is restored in twenty-eight days. All of it is according to dispositions of complexions, and dispositions of virtue.
On the restoration of fractured hands.
Know that fractures of the fingers and the knuckles are rare. And this does not happen to him unless through much rubbing.
When therefore occurs a fracture of the foot, or attrition: then it is necessary that the sick man should sit square and in front of him a seat according to equality. Then place his hand upon it extended.
Then let the assistant extend the broken bones, and the physician straighten them until they are joined together in a good connection.
Then it is necessary that he place a plaster and a wad, if there does not occur a hot abscess. Then put a splint above according to the amount of space: and you have already wrapped it in linen cloth.
If the fracture is near the lower part of the foot, then make a sphere like shape out of cloth: and order the sick person to bind it over the broken foot: then bind it with a long strip of cloth.
Let a pad be made of leather, which has some softness, so that it can be applied to the skin with the body of the plant, and bind it as is necessary.
If the fracture is external, then you must: place one splint above and another below in the sole of the foot; and let the hand be open and straight. Then place a ligature as one turns the hand; and extend it between the fingers with a band.
If a fracture happens to one of the joints of the fingers, then if it is the thumb, it shall be set according to what is necessary. Then it is bound with a plant, and if you please, put in for it a straight spindle, small: that the fracture may be raised up, and not moved.
If the fracture is in the remaining fingers, such as the middle, index, or little finger, and ring finger, then it should be aligned and bandaged with the healthy finger that follows it; or all of them should be bandaged according to their continuation; and this is better. Or place a small bandage over it, as we said about the thumb.
And consider in your restoration, and after the same arrangement of the hot abscess: and oppose with it what is necessary, when something happens from it, according to what the narrative of its recurrence says.
On the restoration of a fractured hip.
The hip bone is broken many times and is made known to the senses because it turns to the inner and outer part.
And restoration of it is made so that a ligament is tightened above the fracture, and another ligament below the fracture. And the sick person should lie down upon his face.
Then he shall extend the entire ligament to its own part according to equality: and this, when there is a fracture in the middle of the bone.
If it is a fracture near the root of the hip bone, then one ligature should be to the side of the wingbone, so that extension goes upwards; and another ligature below the fracture.
And thus similarly if there is a fracture near the knee, then let the ligament near the knee be, so that it extends to the lower parts.
Then the physician straightens the bone with both of his hands, until he brings it back according to the likeness of the natural figure: and the bone is joined together with a good joining.
Then it is necessary, that you place a plaster and a constriction, if an abscess does not occur in the limb. If it happens that an abscess occurs there, then leave it alone for some days until the abscess settles and becomes warm, then return to your treatment.
Its stricture should be so. It is necessary that you wrap a hard bandage over the fracture, namely, twice or three times; and let there remain from it an excess. Then wrap the leg until it reaches the heel at the root of the foot.
And introduce a long thread between the hip and leg near the knee to the lower part, and let the ends of the thread come from the upper part of the two parts. Then roll over the Leg and Hip what is surplus from the meal.
Then place upon the hip at the site of its fracture a splint, and place one splint from it on the bone of the leg.
Then fill the cavities between the thigh and hip with soft cloths so that the tightness is equalized.
Then begin with a strong constriction in the middle above the fracture site for three, or four turns. And the more elongated it is with the ligament from the place of the fracture, the stricter and smoother, and gentler will your constriction be.
Then attend to the two extremities of the thread that you introduced between the hip and leg. Therefore, tie with both three stars which are above. Then go with two ends of thread to the lower part until you reach the heel of the foot. And when both are tightened also the extremities of the stars from the other part: so that the ligament may not be moved from its place.
Then loosen the constriction over it while it remains, so that it does not happen to the member, corruption, or abscess, or inflation, and similar things to these. If something happens from that. Therefore, with its solution, and rectify that which happened from it altogether, according to what we have said many times.
If there is a piece of bone within a bone that pricks, then it is necessary for you, if it is possible, to make it equal. And if not, find upon it, and remove it: and care with him whose precedent recollection, until it is healed.
And sometimes this fracture of the hip is tightened, besides which stars are added to it, as we said in the aid and arm.
However, in the restoration of it thus broken, lameness does not occur in the weak person. And if only one leg is restored, besides applying another leg to it, then it is necessary that the one whose it is will always limp.
And know that the hip is bound for fifty days, or a little is added, or a little is taken away: all this is according to the diversity of constitutions and other arrangements.
On the restoration of a broken kneecap.
Know that a fracture of the kneecap rarely occurs, but attrition happens there often.
If a fracture occurs to him, then not unless a fissure or hole in his parts. And it is that with a wound, and without a wound. And it is placed over the whole with sense.
And its restoration is so that it may be equal to what was broken from its parts with the fingers until it is brought together, and joined according to what is possible from equality and ease, and fabrication.
Then place a plaster of sesame meal, or millet flour, with egg white: and place on top of it a round splint, if the latter is necessary: and tighten from above with a suitable bandage to that.
Then consider all the arrangements that we have related in other fractures: such as a hot abscess, and similar ones: so, that you may oppose every accident to what is suitable for it, until it heals.
On the restoration of a broken leg.
Know that there are two bones in the leg, one of which is thick and called Femur; and the other thin and called Fibula.
And it happens to both kinds of fractures that happen to the two bones of the arm: and because of this restoration is made of it, just as the restoration of the arm equally, and the operation is one.
If two bones are broken at once, the leg turns in every direction.
And if a bone is broken more subtly, the leg is turned to the front part.
And if a thick bone is broken, you will find it below; and it is among those which are not hidden from you.
Then administer extension, and equation, and ligation of the stars according to that equally.
Nevertheless, if a fracture of the leg is a foul fracture, having many fragments: that the extension be less and with gentler alleviation.
And in the leg indeed there is something from the operation above the arm: and it is, that when you equalize the stars, and compile all your operation, then take two fascias from pine wood, which is administered in the making of sun dials that are put under tables: and they are made from palm spatulas: and choose from them those which have small thickness, and do not be from the smallest: and let their length be according to the length of the leg from the knee to below.
Then wrap over each piece of cloth two foldings according to its length. And place one part of the thigh from one side, and the other from the other side: and let them be from the knee down to the foot.
Then tie two fasciae in three places from the two extremities and middle. When, however, constriction is prevented, the limb leans to the right and left, and stands in good position.
And now indeed let a splint be made from wood according to the length of the leg, and lay the leg in it so that it will keep it from moving.
And most importantly, you must do this in a fracture when there is also a proper wound with it.
Then consider the bone on every day, and study it with utmost diligence from abscess, or inflammation, or other ailments. If therefore something of these things happens, then oppose it with that which is necessary, until it is healed, if God wills.
On the fracture of bones of the foot and fingers.
A fracture of the calcaneus does not occur completely.
Sometimes a fracture occurs in the bones of the foot, and sometimes also in the fingers. And it does not occur to them except by attrition in many positions.
If a fracture of the bones of the foot occurs and you see those bones rising above each other, then let the sick person put his foot on the prepared ground, as if walking.
Then stand, and put your foot upon that which rises from those bones: afterwards press, or tread upon it: and make it even, until it returns to its places.
Then place a plaster, and a wad on top: and place under the foot a small board with two flat heads.
Then bind it with a foot band of good measure, after you have wrapped it with cloths and whatever else it needs.
When therefore three days or four have passed, then loosen the ligament. For you will find bones that are equal, whether they are broken or displaced. The operation therefore is, as you see.
If some fingers are broken, then restore them and make them equal according to what I have told you in the restoration of the fingers of the hand. Then place your finger like a small stick in accordance with the length of your finger, and let it be slightly wider; then place under the foot this table which I have told you about, and tighten it with a decent constriction.
If however that which is broken from the bones is two, or three, or more, then place over every finger a starlet of its own quantity, wrapped in linen cloth: and bind the board to the lower foot: and let it stand on one head of the board outside the foot's belly, so that it may hold good retention.
And it behooves you, that you do not forget, but consider all that I have said to you in the remaining fractures from accidents which we have discussed: and oppose every accident with what is fitting.
On a fracture of the woman's vulva, and of the breastbone, and of the male thigh bone.
When a woman's vulva is fractured, then make her sit squarely. Then incline it toward the back part of him a little, and it shall be held back.
Then the midwife fills the womb with cotton until it is full; and let a sphere be made in the womb from it.
Then beat the woman and raise her back gradually. This one comes out again, until it is made before the gate of the vulva like a sphere, and the broken bone returns.
Then place a small thing upon his back. When therefore he wants to urinate, remove the catheter with ease, then let him urinate: and replace it according to the way you inserted it at first: then return to his bed.
Let him do this for seven days, or thereabouts, for the bone itself is being restored.
And if you wish, take a sheep's bladder and place over its opening a reed tube, and introduce the whole bladder into its vulva. Then blow into the tube with force until the bladder within the Vulva is inflated, for the fracture will return.
Then it should be filled with a poultice, and stand for several days, according to what we have said, until it is healed.
And when the bone of the breast of a man or woman is broken, then administer in its restoration and its setting that which we have narrated about the bone of the hip.
And the rectum is not concealed in these nearby fractures, which happen little, especially to one who has some study or use of it.
And understand this book of mine very well, because the fracture itself signifies to you in many arrangements the straight way in the restoration of itself, and its constriction: therefore understand.
When a man's phallus breaks, take a goose gullet and insert it into it. Then place over it a bandaging from cloth and bandage it, and let it be left for three days, or about that many, and it will heal if God wills.
On the restoration of a broken bone when there is a wound.
It is necessary for one who has a fracture with a wound, especially if it is large, such as the hip bone, or of the arm, or similar to that, to act quickly, and immediately have him bled; if the conditions for bleeding are present, as we have previously stated.
If a wound is flowing blood, then you should hasten to its severing, so that powdered earth may be placed upon it, if you have nothing else.
Then begin to restore the fracture on that very day; and do not postpone it, unless an abscess occurs.
If however a hot abscess occurs, then dismiss its restoration until the ninth day, until the abscess settles. And you will not approach him on the third day, and on the fourth at all, for you would cause misfortune to happen to him.
If however the broken bone is protruding above the skin and exposed: then it is necessary that you try to reduce it, and level it with your hands with ease and small extension.
If, however, it is not prepared extension of it, and its equality by your hands; then reduce it with this instrument. And there is an instrument which is made of iron, whose length is the quantity of seven fingers, or eight: and its width is according to the quantity of the wound. Therefore, it is necessary for a physician to take from them three, or four, according to the quantity which he needs for that curing in every kind of fracture.
And let it be round: and let there be a small thickness in it, so that it is not doubled upon itself during compression at the time of the operation: and let it have a sharp extremity, which has an edge at its extremities. And let its upper part be equal in thickness from the middle to the lower part.
And this is its form.
It is necessary in this operation that the sharp end be placed upon the prominent end of the bone, and to drive it with one vice until when the bone returns and becomes equal to some equation.
Therefore, try to equalize the extremities of the fracture against each other.
If the extremities of it are broken finely, and does not receive it well with the instrument, then it is necessary that you cut the extremity of that bone until the instrument has power over it.
If you cannot restore them with what we have narrated at all, then cut it with what is fitting from the incisions which we have said; or saw it with a saw in any way possible to you.
Then rasp that which remains in the bone from roughness and tiny bark.
When therefore the bone is set, and the weak one finds after its setting a violent pain that wounds, then know that the bone has not returned to its natural place.
Do therefore that. For you will bring great injury to the infirm limb when your restoration is completed which you make of bone.
Then immerse the cloth in black wine vinegar. And especially if it is in summer. And do not place a cauterizing substance over a wound, nor anything that contains oil: so that it may not cause rot and corruption within it.
Then administer splints at the hour of completion of your restoration of bone. And let the wound be opened: so that with forceps you make a hole in the folds according to the size of the wound.
And with all caution, lest you bind a wound with a fracture. Many times indeed foolish physicians have done this, and they have caused to happen upon their sick either death, or corruption, or zuchem, that is, gangrene. And let your constriction be soft, gentle, different from the constriction of other fractures.
If the wound is bad, or is a large wound, and fears something of the evils which we have narrated occur upon it, and you find pain in the suspended place: then it is not necessary that you put upon it stars. And make for them bandages of hard cloths in place of the stars, and bind him with these.
When however it is after one day, or two days, and you see the wound already beginning to produce pus in it; then remove from above that cloth which you placed over it with wine. Then administer a linen cloth and ointments with which it is our custom to heal wounds, such as ointment of four drugs, and those similar to it.
And it is fitting for you that you loosen the ligament. And consider the wound every day morning and evening until it consolidates and heals.
And it is necessary that the limb be prepared so that pus may run from it downwards with ease.
If many days pass by a wound and it does not heal, and the pus is not cut away from it: then know that there are small fragments of bone there. Therefore, it is necessary that you inquire about the wound with a probe. How many of those abscesses are separated, remove them, and extract them. And those that are not separated from them, and prick the limb, and cause pain to happen, try to cut them, and remove them every way that is possible for you.
If it happens that a wound is festering, or has a discharge of pus, that is, an abscess, or any other kind of corruption and putrefaction: then it is necessary to oppose each of these with what is suitable for its cure, whose preceding account is in its own chapter.
And in that which it is necessary that my speech should stand, and to whom you make your present mind when a bone breaks, and protrudes above the limb, like the hipbone, and of the thigh, and similar to them from the large limbs, it is that you do not presume the traction of it, for many times it happens from this death, even let it go, until it rots.
Perhaps it will fall of itself after twenty days, or thirty.
Therefore, if you deem it necessary, care for the wound; otherwise, let it be.
On the treatment of a knot in the track of a fracture.
Many times this Knot occurs in the course of healing a fracture. Especially that which is near the joints: and it defiles from this figure of the limb. And perhaps it prevents the limb from its natural operation.
Therefore consider if the knot is recent: administer to it medicines which have astringency, such as aloe, and frankincense, and myrrh, and sarcocolla, and acacia, and things similar to these.
Therefore, so that you may receive some of these, or all of them: and make them with a stich wine, or with the white of an egg, or with vinegar: and place them on the knot in a wad, and tighten them over it with a good stricture.
And let the stricture be released, and do not delay it for many days. Then loosen it and repeat another until the knot is removed.
Or tighten over the lead plate itself centered. For lead is a property for removing all that is feared from the limbs.
If, however, the knot has already been turned into stone and is tight, and urgency demands that it be removed; then cut above it from its upper part, and cut away the protruding superfluity; or shave it with certain razors until it is removed; and take care of it until it heals.
On the healing when it is restored, and the limb remains afterward subtle, not according to its nature.
When a bone fracture is repaired, and the limb remains after it subtle, weak: then it does not become unless for many causes. One of these is the multitude of the solution of the ligament and its ligature not as it ought; or because of the superfluity of the stricture of the ligaments, until nourishment is prohibited from running to the limb; or because of the multitude of superfluous embrocation in the wrong hour; or because of the scarcity of blood in the body of the infirm, and its weakness.
And the treatment of this is, to feed the sick person, and make his body soft, until blood multiplies in him. And administer a bath. And introduce joy and gladness upon him.
Then place pitch over the limb so that it may attract the nourishment of the pitch itself. And diligently apply its fomentation with warm water until it drains nourishment and returns to its natural shape.
On the treatment of fractured bones when they are set crookedly.
When it happens that after the healing of a limb there is crookedness, and prominence of the broken bone, or a knot; and it is defiled by this form of the limb, yet the limb is not hindered from its natural operation: then you should not take heed of the speech of him who thinks that the limb ought to be broken again.
And now there have been many foolish physicians and restorers who have done this in our land.
And this operation is indeed blameworthy and very dangerous, leading to death or great fear, so that it may be mortal.
But if the lameness and knotting are recent: then it is necessary that it be embrocated with tepid water, in which softening herbs have been boiled, such as the leaves of altaea, and its root, and melilot, and those similar to them. And let a plaster be made for him with a softening plaster, like dyachilon properly prepared.
Either a mucus of althea root is taken and beaten with chicken fat, or sesame oil: and it is plastered on with this.
Or take fat figs and pound them with dove dung, and similar things which are called from medicines diminishing the consolidation.
And sometimes a knot is loosened with constant, old-fashioned, and gentle friction, which is done with wet hands. And let movement of the limb be given to every part throughout the hours.
If the twisting has already preceded, and it has become strong, and it has been turned into stone, and necessity has been brought about for its treatment with iron: then it is necessary that the upper part of it be cut, and the continuity of the bone be loosened, and what is superfluous from the knot or bone be incised with delicate cutters; and ease should be administered in it with care and diligence.
On speech in dislocation.
Dislocation is an exit of a certain joint from its place. Therefore it is forbidden from movement, and its figure is defiled by the limb. And he causes pain and grievous wounds to occur upon the sick person.
When therefore dislocation happens to anyone, then it is necessary to hasten to its restoration: and do not delay it at all.
For if it is delayed, the place will become a boil, and perhaps with it a reduction of the dislocation.
Therefore, it is not necessary that it be moved or extended during the hour of its suppuration. For often it causes spasms and painful pains.
But when this happens, then it is necessary that you hasten to bloodletting of the sick man. Then it shall be left alone until the abscess rests.
Then the limb will be sutured with warm water and oil. Then it should be reduced with ease.
And every limb is cured with that which comes to its place.
I have indeed arranged the chapters on dislocation, also as before with respect to fracture, from the upper part of the body to the lower part of it.
On the treatment of a dislocated lower jaw.
Two jaws are rarely dislocated.
And dislocation of both occurs according to one of two modes. Or they are removed from their places with slight removal and softened for a short time. Or they are dislocated by dislocation, whole and complete, until they are softened to the part of the chest, until the saliva of the sick runs and he is not able to hold it, nor can he close his mouth, and his tongue is entangled with speech.
When the dislocation is small, it returns to many positions by itself with little effort.
If it is a complete, entire dislocation, then it is necessary that reduction be administered quickly, and it should not be delayed at all: and let the minister hold the head of the sick person, and let the physician insert his thumb into one side of the jawbone's root between its bones, if the dislocation is on one side. Or he may insert two thumbs at once, if the dislocation is from two parts. And the remaining fingers of his hand be outside, with which he may equal it.
And command the sick person to cleanse his jaws and finish them for eating on every part. And the physician sets the jaw and pushes the jaw until it returns to its place.
If the reduction is difficult, especially if both jaws are involved, then administer an evaporation with hot water and oil until easy reduction of both is achieved.  Do not delay the reduction of both at all, as we have said.
When therefore they return and become equal, and the bone of the infirm is closed, and are not softened: then put above them small cakes of cloth, with cerate which is made from wax: and rose oil.
Then it is bound with ease with soft ligaments.
And let the sleep of the sick person be upon his back: and his head should stand between two pillows, so that he does not move it to the right or left: nor try, or be compelled to chew anything: indeed put his food in a gentle broth, until when the pains are removed, and the jaw is knotted: then eat what he wants, and administer it with ease.
He shall not toil upon the operation of his mouth at eating and drinking, and speaking, until the jawbone is knit together, and has healed.
If the reduction of the two jaws is difficult when they are dislocated at some time and do not return to their places, then often fevers and persistent inflammation occur, and perhaps the patient's belly loosens, and perhaps he vomits pure bile.
When therefore you see this: then know that it is destruction, and many times he who has this dies within ten days.
On the reduction of the dislocation of the clavicle and the extremity of the humerus.
The collarbone indeed does not dislocate from the inside, because of its continuity with the chest. And it is sometimes dislocated to the outside: and that is made known to the sense.
And its restoration is, that he lie down sick on his back, and extend his arms. Then bind the place with your palm firmly, for it will return.
Then place over the same dressing, and pillows, and bind it.
The extremities which follow the elbow and continue with it, are not dislocated, except rarely. If it is dislocated on some day, then it is necessary that it be reduced and made equal according to what we have said, and which has been prepared for you.
Then place over the same plasters and pellets and constriction. And command the sick person to adhere to tranquility and quiet until he is healed.
And with this same treatment the extremity of the Humerus is reduced when it is also separated from its place.
On the reduction of a shoulder dislocation.
Know that the humerus is not dislocated unless in three ways.
One of these is that it be dislocated to the part of the thigh towards the lower region. The second is, that it be displaced to the side of the chest. And perhaps it is dislocated to the upper part of the humerus: and this happens rarely.
And it is not dislocated posteriorly, because of the spatula. And it is not dislocated anteriorly, because of the nerves.
And it is most often displaced and moves downwards towards the groin, especially in those whose flesh is little. Because it goes out quickly and enters quickly.
In those, however, whose flesh is much, there exists the opposite, namely that it comes out with difficulty and enters with difficulty.
And perhaps it happens to some people a blow, or a fall, and the humerus becomes inflamed with a hot abscess, and it is thought because of this, that it has already been dislocated.
Therefore, you ought to test it until you stand upon its certainty, and then proceed in its cure.
And it is known that dislocation is when there is a pulling downward of the shoulder; and distinguish between a dislocated shoulder and a sound shoulder. For you find among them a manifest diversity, and you find in the head of the Humerus concavity, and under the little one at touch the head of the humerus as if it were an egg: and the weak cannot reduce his hand to his ear, nor move it with all movements.
And similarly, if it is dislocated towards the chest, or upwards: then you find a manifest touch, and it is not hidden.
And the reduction of this dislocation is easy when it is recent, or when the boy is weak.
And its reduction is, that the minister raises his hand upwards. Then place your thumbs under its edge, and raise the joint with strength upward to its place: and the assistant will lift his hand and extend it upward. Then he places it lower, for he himself returns quickly.
If however it does not return by that which we have said, and there are many days from the time when the dislocation occurred: then it is necessary that the sick person bathe in hot water, and an embrocation be given which softens and soothes, as if roots of althea, fenugreek, and melilot were decocted in water, and be administered.
Then the patient is laid on his back: and under his chest a sphere and wool are placed, which is equal between softness and tightness.
Then the physician places his heel on the sphere and raises the shoulder with strength, and pulls the hand of the infirm to the lower part.
And if you wish, reduce him according to this method: and it is, that you make present a man who is longer than the sick one, and stand on the side, and introduce his shoulder under the arm of the sick one: and elevate his arm upwards until the sick one is suspended in the air. And another assistant shall draw the hand of the infirm to his lower belly. If, however, the weak person is light: then it is necessary that something else be hung with him, so that he may weigh down. For dislocation immediately returns.
And it is reduced also according to another way: and it is, as if one were to fix in the earth a long piece of wood, whose head be round in shape: like the sleeve of a mourner's robe: that it be not coarse, nor fine. Then soft cloths should be placed under the patient after they have been placed above the head of the wood: and the patient should stand according to the length of the wood.
Then the hand of him shall be extended to the lower part on the other side, and the body shall be extended from the other side with force: for the joint returns to its place with speed.
If its reduction is difficult through all that we have said, then use this treatment: and it is, to take a piece of wood whose length is the quantity of two arms, and its width is the quantity of four fingers, and its thickness is the quantity of two fingers: which has a rounded head, so that the entrance of it into the deep titillus may be eased, according to this form.
Then soft cloths are bound over the rounded head so that it does not harm the weak wood.
Then a plank should be placed under the patient's arm, so that the hand or arm can be extended over the wood up to the lower part: and the wood is fastened over the assistant and arm and extremity of the hand.
Then the arm is placed upon the step of the ladder across its width, and the hand is extended downwards, and the rest of the body is suspended from the other side. The junction then enters immediately.
When therefore the entrance is completed according to whichever way is possible: then it ought to be, that you place under the titillinum a sphere equal in quantity out of wool. Then place a prepared plaster made from ground flour with frankincense and egg white over the entire shoulder, as it is turned upwards.
Then bind with a strip of linen under the bandage; and roll the band above the plaster; and attach its hand to its neck, and let it go, and do not move its hand for seven days.
And it is necessary that little food be given to the sick person until the limb is restored; because it is more rapid to its healing.
Then a larger one is to be placed after seven days, or five, and it is taken out with motion. If therefore it is strengthened and not softened, then it is already healed.
If a joint is dislocated many times, because of the humidity that happens to it, or because of another cause: then it is necessary that cautery be administered to it with three holes, according to what was said before in the chapter on cautery.
If all of this is done, and the ligament is loosened after seven days, and the joint does not hold together, and the plaster is reapplied and tightened repeatedly over it, and it does not hold firm, and falls off, and it cannot be elevated to a higher position: then know that the nerves which are to the head of the humerus, have already been cut or stretched or softened: and know that the joint will never heal in its place.
As for a dislocation that occurs towards the chest and nipple, or to the rear, reduction is carried out with impulse and extension with hands; until it returns, and the rest of the constriction and care are administered until it heals.
If it happens after healing that there is heaviness in the limb and slowness in its movement, then let the sick person use a bath many times, until that heaviness is lessened and it returns to its first nature.
On the treatment of elbow dislocation.
Know that the joint of the elbow is dislocated with difficulty, and similarly it returns with difficulty; and indeed it is dislocated to all parts, and especially to the anterior or posterior.
And its dislocation is not hidden from you: because it falls under sight and touch, according to whatever form it is dislocated. And when you join a dislocated limb with a healthy one, it will be made manifest to you. For the joint is concave and cannot flex the arm, nor touch its own shoulder with it.
And it is necessary that you hasten, and reduce the dislocation immediately, before a hot abscess occurs to it.
For if a hot abscess happens to him, its reduction is difficult; and perhaps it does not heal at all, especially when it is towards the rear. For it is worse than all kinds of dislocations, and of more violent pain; and often death descends with it.
And the restoration of it when it is one of those things that can be reduced, is, that the hand of him be extended with two hands: and his arm be expanded, and the hands of the physician be above the elbow and below it, and he himself impels the joint with the thumbs of his hands together, or with the root of his palm, until it returns to its place.
Or if the dislocation is to the front, then it returns so that it doubles its hand with its own hand until it strikes with the root of its palm the elbow which is dislocated.
If the dislocation does not yield to reduction, then administer a violent and strong extension; and let two servants extend the arm, and let two others also hold it, so that it may not move during the extension.
Then the arm is rolled back to every part after it has been wrapped above the hand with a double length of cloth or a broad bandage.
And when the physician touches a joint with his hand, he should anoint it with oil so that it may be one of those things which help in the lubrication of the joint with ease. Then drive the joint with a strong thrust until it returns.
