And after setting it, it is necessary that a plaster be placed upon it which has astringency and drying power with the membrane of an egg; and let the bandage be tightened decently. And the arm shall be suspended from the neck of the sick person: and let it be released after several days.
Then it shall be loosened. If therefore the joint is fixed in its place, then loosen the ligament from it, and release it.
And if you see the joint not well joined, then apply a new plaster and ligament, and leave it for several days until it is tightened. Then dissolve it.
If, however, heaviness occurs to him after reduction of the joint, and the position is in motion: then administer humidification in bathing, and gentle friction, and compression, until it straightens, or put on above the hip joint, that is, a fat ram's fleece. Then it is bandaged, and it is left on it day and night. Then remove it, and introduce it into the bath. When therefore he sweats, then rub the joint with friction tempered. Then repeat upon the same wound with one and then a second and third bath until it softens.
And if you wish, place over it moistened dung of cow, heated with butter, and bind it on top of it.
Do that several times. For it is softened, and returns to its own arrangement.
On the treatment of dislocations, Al-Zahrawi.
The joints of the hand are often dislocated, that is, the joint between the arm and the hand.
And the reduction of its dislocation is easy. Against the other joints. Nevertheless it is necessary that speed be in the reduction of a dislocation at the hour in which it occurs, before the place becomes suppurating; or else there may happen to it a hot abscess.
And the reduction of a dislocation in this is, that you place Mahassems hands of the weak hand upon a table, and extend the minister's hand: and let the physician put his palm upon the prominence of the joint, and push it until it returns.
Nevertheless, it is necessary to consider, if the palm has already retreated to the belly of the hand: then let the sick person place the back of his hand on a board at extension and reduction; and if the dislocation is proceeding toward the back of the hand, then let the position of his hand be intrinsically upon the board, that is, the inner part of his hand upon the board, so that the hand of the physician may fall upon that eminence of the joint.
If it returns immediately, it is good: and if not, then bind it with a soothing poultice for the abscess: and dismiss him, and do not repeat this.
If he does not tolerate it, and you are unable to reduce it after days have passed, but the joint nevertheless remains according to its tortuosity, and it does not harm the patient except that his hand is softened, and cannot grasp anything: then know that the nerves have been severed or rubbed.
There is therefore no skill in it unless it is tightened with a cautery near its edge. And perhaps it doesn't matter something.
When therefore Al-Maghassi returns not, then place upon him the plaster which we have recounted. Then it is bound and left for five days; afterward it is loosened and left again, and the hand is exercised.
If its movement is hindered and something heavy falls upon it, then soften it with warm water and friction several times until it softens.
On the treatment of finger dislocations.
When a finger is dislocated from any part, or from the palm of the hand, or from its belly: then extend the finger, and drive back the dislocation with your thumbs until it returns.
Then support the head of the finger, and suspend it towards the part to which it is dislocated, and release it for two days.
Then suspend him and stretch him until he is equal to straight on that day of his.
When therefore night has come, bind him according to the same account: and do not cease to loosen him in the day: and exercise him with movement: and bind him at night.
Do this for several days until it tightens.
And similarly do this, if it is dislocated to the belly, hand; and bind him to that part itself, and do with him your first operation, until it heals.
And similarly do this when it is dislocated to the other parts.
On the treatment of a dislocation of the lumbar vertebrae.
When it happens to someone that there is dislocation of the spine of the back or neck, complete, or many vertebrae are removed from their places: then there is no cure for it, because death hastens the weak.
And the sign of this is that the stool of the sick person comes out without his will, and he cannot hold it; and many times some of his limbs are softened, or two legs of him, or two arms of him, or one of them; or a single hip bone is removed from its place.
For it is often removed, which is often removed to four parts.
That which is removed toward the rear, is called Gibbosity.
The cure of this is, as you consider then, if gibbosity happens from childhood: then there is no cure in it, nor is there any healing at all from it.
In those things which happen by chance, or blow, or similar events, ancients have already spoken of modes of healing in lengthy speech. Of which most does not return with profit.
I myself have now abbreviated from that one, which little excuses itself from much of its proximity to my intention: and in the exposition which I have exposed it, and formed its instrument in a different way from how they showed it and laid it out.
Therefore I say that in a hunchback which occurs from the front part, there is no remedy or cure.
And similarly in that which is done to both sides also.
And it is not cured from these things, except that which happens on the back properly, as I say: and it is, that the sick person be extended on his face on a level platform near the wall: and that a wet covering be spread under him, so that his chest is not harmed.
Then a straight fixed piece of wood is to be placed in the pit in the ground, at the end of the foot, about its head: and another piece of wood about its feet at the other end of the foot: and let the servant hold the wood, and let it not be fastened in the pit. And another assistant shall hold another.
Then wrap above the head of the sick person, and under its hairline a soft, firm bandage: and extend the extremity of the bandage to the wood which is at the head; and bind it there.
Then bind another ligament above its groins, and above its knees, and at its hip joints. Then gather all the ligaments and bind them in a piece of wood that is at his feet.
Then let every assistant extend the limb to the part of the wood, and let not two ligatures be removed from their places where they are fixed; however, they shall not be fastened, as we have said.
And the physician places his palms upon the vertebra with strength until it returns; or he places a board upon it. Then he binds his feet upon the table until he returns.
If it does not return through this treatment, then take a board whose length is about three cubits. And the cavity in the wall of which we said was near the infirm, place where the end of the board may enter. Then place the middle board over the Spina. And let the physician place his feet upon the other extremity. And he should bind it well until the joint be contracted and return to its place.
Then if you wish, operate with a lathe which is wrapped with your hand: and it is, as you fix in the earth at the head of the sick person at the farthest foot two beams, whose length shall be the length of one arm: and at his head at the farthest foot two beams.
And let the length which is between every piece of wood be one palm; and now a hole has been made in every place, through which a rope runs; and let the whole wood be firmly fixed in the earth very much, so that it may not move at all.
And a round piece of wood is inserted; and it is a whorl in which the ligament revolves in two holes of two pieces of wood.
And at its end is a hole, in which wood is fastened, whose length is a palm with that which turns: and in two other pieces of wood there is similarly that of it.
Then bind the ligaments which you have bound in the chest of the sick man to the band that is at his head: and the ligaments which you have bound in his legs to the band that is at his feet.
Then let there be at every lathe a minister, who twists his hand with linen, with which the lathe is turned; and the physician straightens the spine, according to what we have said.
This is the form of the tourniquet that follows.
Then after the sponge has been returned and the place is leveled, it is necessary to place a dry plaster with egg white. Then a plug. Then place above the plaster a star made of wood, whose width is three fingers, or thereabouts. And its length is the quantity which it takes up of gibbosity, and above the part of the healthy vertebra.
Then a bandage is applied with the ligament which we have said. And let the sick person use fine nourishment until he is healed.
If some elevation remains in the place at the end of healing: then it is necessary that softening and soothing medicines be administered, along with the tablet which we narrated for a long time. And now a plate of lead is administered in that.
And sometimes there occurs a prominence on the last vertebra of the back: and it is thought to be dislocation through it, and this bone which now protrudes. Therefore it ought not be presumed that this medicine should be given to him, for perchance it may happen to cause death.
On the treatment of a dislocated hip joint.
Know that the joint of the ankle and the joint of the shoulder never occurs except for a dislocation only; nor does it occur to them as happens to other joints by slight removal and concavity.
And indeed the joint of the Ancha is dislocated in four parts. Which is, because it is displaced forward and backward.
It is indeed most frequently dislocated towards the interior, and little towards the front and back.
And the treatment of dislocation inward is: because when you join the sound leg to the unsound, it is longer; and the knee of it is more prominent than that which is sound, and the unsound cannot double its foot at the groin; and there is a place following the groin, an abscess with hot pus, because the head of the hip has already come there.
And the sign of him who has a dislocation toward the outside is that those things which are contrary to these happen to him.
And the sign of one to whom a dislocation has occurred anteriorly is that he expands the sound leg according to its complement; however, he does not double it without pain which is in the knee: and if he tries to walk, he cannot go forward over it: and his urine is retained, and his groin suppurates, and in walking his foot falls upon his heel.
And the sign of one who has a dislocation to the rear is that he cannot extend his knee, nor can he bend it before he bends his groin: and his leg is also shorter than the other: and his groin is softened, and the head of the hip is at the place of the ilium.
The reduction of this dislocation, however, as you consider it, is, if the dislocation is old, which has already aged with the one having it. Then if you administer a reduction of it, and it remains according to its disposition: then there is no treatment at all in it. Therefore it is not necessary that presumption be made of him entirely.
In that case, in which the dislocation is recent, and it belongs to one of the four kinds of dislocation: then hasten to this, so that you will join the joint, and extend it both inwards and outwards, and move it with your right and left hand: for perhaps it will return, and does not need other cure.
If this method does not work, then it is necessary that you prepare a strong assistant, and he should extend the leg from its lower part: either with his hands, or with a bandage, which is tied above the knee: and another assistant should extend his hand from above, so that he may insert his hands under the pubic bone. Then he binds with a soft ligament over the root of the hip, and holds the extremity of the other ligament.
And let its extension be either from the front part of the groin to the shoulder blade, or from the back part to the back.
And let the extension of all of them be one vice, until the sick person is lifted with his body from the ground and remains hanging.
And this kind of extension is a common kind of the four kinds.
If therefore the dislocation returns with what we said, it is good: and if not, then it is necessary to do all kinds of that which I shall say about proper cure.
When indeed the dislocation is inward, it is necessary that the sick person be laid upon his sound side in reducing it. Then he places a ligature over the root of the hip, where it is between the head of the hip and the place which is under the groin. Then extend the ligament upward from the groin region to the higher part of the body, to the clavicle area.
Then let another minister take strong arms and reduce the large place of the weak hip, extending it to the outside with vigorous extension. For now it returns to its place.
And this kind is easier of cures than the other kinds, in that this member is reduced.
If however he excuses himself from you and does not obey you for the entrance with this kind of cure at all: then it is necessary that two feet be bound together along with a strong, soft ligature above two heels and two knees: and let the length of each be four finger widths from its fellow: and let the weak leg be extended more than the other by the quantity of two fingers.
Then the sick person should be suspended over the head of a log, which is in the house: and let its length from the earth be the quantity of two arms.
Then command a strong young man to support the head of the hip: and let another support him who is weak when found: and let another young man push, supporting the hip with strength, for the joint returns to its place quickly.
In setting it properly, when a dislocation is external, it is necessary that the sick person lie on a stool, as we have narrated in one with hunchback. And bind the ligament above his leg, which is weak properly, and above his chest.
Then two cords are placed, one at his feet, and the other at his head. Then a piece of wood is to be placed in the middle of the footbed, firmly fixed: upon which cloths should be wrapped that it may not harm the patient; and let the wood be between his hips so that it may not be pulled down when extended.
Then let every assistant extend to his part, and the physician equalize the reduction with his hand.
If therefore he yields to reversion, it will be good.
And if not, place upon the same table, and press it as we said about the prominence, equally. Nevertheless, it is necessary that the sick person lie on his sound side.
And when the dislocation is to the front, then it is necessary that the leg of the infirm be stretched quickly: and it is according to this same story on a platform: and the physician puts his right hand palm over the groin of the infirm. Then he constricts it with the other hand, and with that constriction puts it extended downwards towards the lower part of the knee.
And if the dislocation is to the rear: then it is not necessary for the weak one to be extended downwards and raised above the ground: rather, it is necessary for him to be placed upon a hard object, as is fitting, as it also happens if the dislocation of his arm occurs outwards, according to what we said about his lying on two planks: and it is over his face, and the ligaments are extended, according to what we recently said.
And it is necessary that a compress be applied with a board also over the place to which the joint goes out, when reduction is completed as it ought.
And the sign of its return is not hidden from you: and it is, that you extend the two legs of the sick person: and when you see them equal, and the sick person tightens his leg, and expands it without excuse: then know, that now the limb returns, as it ought: then join the two hips, and put a plaster, and bind with an imemati, that is, a wheel: so that it may not move anywhere at all: and the sick person adheres to quiet for three days, or four.
Then loosen the ligament and the plaster, and compare the leg with the other; then if you see them equal in condition, then know that the dislocation is now firm: therefore discharge the sick person to walk.
And if you see any softening: then return, and apply a dressing to it, and bind it according to your first constriction: and leave it for three days.
Then dissolve it and make it slow down in walking on both sides for several days, until it is well fortified.
On the dislocation of the knee.
The knee is dislocated in three ways. It is dislocated outwards, inwards, and downwards, namely to the rear.
And it is not dislocated anteriorly.
And the sign of its dislocation is, that you bid the sick man apply his leg to his hip. If it does not adhere with the hip: then you shall know that the knee is dislocated.
And the restoration of all types of dislocation is to make the weak leg straight, so that it may now extend its leg if it can over it, and adhere your back to its face: and place his foot between your feet.
Then cause his knee to adhere with your palms, and straight make them between your fingers upon his knee. Then apply with your palm both sides of his knee with strength. And another attendant shall extend his foot until the knee returns to its place.
And the sign of recovery is that the leg adheres to the hip with ease, not with effort.
Then place a plaster over it and make the leg adhere to the hip. Then tie that with a thread for three or four days. Then loosen it.
And let not the sick person walk for several days until he is healed.
If reduction is refused to you, then administer a strong extension with ligaments, the previous account of which in the treatment of the ankylosis, until it returns.
On the treatment of calcaneus dislocation.
Sometimes the calcaneus is removed by a small incision, and sometimes it is dislocated according to the completeness. And its dislocation is made either inwards or outwards.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you see the heel bone swollen, proceeding towards the part to which it is dislocated.
The treatment of its removal is easy restoration: and it is, that it be extended with facility with hands, and be made equal, until it return.
In the care of it when it is dislocated according to the completion, it is necessary that you make the patient sit straight; and let him hold him strong by the posterior part of his back, in the middle of it.
Then hold his foot with your right hand from the upper part of it, and with your left hand from the lower part of his foot in the place of the heel.
Then draw the foot toward you with your right hand. Then with two left stitches.
Then extend with the left hand and drive the breast of the foot with the right hand towards the leg without effort.
Do that twice, as we have narrated.
Then press the chest of the foot toward the leg in a third position, and you pull the thread.
If therefore it returns in one or in two according to this mode, and you see the foot equal, it is good: and if not, then repeat the operation upon it; for it itself will return.
If you are prevented from reduction by that which we have narrated: then make the sick person lie on his back on the ground, and fix a small piece of wood firmly on the ground, so that it falls between his hips: and now you have enveloped him with cloth, so that he is not harmed.
Then let the assistant take hold of his hip: afterwards let another assistant extend the foot, either with his hands or with a bandage that is tied above the ankle. Then let every attendant extend against the extension of his own comparison: and a pad be standing between the two hips of the infirm holding him, so that the body of him is not drawn down in the extension.
Then the physician reduces the dislocation with his hands, and another assistant holds the sound leg downwards. For a dislocation returns with speed.
When therefore a dislocation returns, and the health of its restoration is declared to you: then place a plaster and a wad, and tightly bind it with ligaments, and tie the foot with a ligament to the leg.
And you ought to be careful or fearful of the nerves which are above the heel bone, so that the ligament is not too strong over them and harms them.
Then let him be dismissed after two days, or three. If the ligament is softened, tighten it; then loosen it on the third or fourth day; and forbid the infirm from walking for forty days. If he attempts to walk before that time, it is not safe unless the dislocation breaks apart above him, and it is corrupted, and does not receive healing afterwards.
If it happens to him that he has a hot abscess, then you must administer to him the same thing which we mentioned before in another place concerning its cure, and an ointment, until it is removed.
On dislocation of the toes and their treatment.
It is necessary that what is dislocated be put back in place with little extension without labor; and indeed this is not difficult, but easy.
If the dislocation is in some joints of the back of the foot: then it is necessary that the sick person make his foot sit upon a level place of the earth, or upon a board: and he stands as if walking.
Then you stand, and place your foot upon that which projects from those joints. Then press that with your foot with strength until it returns and you see it leveled, and there is no prominence in its place.
Then place under his foot a board which should hold the whole foot, having two heads. Then bind it tightly with a bandage for three days. Then loosen it and make him rest from walking for many days until it tightens and is secured by the reduction.
On the kinds of dislocations with a wound or fracture.
When something happens from this, and you try its healing and restoration, many times death follows: and for that reason it is not fitting that he should presume to cure it, of this kind, unless he is prudent in the art, long used, merciful, pious, slow, not bold, nor presumptuous: and that he should use in the beginning medicines which calm hot abscesses only: and dismiss the sick by divine judgment. Unless you hope for salvation from death with gentleness of sickness, and in which appears something of hope. Therefore, attempt the reduction of it immediately at the beginning of the matter, before a hot abscess occurs.
If therefore the limb returns, according to what we want, then administer a treatment that calms the abscess; and care for the wound with that which is fitting for it from drying ointments.
If the dislocation is with a fracture and these occur in separated bone fragments, then attempt the removal of them, and do what we said in simple ailments from what precedes regarding their places.
And beware according to your strength, and guard yourself from entering the way of fear: according to what my prediction has preceded for you.
For that is better for you with your Creator, and more lasting for your glory, and more healthful for your body among those in your time.
